Summary 0:
UCITS — rules on marketing and investment in transferable securities (UCITS) (until 31 December 2019) (see summary)
UCITS – rules on managing and marketing UCITS (until 1 January 2021)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/856 — rules for marketing and marketing in the field of UCITS
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the transparency of the EU’s financial services sector by setting out common rules for the marketing and communication of UCITs (see below).
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all UCITS* and their managers.
It does not apply to:
social entrepreneurship funds (SMEs);
investment funds (i.e. small and medium-sized enterprises);
mutual funds (MSFs); and
investor-owned enterprises (IOPs).
It also applies to funds that are not covered by the directive, such as pension funds, pension funds and mutual funds.
The rules apply to the following types of investment vehicles:
investors’ funds;
management companies;
investments in UCITS managed by AIFMs, EuVECA managers or EuSEF managers;
managed by a management company.
Management companies must:
ensure that:
all UCITS are managed by the same person or company;
have the same legal personality;
provide the same level of protection to the investors;
use the same investment product or service to all investors, irrespective of their nationality or place of domicile;
apply for the same type of investment;
are subject to the same conditions as other investment vehicles;
follow the same internal and external investment vehicles.
They must also:
notify the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the competent national authorities in the EU and the European Investment Bank for the Investment Industry (EIB) of the information they provide to the public on the UCITS they manage;
not disclose information on their activities to the general public;
report to the EIB and to the European Commission on the activities they undertake to market UCITS;
inform the Commission and the national authorities of the content or of the addressees of pre-marketing, and of any conditions or requirements other than those set out in this directive, before they engage in marketing activities.
In addition, the directive sets out common principles concerning fees or charges that UCITS managers must charge to investors.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 2 February 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 February 2021.
BACKGROUND
The UCITS directive is one of a series of measures designed to modernise the financial sector. The other measures are:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/849 on the European Pillar of Social Rights and the establishment of a European Union Investment Fund for Social Entrepreneurship (UCIF);
Directives 2014/65/EU on markets in financial instruments and amending Directive 2002/92/EC and Directive 2011/61/EU;
Delegated Regulation (EU), Euratom Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 on the supervision of financial institutions and the prudential supervision of credit institutions and investment firms;
Regulations (EU and Euratom).
For more information, see:
‘UCITS and social entrepreneurship funds’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* UCITS: an investment vehicle in the form of a transferable investment fund.
* Social entrepreneurship fund: a fund that invests in the social entrepreneurship of individuals and companies in the European Union (EU).
* Investment vehicle: a financial instrument that is open to all individuals, companies and entities, whether they are individuals or companies.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU)* 2019/884 of 3 February 2019 laying down rules on the marketing of and marketing of certain types of undertakings and UCITS and repealing Directive 2009/64/EC (OJ L 183, 4.4.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Directive (EC, Euram) 2019 /884 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 1:
Ensuring the enforcement of EU harmonisation legislation on products
 Ensuring the enforceability and effectiveness of EU legislation
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/515 — enforcement of certain Union harmonisation measures
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ national market surveillance authorities (MSA) and the European Commission work together to ensure the effective enforcement of the EU’s harmonised rules on the sale of certain products.
It sets out the rules and procedures to be followed by MSA and the Commission when enforcing these rules.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
all products on sale in the EU, whether or not they are manufactured or manufactured in another EU country;
all goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries, whether they are intended to be sold on the EU market or not;
new products entering the EU;
non-EU-registered products which are subject to EU legislation.
The legislation applies to all products, including:
goods imported from outside the EU and goods imported from the EU.
Controls on products:
EU countries must ensure that:
manufacturers, importers, distributors and other economic operators comply with the legislation;
the information and communication system (ICSMS) is up-to-date and accessible to the Commission, the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (ECBAR) and national customs offices;
market surveillance authorities are informed of any changes to the legislation and take the necessary measures to ensure compliance with it;
importers and importers comply with it.
Importers must inform the Commission of any new products they wish to place on the market and inform the competent national authorities of the reasons for their decision.
National authorities must:
ensure that the information provided in the regulation is entered into the ICSMSAs soon as a product is found to be a non-compliant product, the Commission must take all necessary measures.
Where a national authority concludes that a product does not comply with EU legislation, it informs the Commission and the other EU countries of its decision and informs them of the reason.
If the Commission considers that a national measure is justified in accordance with the applicable safeguard procedure, or where no market surveillance authority has concluded the contrary as referred to in Article 11(9), the Commission may adopt an action to prevent the product from being put on sale.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been working towards harmonising its laws on products for more than a decade.
For more information, see:
Regulation (EU).
KEY TERMS
* Importers: any person, business, organisation or other person, company or organisation with a legitimate interest in selling a product.
* Distributor: any business or organisation that sells a product for profit.
ACT
Commission Implementing Regulation(EU) 2017/1369 of 4 July 2017 setting a framework for energy labelling and repealing Directive 2010/30/EU (OJ L 198, 28.7.2017, pp. 1-8)

Summary 2:
Retransmission of television and radio programmes — licensing and remuneration of rights and retransmission services
Retransmissions of television or radio programmes – licensing and payment of rights
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/36/EU — licensing of rights for retransmissions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure fair competition in the licensing of broadcasting and radio rights in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the licensing and licensing of the rights of broadcasters to retransmit their programmes to the public.
It does not apply to:
the rights of rights holders (e.g. authors, producers, etc.) who are represented by collective management organisations (i.e. collective management bodies that represent the interests of all the rights holders in a collective management organisation);
the right of the broadcaster to make a payment for the right to transmit a programme to a retransmitter;
rights holders who are not represented by that collective management body.
This directive does not affect the licensing or licensing of copyright and other intellectual property rights.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision (EU) 2016/849/EU has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU has adopted a number of measures to ensure that the EU-wide licensing of broadcast rights is fair and transparent.
For more information, see:
‘Licensing of broadcasting rights’ on the European Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Collective management organisation: an organisation that manages the collective management of collective management agreements between broadcasting organisations and operators of retransmitting services.
* Retransmission service: a service that allows viewers to watch, listen or listen to a programme on the internet.

Summary 3:
The.eu top-level domain name (TLD)
The.eu Top-Level Domain Name
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 — the.eu Top Level Domain Name and the establishment of a registry
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to establish a registry for domain names under the top level domain (‘.eu’) name.
It sets out the rules and procedures for the establishment and management of the registry.
KEY POINTS
The registry is managed by the European Commission and is responsible for:
designating the domain name ‘.eu*’;
registering domain names;
receiving and revoking domain names.
The regulation sets out a number of conditions for the registry, including:
the conditions under which domain names are registered;
the criteria for the selection of the registrar;
rules for the revocation of domain names and for the registration and revocation of.eu names; and
the terms and conditions relating to the registry’s management.
Registry
The Commission designates the registry under the regulation and appoints the registry registrar. The Commission is assisted by an Advisory Group composed of representatives of stakeholders established outside the EU. The Advisory Group may include one representative of EU countries and one representative from non-EU countries.
A registry is established by the Commission and the Registry must comply with the principles of non-discrimination, transparency and fair competition. It must:
be independent and transparent;
cooperate with the EU countries’ national registrar systems;
be free of charge;
operate in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 733/2002 (see summary);
be open to the public;
not discriminate against any person or organisation established in the EU without prejudice to the application of national law;
provide services to undertakings with whom it competes on downstream markets.
Registration and revocation
The Registry is managed and administered by the Registry. It is open to all persons, organisations and natural persons within the EU who wish to register a domain name under the TLD. It may also be used by organisations established in other EU countries, but not established in an EU country.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The domain name is reserved for the European Union (EU).
It is valid until 12 October 2022.

Summary 4:
Cohesion Fund 2021/2021 — funding for transport, energy and digital infrastructure projects
Cohesion fund 2021/2020 — financing for transport and digital infrastructures projects
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1921 establishing the European Cohesion Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
It sets up the European cohesion fund (CEF), which aims to provide financial support for the development of innovative infrastructure projects in the transport and energy sectors.
KEY POINTS
The CEF is the main instrument of the EU’s common investment policy (CEP) and is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The regulation sets out the eligibility criteria for projects to be eligible for funding under the CEF.
Eligible actions
The following actions are eligible for CEF funding:
transport infrastructure projects: projects that contribute to the transition to low emission energy and mobility* and that improve security of supply, while seeking greater energy independence for the EU;
infrastructure projects that improve the mobility of people, goods, capital and services;
energy infrastructure projects that support the growth and competitiveness of the transport sector;
digital infrastructure projects.
Projects are eligible if they meet the following criteria:
they contribute to:
improving the efficiency of transport infrastructure;
increasing energy efficiency;
reducing energy consumption;
fostering the use of renewable energy;
creating jobs;
contributing to the sustainable development of the energy and transport sectors;
inclusive of all EU countries;
providing a high-quality, safe and secure transport infrastructure.
The European Commission is responsible for the selection and evaluation of eligible projects and the selection of the projects to receive funding. The CEF supports the following types of projects:
public-private partnerships;
investment projects;
research and innovation projects; and
projects in the digital sector.
In addition, projects may be eligible under the following the following conditions:
the project must be carried out in an EU country;
the total cost of the project must not exceed €1.5 billion;
a maximum co-financing rate of 30% of the total cost.
EU countries are responsible for monitoring, monitoring, evaluating, evaluating and controlling the results of these projects, and for ensuring that they are recognised and evaluated.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The EIB and the ERDF are the main instruments of the European investment policy.
For more information, see:
‘The European Investment Fund’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			  	 	
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratom and the Council of 11 December 2021 establishing the Community Cohesion fund and repealing Regulation (EC) No 182/2011 (OJ L 347, 12.12.2021, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 231/2011 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 5:
Removal of terrorist material from the EU’s digital single market
Removals from the European single market: removing terrorist material
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the removal of terrorist content from the internal market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
   
It aims to ensure that hosting service providers and users’ trust in the online environment are better protected against terrorist material.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out a number of measures to prevent and remove terrorist material on the European Union (EU) single market. These include:
a ban on hosting sites hosting terrorist material;
a requirement for service providers to remove or disable access to terrorist content, following an assessment by the competent authorities;
an obligation on hosting services providers to:
ensure that service providers are informed of the reasons for their decision to remove terrorist content;
provide information on the nature and content of the removal order, including the reason for the decision;
allow users to request clarification and feedback in relation to removal orders issued by competent authorities.
It also sets out procedures for cross-border removal orders.
The European Commission has adopted delegated acts to supplement this regulation with technical requirements for the electronic means of transmission of removal orders and for the establishment of contact points.
In addition, the regulation sets up a mechanism for the exchange of information between EU countries and the European Judicial Network (EJN).
The EJN is responsible for the protection of the personal data of individuals who have been ordered to leave the EU and who have not complied with removal orders, as well as for information about their legal representatives.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2005 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
 The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the functioning of the EU single market in the context of the fight against terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Fighting terrorism online’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Terrorist material: any material which is considered to be a threat to public safety, public health or the safety of the general public.
* Hosting service provider: a service provider that allows users to access a web page, such as a website or an application, which is hosted or hosted by an internet service provider.
REFERENCES
Act

Summary 6:
Scientific, technological, economic, environmental and societal impact: Horizon Europe 2021-2027 (FSPRE)
Scientific and technological, societal and economic impact: Research and Innovation (FPSRE) 2021–2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/856 — Horizon Europe — the Framework Programme for Research and innovation 2021-2020
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the framework for the EU’s investment in research and innovation (R&I).
KEY POINTS
The FSPRE is the main instrument of the Horizon Europe programme, which aims to support the European Union (EU), its Member States and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) in the field of R&I.
It is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the Cohesion Fund.
The EIB and the CSF are the main sources of funding for Horizon Europe, while the EIF and the EFS provide the EU with additional financial support.
Horizon Europe is one of the main investment instruments of the EU, along with the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) and Horizon Europe for the period 2014–2020.
Funding
The programme is funded through the following sources:
the EIB, the EAFRD, the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), Horizon Europe and Horizon 2020 (see summary);
the Cohesion Funds, the EU Social Fund, Horizon 2020, Horizon 2021 and Horizon 2027;
the European Investment Fund (EIF), Horizon 2020 and Horizon 2021.
In addition, Horizon Europe is eligible for grants from the European Research Council (ERDC) and from the World Trade Organisation (WTO).
The following types of grants are also eligible:
grants for research and development (R & D) projects, including grants for the development and demonstration of innovative products and services, and grants for R&D projects for the implementation of R & D projects, such as the development of new technologies and the demonstration of the use of existing technologies, and for the dissemination of scientific and technological knowledge and best practices.
Procurement
The Programme is open to the participation of any person or legal entity, regardless of its place of establishment and including legal entities from non-associated third countries or international organisations, provided that the conditions laid down in this regulation have been met together with any conditions set down in the work programme or call for proposals.
Participation
The Commission may adopt conditions for participation of beneficiaries of other programmes. These conditions are set out in the programme's work programme and in the calls for proposals, as well as in the Commission's annual work programme.
Cooperation
The EU is committed to promoting cooperation between its research institutes, universities, research centres and other research organisations in order to achieve the objectives of Horizon Europe. In this respect, the Commission is assisted by the Joint Research Centre (JRC), which is responsible for coordinating the activities of the EIC and the InvestEU programmes.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
  (European Commission)
The Horizon Europe Framework Programme is the largest investment programme of its kind in the EU. It supports research, innovation and technological development in Europe.
For more information, see:
‘Hornizon Europe’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Innovation: any research, development or technological activity that has a direct or indirect impact on the environment, human health, the economy or the social life of an individual or a group of individuals, or on the social, economic or environmental impact of an organisation or group of persons, as defined in Regulation (EC) No 596/2014.
ACT
  барска нашка (OJ L 183, 7.7.2019, pp. 1–5)

Summary 7:
Programme for the European Union’s participation in the International Space Station (EU-2020)
Programming for the EU's participation in international space activities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 541/2014/EU on the EU-2020 programme for the participation of the Union in the international space programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and procedures for the implementation of the EU–2020 programme.
KEY POINTS
The programme is designed to:
support the development of the European Space Agency (ESA) and the European Geostationary Operational Research and Development Agency (EGNOS) in their activities in the field of space;
promote cooperation between ESA and the EU countries in the area of space research and development;
contribute to the development and operation of the Galileo and EGNOS space stations;
facilitate the transfer of knowledge and expertise between the ESA and EU countries;
provide the European Commission with the necessary information and expertise in the areas of space and space policy;
support and coordinate the activities of the Expert Teams set up under the programme;
ensure that the programme is implemented in line with the objectives of the General Programme for European Space Policy (GSP), which is the main instrument of the GSP.
The regulation lays down the rules for the organisation of the programme, including:
the structure and structure of the Programme;
the role of the Executive Director, who is appointed by the Commission and is responsible for the overall programme management;
implementing the programme in accordance with the EU country-by-country priorities;
a Security Accreditation Board, which ensures the programme complies with the requirements of the Security Council and the General Agreement on the Protection of Life and Health of the Earth (GALI);
the responsibilities of the Commission, the Agency and ESA, which are set out in the ESA-EU Framework Agreement, which is based on the 2004 Framework Agreement.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
The GSP is one of the three pillars of the ESA–EU programme, along with the European Research and Innovation Fund (ERIFIFIF) and Horizon 2020.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union in space’ on the ESA's website.

Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Hubs (EDI 2020/2027 – 2021-2027)
European digital innovation hubs (EDII 2020/2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/2022 establishing the European Digital Innovation Programme (EDIN 2020/21/2021)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a programme to support the development of European digital innovation in the context of the European Commission’s resolution on digitising the European Union (EU).
It sets out the specific objectives of the programme and the delivery mechanisms to be used to achieve them.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The programme is designed to:
facilitate the sharing of knowledge and experience between EU countries;
promote the use of exascale and post-exascale computers in total and by various stakeholder groups (universities, SMEs, industry, etc.) in cooperation with relevant industries;
encourage research and development in the areas of artificial intelligence (AI) and high performance computing (HPC);
support the development and use of advanced skills in all domains and at all levels through mobility experiences.
The objective of the EDI programme is to support actions in the following fields:
research and development;
creativity;
innovation;
employment;
research;
education;
advanced skills.
Eligible actions
The EDI actions will be funded by the European Social Fund Plus (ESF+), the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and Erasmus+ and the European Education and Training Fund (ETFinance.
Specific objectives
The following are eligible for funding:
1.
Research and development:
the EDIAs part of the Horizon Europe programme, the ESI programme supports the creation of a European Innovation Hub (EI Hub) in each EU Member State. The EI Hub will be financed by the EU budget and will be based in the territory of the Member State in which the EI hub is located.
2.
Academic and training:
EIHubs will be supported by the ERDF or ESF+ and will complement the activities of Horizon Europe.
3.
In addition, EIH will support the following activities:
ex-ascale computing:
supporting research and innovation in HPC;
support for the development, use and dissemination of new technologies in the area of AI;
the development of new knowledge and skills;
exchange of best practices and best practices in the field of HPC.
4.
Educational and mobility experiences:
participation of young people in the programme will be facilitated by the participation of universities, research institutes and training institutes.
5.
Participation of non-EU countries and territories (non-EU Member States):
the participation of the countries of the Western Balkans, the Faroe Islands, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and Turkey will also be encouraged.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  (European Commission)
The European Commission has set up a multiannual financial framework for the period from 2021 to 2027.
For more information, see:
‘The European Innovation Programme 2021/202027’ on the EIN2020 website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			 	  	
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2019/EU of 11 December 2019 establishing the programme for a period of seven years from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027 and repealing Regulation (EC) No 966/2012 (OJ L 193, 30.12.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2019-2022 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 9:
Funding for innovation and entrepreneurship in Europe (2020-2027) programme (FARE-2020)
Fare-2020 programme (2027-2028)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1291/2013 establishing Horizon 2020 – the Framework Programme for Research and Innovation (2014-2020), and repealing Decision No 1982/2006/EC (OJ L 347, 20.12.2013)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s Horizon 2020 programme for research and innovation (2014–2027).
It sets out the objectives of the programme for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
KEY POINTS
The programme aims to:
promote the growth and competitiveness of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in the European Union (EU);
facilitate research and development (R&D) activities;
support the development of innovative products and services;
improve SMEs’ access to the labour market;
encourage the sharing of knowledge and expertise between EU countries;
contribute to the creation of a knowledge-based economy.
The objectives are set out in Annex I to the regulation.
Objectives
The objective of the Horizon 2020 Programme is to support actions in the following areas:
encouraging innovation and entrepreneurialism in the EU;
fostering the creation and diversification of the EU economy;
promoting the participation of SMEs in the activities of the private sector;
facilitating the exchange of information and communication technologies (ICTs);
supporting the development and dissemination of knowledge, skills, skills and competences;
creating an environment conducive to innovation;
improving the knowledge base of EU citizens and businesses.
Specific objectives
The following actions are eligible for funding:
the following actions implementing the specific objective referred to in Article 3(2)(a) of the regulation:
research and development activities, including:
exchange of best practices and best practices;
the development of new products and technologies;
exchanges of experience and best practice in the field of R&D;
research, development and technological development;
development of products, technologies and services in the areas of health, safety, environment, social protection and the environment;
investment in research, development, innovation and SMEs;
employment and training;
innovation in the area of the environment, energy, transport and mobility;
environmental, social and consumer protection;
agriculture, forestry, fisheries and aquaculture;
health and safety;
food and feed;
science and technology;
biotechnology;
information society;
education and training.
In addition, the programme is designed to support the following activities:
facilities and facilities for the training and development of researchers, engineers, architects, technicians and other professionals;
participation of EU countries in the programme;
organisation of research projects;
training and mentoring of researchers;
visits and exchanges of scientists and technical experts;
reciprocal access to research facilities and facilities;
rehabilitation of researchers and engineers;
sharing of facilities and expertise;
cooperation between research institutes, universities, research centres and other research institutions;
initiatives to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the European Research and Development Fund (ERDF);
the sharing of information, research and training activities between universities and training institutes; and
the exchange of experience between research institutions.
Implementation and forms of EU funding
The European Commission is responsible for administering the programme. It is assisted by a steering committee composed of representatives of the Member States, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions. The Commission is assisted in this by the European Parliament and the Council and by the Committee on the Functioning of the Union, the Committee for the Internal Market and the European External Action Service.
It is funded by the general budget of the Community, the EU budget and the EU countries’ contributions to the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014. It has applied for a period of 5 years from the date of entry into force.
BACKGROUND
The Horizon 2020 Framework Programme is one of the three pillars of the Europe 2020 programme. The other two pillars are the European Pillar of Social Rights (Euratom) and the Erasmus+ programme.
For more information, see:
Horizon 2020 (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Innovation: the ability of an individual or a group of individuals or companies to create, develop and implement innovative products or services.
* Research and development: the process of discovering, developing and disseminating knowledge, technology or services, and the results of that innovation.

Summary 10:
Traceability of firearms and essential components: European Firearms Pass (until 30 years after their destruction) (until 31 December 2023)
Tracingability of Firearms and Essential Components: EU Firearms Pass
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/43/EU — tracing of firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to make it easier for law enforcement authorities in EU countries to trace the ownership, ownership and transfer of firearms, essential components and ammunition.
It codifies and repeals Directive 2006/53/EC (see summary).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all types of firearms:
converted weapons* (including rifles, shotguns, pistols, assault rifles, revolvers, handguns, grenade launchers and hand grenades);
firearm accessories (including ammunition, ammunition, spare parts and spare parts);
convertible military equipment (including explosives, bombs, torpedoes and landmines);
automatic rifles and shotguns;
machineguns;
explosives and grenade launchers;
live ammunition;
battery-operated rifles and carbines;
automatic grenade launchers.
EU countries may authorise the temporary handing over of firearms in accordance with procedures which they must lay down in their national law.
The holder of an EU firearms pass must:
have a valid EU country’s national identity card;
have the right to carry a weapon or firearm in their possession;
use the pass for up to 30 years;
be able to carry the weapon or firearms in their country of residence;
inform the competent national authorities of their identity and the name and address of the holder of the pass;
ensure that the pass is valid throughout the EU;
notify the competent authorities of any confiscation, confiscation or transfer of the firearms or essential components in question within 30 days of receiving the pass.
Passes are valid for 10 years from the date on which they are issued. They may be renewed for a further 10 years after the destruction of the firearm or essential component in question.
Eligibility for holders
Holders of firearms of categories A, B, C, D, E, F, G, H, I, II, III, IV, V, VI, VII, VIII, IX, X, XI, XII, XIII, XIII and XI must have a valid national identification card issued by the EU country issuing the pass, which must contain the following information:
the holder's name and contact details;
the number of rounds of ammunition and the number of magazines in which they can be fired;
a description of the type of weapon and ammunition and of the ammunition and ammunition magazines in each of the categories.
Transfer of firearms between EU countries
Transferring a firearm between an EU country and a non-EU country is subject to the following conditions:
transfer of the transfer of a firearm from one EU country to another EU country without the transferee obtaining or having a licence or being specifically permitted to possess those firearms;
transfers of a firearms of category C or D must be subject to a licence issued by a country in which the transfer takes place.
Transfers must not be carried out without the authorisation of the competent authority of that EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat the proliferation of illegal firearms. These include:
Regulation (EU) 2019/856 on the traceability of all firearms and their essential components (the Traceability Directive);
the Traceable Firearms Directive (the Firearms Directive); and
the Restrictive Firearms Directive.
For more information, see:
‘Tracing Firearms’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 11:
Maritime Single Window Information System (EMSWe) for the management of the EU’s single market in maritime transport (2014-2027)
Maritime single window Information System for the Management of the European Union‘SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment of the maritime single window information system (EMSW)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the maritime national single window (EMSW) Information System, which collects information on all information elements requested by national authorities or port operators for administrative or operational purposes when a ship makes a port call.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ national authorities and port operators (i.e. the EU countries themselves) when a passenger vessel calls at one of their ports.
It does not apply to:
ship identification information, particulars and exemptions;
presentation of goods to customs;
temporary storage declaration of goods;
customs status of goods.
EU countries must ensure that the ship identification information and particulars are recorded in the EMSWe ship database and are made available for any subsequent port calls within the EU.
The data set is made available and reused in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 on the common ship sanitation database (see summary).
The European Commission is responsible for the operation of the data set. It is assisted by the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).
EMSWe data set
The EU country in which the ship is registered must:
ensure that:
identifies the ship;
ensures that the information is entered into the database;
keeps the information for at least 5 years;
provides the information in a format that is easily accessible to the public;
publishes a report on the use of the database, including:
the number of ships registered in the EU and the number of persons on board;
the total number of passengers and cargo;
details of the number and type of cargo and cargo types;
information on the number, type and duration of the port call;
a description of the ship and the port where the ship makes its last port call, including the name of the master/ssO/CSO/ship's agent or ship's agent.
Implementing act
The implementing act lays down the rules for the implementation of the implementing act.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending act(s)
The amending act introduces:
a common ship sanitation database (common ship database);
a harmonised reporting interface module to be used by EU countries when reporting formalities for ships arriving in and/or departing from ports of the Member States and repealing Directive 2002/6/EC (OJ L 283, 29.10.2010, pp. 1-8);
the common ship registration database (CSDB);
common ship sanitation data set (common data set) to be set up by the Commission;
an EU country may request the Commission to introduce or amend the data elements in the information set.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 15 August 2018 and had to become law in the Member countries by 15 August 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Maritime Single Window is the backbone of trade and communications within and beyond the single market.
For more information, see:
‘EMSW’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Maritime Single Windows Information System: an information system that collects and stores information on the information elements that might be requested by a national authority or port operator for administrative and operational purposes, such as:
passenger identification information (e.g. name, nationality, nationality of the person or entity providing the information);
ship registration;
ship number;
description of the vessel;
number of passengers;
cargo type;
duration of port call and cargo type.

Summary 12:
E-tolls and electronic vehicle tolls (EETS) — rules on cooperation between EETS providers and toll chargers (recast)
EETS and electronic ticketing systems (EETS)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/680/EU on the application of the rules of the European Union’s Electronic Traffic Electronic System (ETES)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EETS (electronic ticketing system) is more transparent and efficient.
It recasts and repeals Directive 2002/58/EC (see summary).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EETS users (e.g. motor vehicles, trucks, buses, coaches, ambulances and trailers) in the EU. It does not apply to parking fees.
Tolls are calculated on the basis of:
the number of vehicles registered in an EU country;
the total number of tolls applied by the EETS service provider to each vehicle; and
the average number of hours of use by each EETS user per day.
E-ticketing system
The legislation applies to the following types of EETS services:
e-toll service providers (e-ticket service providers):
toll chargers;
transit service providers;
passenger transport services;
ticketing service providers.
Cooperation
The EETS provider must:
cooperate fully with the toll charger in their enforcement efforts;
ensure that:
they inform the national authorities of all the details of the number of EETES users using their service;
provide the authorities with all the necessary information, such as: the name and address of the person or company responsible for EETS;
their contact details;
all the information necessary to calculate the applied toll on the vehicle;
inform the authorities of the reasons for the decision to apply the toll;
give the authorities all the information they need to carry out their enforcement activities;
allow the authorities to use the information provided to identify suspected offenders;
use the information as evidence in criminal proceedings;
take the necessary measures to prevent the misuse of the information;
notify the competent authorities of any infringements of the law;
assess the impact of the legislation on the safety and security of users;
report to the European Commission every 5 years on the implementation of the directive.
Safeguard procedure
The following measures are in place to protect users’ personal data:
in the event of a failure to pay road tolls, the user has the right to request from the controller access to and rectification or erasure of personal data and restriction of processing of their personal data.
In the case of a non-compliance with the directive, the holder of the specific registration certificate is entitled to:
a refund of the road toll charged;
a right to be reimbursed for the amount of the toll charged.
If a user does not comply with the above measures, the EU country in which the vehicle is registered must inform the competent authority of that country of the reason for the failure to comply.
The competent authority must also take the necessary steps to prevent, detect and prosecute infringements.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Electronic ticketing service (ETS): a system that allows users to pay tolls on the use of an electronic transport system.
* Toll charger: a toll service provider that charges a toll on a vehicle for the purpose of providing a service to a user.

Summary 13:
EU immigration liaison officers network
EU immigrants’ information exchange network
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/856 — setting up an EU immigration liaison officer network
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a network of EU immigration officers to facilitate and support the return operations of the EU’s migration policy.
It recasts and repeals Regulation (EC) No 377/2004 on the creation of an EU migration network.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the European Commission and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Europol).
The Commission and Europol are responsible for:
coordinating the deployment of EU-wide networks of liaison officers;
organising and coordinating the information exchange between the Commission and EU agencies;
providing technical assistance to the Europol, Eurojust, Europol and other EU agencies in the field of migration;
assisting the European Migration Agency (EMSA) in its work;
exchanging information and best practice on migration with the Commission.
The European Commission is responsible for setting up a Steering Board, which is composed of representatives of the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the EU countries and is chaired by an EU country representative. The Commission is assisted by a secretariat set up by the Commission in the event of a crisis.
Role of the Steering board
The steering board:
Establishes and manages the network and its tasks;
coordinates the deployment;
identifies deployment gaps and outlines possibilities;
estabides with the EU agencies and national authorities;
is responsible for the coordination and coordination of the network;
draws up the list of deployment gaps;
prepares a list of vacancies;
ensures that the Commission does not deploy liaison officers in locations where it does not have the capacity to do so;
supports and supports the networks provided for in the regulation;
cooperates with national authorities in the areas where it is not possible for the Commission to deploy immigration officers.
Information exchange platform
The information exchange platform is based on a secure web-based information exchange system. It is managed by Europol. It allows for the exchange of information between EU agencies, EU countries’ competent authorities and the Commission on migration policy and the return of illegal immigrants.
EU countries are required to:
ensure that the information exchanged on the network is up-to-date;
share personal data processed in the course of the work of the liaison officers for the purposes of implementing the return operation of the Union in accordance with the conditions laid down in Article 49(1) and (2) of Regulation (EU).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 18 February 2019 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 18 February 2020.
BACKGROUND
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that the migration policy is implemented and maximise the impact of EU action on migration.
For more information, see:
EU migration policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Immigration liaison officer: an immigration officer who assists the EU migration policy in its implementation and maximises its impact.
* Migration policy: an EU action plan that aims to reduce the number of illegal migrants crossing the external borders and to increase the effectiveness of its return operations.

Summary 14:
Biometric data in identity cards and residence cards of family members who are not nationals of an EU country
Biometrics in identity card and residence card and family members of non-EU citizens
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1954 — minimum security standards for the use of biometric data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down minimum security requirements for the identification of EU citizens and their family members with regard to their biometric identifiers (fingerprints, facial images, iris and iris).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU citizens, their families and non-nationals.
It does not apply to:
non-EU nationals who are entitled to stay in the EU on a temporary or permanent basis;
national identification cards issued on a provisional basis with a period of validity of less than 6 months;
validated residence permits issued by EU countries to non-European Union citizens.
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that the security of the EU’s single single market and the protection of personal data.
Biometric identifiers are used to verify the identity of the holder of an identity card or the residence card of a family member who is not an EU citizen.
National IDENTITY CARDS
National identity cards must be issued by the competent national authority and must contain:
fingerprints or iris images;
a facial image;
an image of a person with a facial image.
EU countries must ensure that fingerprints are collected only by qualified and duly authorised staff of the competent authorities of the country in which the card is issued. Children under the age of 6 years are exempt from the requirement to give fingerprints, except in the case of applications submitted to the diplomatic and consular authorities.
Residence cards
Family members of a EU citizen are issued with the same security standards as national identity cards, but are subject to the same requirements as national identification cards.
Family cards issued to family members are called ‘Residence card’. They are valid for 6 months and do not include fingerprints or facial images.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of this regulation and on its impact on fundamental rights and data protection principles.
For more information, see:
‘Biometric identity cards’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* National identity card: a card issued by an EU national to a non-member of the European Union (EU).
* Residence card: an identification document issued to a family Member of a member of the same EU country.
* Non-EU national: an individual who is a national of another EU country but is not a resident of that country and does not have the right to live or work in that country under the same conditions as a national.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management
Integration of EU Information Systems for security and border and immigration management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/817 — interoperability of the European information system (eu-LISA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the interoperability between the EU’s information systems in order to improve border control and border management.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes the European Information System for Security and Border and Migration (e-lISA) and the European Search Portal (ESP).
The aim of the regulation is to:
ensure that EU countries’ border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities have the necessary information at their disposal to prevent, detect and prevent illegal immigration;
improve the security of the internal borders of the EU;
facilitate the exchange of information between national border guards and customs authorities;
increase the speed and efficiency of border control.
It also aims to reduce the administrative burden on national authorities.
Scope
The legislation applies to the use of e-lisa, the European search portal and the multiple-identity detector (MIDDLE), as well as to the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Europol).
It does not apply to the information exchange system (E-CBSA).
Use of the Eu-elisa and the MIDDATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
This regulation is one of a series of legislative acts adopted by the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission in 2018. The other legislative acts include:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/1861 — establishing a European Border Information System (Eu-ELISA) — establishing an EU border information system and establishing a common EU information system for border and cross-border border management;
Commission Regulation (EC) No 767/2008 — setting up a common information system in the field of security for border management (e.g. EC) (see summary) (See amending acts).
In 2018, the Commission adopted a Communication on the implementation of the communication.
The Communication initiated a process towards achieving interoperability in the EU's information systems.
For more information, see:
EU information system interoperability (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Multi-Identity Detector: a system that can be used to detect the presence of a person or persons with a different identity than the person who is the subject of a search query.
* MIDIDLE: a tool that allows a border guard or customs authority to check whether a person is the same as the person they are looking for and to verify the identity of that person. It can be set up by an EU country or by an authority in another EU country to check that the same person is at the border and that the person has the same travel document or visa.

Summary 16:
Biomass and biofuels — renewable energy and renewable energy technologies
Biomedicine and biofuel: renewable energies and renewable technologies
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/856 — renewable energies & renewable energy & biomass fuels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions (GHG emissions) from the production and use of renewable energy (i.e. energy derived from biomass, biofuel and bioenergy) in the European Union (EU).
It recasts and repeals Directive 2004/36/EC (see summary).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to renewable energy* and biomass* technologies (e.g. biofuel, bioliquids and biomass fuels). It does not apply to the production or use of fossil fuels.
It does not affect the EU’s 2020 renewable energy targets.
EU countries are free to set their own national targets for renewable energy, but they must ensure that they are not higher than those set by the EU 2020 target.
The renewable energy target is set at 20 % of the total energy consumed in the EU by 2020. It includes:
20 % renewable energy in the form of electricity, electricity generated from renewable energy sources;
10 % of energy generated from biomass;
40 % of biomass; and
20% of energy from biofuel.
In addition, the EU countries are required to:
encourage the development of a low-carbon energy economy;
support renewable energy;
promote the use of biomass and biolucates;
ensure that renewable energy can be used as a transport fuel and as a heating and cooling fuel;
increase the share of renewables in the energy mix of energy production and consumption;
take the necessary measures to reduce the carbon footprint of the EU energy system;
implement a system to monitor and monitor renewable energy installations and installations;
inform the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the measures they are taking to achieve the renewable energy goal;
establish an EU-wide renewable energy strategy, which includes renewable energy as an integral part of its policy objectives;
develop and implement a renewable energy policy, including renewable energy for transport, heating, cooling and electricity generation;
introduce measures to support the development, demonstration, demonstration and demonstration of renewable technologies;
provide incentives to encourage the development and commercialisation of new renewable energy projects;
encourage the use, demonstration or demonstration of innovative technologies, such as photovoltaics, for the production of biofuel for transport and heating, as well as for the generation of heat and/or cooling.
Support for cross-border participation
The European Commission is responsible for setting up support schemes for joint projects between EU countries. These are open to any EU country wishing to participate in a joint project.
Cooperation
The Commission is also responsible for:
monitoring the application of the directive;
assessing the impact of the legislation on the energy market;
making recommendations to EU countries and the public on how to implement the directive.
Implementing the directive
The legislation sets out a number of measures, including:
the following:
rules on the participation of non-EU countries in the scheme;
the eligibility criteria for participation;
rules to be followed by EU countries;
a list of the types of projects eligible for participation in the joint projects.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2020 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been pursuing a clean energy strategy for the period 2020-2030.
For more information, see:
‘Clean energy’ (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Renewable energy: renewable energy is defined as energy that can be produced, stored, transported, stored or used in a way that minimises its environmental impact.
* Biofuels: biofuel is a fuel derived from food or feed crops.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  The Renewable Energy Directive (REFERENCES

Summary 17:
Radio Spectrum policy in the EU
Radio Spectrum Policy in the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 — radio spectrum policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the allocation of radio spectrum (RF) to the EU’s electronic communications markets.
It aims to ensure that the EU has a single market for electronic communications services and that the use of the radio spectrum is fair and equitable.
KEY POINTS
   
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to:
the EU countries’ national regulatory authorities;
telecommunications service providers;
the European Commission;
electronic communications networks or services and associated facilities (e.g. mobile phones, fixed-line telephony, broadband internet access, etc.).
It applies from 1 January 2014.
Scope
The directive applies to the provision of electronic communications networks and services, including:
communications services, at a fixed location;
mobile telephony;
internet access;
access to the internet;
radio spectrum; and
mobile broadband access.
EU countries must ensure that:
all users have access to the same radio spectrum, irrespective of their geographical location, regardless of where they live or work;
all services are free of charge;
users have the right to switch and number portability;
services are provided by a single provider or a single service provider;
information about security and network security is provided free-of-charge;
service providers are required to provide information about security to end-users;
end-users are entitled to a high level of protection against security threats.
National regulatory authorities may impose obligations on providers or beneficiaries of access, where necessary to ensure normal operation of the network.
In addition, EU countries may impose conditions on providers and beneficiaries of the same access, such as:
a minimum age limit of 18 years;
a maximum duration of 5 years for access to a given service;
an obligation on providers to provide the same service to all users irrespective of the geographical location of the end-user;
protection of personal data;
preventing interference between users;
protecting personal data protection;
ensuring the security of the user;
promoting interoperability of the electronic communications network and services;
encouraging the free movement of persons, goods and services across the EU.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending Regulation (EU) 2019/856 introduces a new regulatory framework for radio spectrum allocation.
The new framework is based on the principles set out in Directive 2005/698/EC (see summary). It is designed to ensure:
an open and transparent regulatory framework;
consistent allocation of the spectrum;
effective coordination between national authorities and the European Network Operators’ Association (ENOA);
a level playing field for all operators;
fair and transparent competition;
harmonised conditions for providers, users and beneficiaries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2013/96/EU has applied since 1 January 2013 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Radio spectrum: the electromagnetic spectrum used to transmit, receive and receive radio waves.
* Mobile telephony: the communication network or services provided by an electronic communications service to a mobile phone or other electronic device, including the internet, over the air or on the ground, over a fixed or mobile telephone or other mobile telephone, or over the internet.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егланска граша барка (Radio Spectrum Decision) (OJ L 108, 24.4.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2013 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 18:
Reporting of heavy-duty vehicles’ CO2 emissions, fuel consumption and fuel consumption
Reporting of heavier-duty vehicle emissions, fuels consumption and energy consumption
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/856 — reporting of heavy duty vehicles‘ CO2 emission and fuel-related data’
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules for the reporting and reporting of greenhouse gas (CO2) emissions, energy consumption and total fuel consumption by heavy duty vehicle manufacturers and operators in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation requires EU countries and manufacturers to report to the European Commission (Eurostat) on the performance of their vehicles and their fuel consumption, as well as on the number of tonnes of CO2 and energy used.
The data are to be reported to Eurostat, which then transmits the information to the EU countries’ competent authorities.
EU countries must ensure that the information is accurate, comparable and up-to-date.
Monitoring and reporting must be carried out by manufacturers and by the Commission, which has the power to impose administrative fines for non-compliance with the regulation’s requirements.
Reporting must be based on a verification procedure, which must include on-road testing and on-the-spot checks by the competent authority of the EU country in which the vehicle is registered.
If a vehicle is found to be in breach of the reporting requirements, the Commission is able to impose an administrative fine of up to EUR 10 000 000 for each year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRYING APPLY?
It entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
This regulation is one of a series of measures designed to improve the transparency of EU legislation in the light of the CO2 market and to improve public participation in the decision-making process.
For more information, see:
‘Monitoring of light commercial vehicles (European Commission)’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Light commercial vehicle: a vehicle designed and built to carry more than 10 tonnes of goods and having a mass of more than 1,000 kg.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еб   бараканска бълганка, блаксанна гарка  гранъка and глана  цакка: heavy commercial vehicles, their trailers, systems, components and separate technical units intended for such vehicles, amending Regulations (EC) No 715/2007 and (EC, No 595/2009 and repealing Directive 2007/46/EC (OJ L 151, 14.6.2018, pp. 1-8)
* Heavy-duty Vehicle: a heavy-heavy vehicle with a gross tonnage of at least 1,500 kg and a maximum mass of over 2,500kg.

Summary 19:
Emergency gas supply and infrastructure in the event of a gas crisis
Emergency gas transport and emergency response
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/856 — emergency gas supply in the case of a natural gas crisis in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that the EU’s gas infrastructure is prepared in the face of a major gas crisis.
It sets out the emergency response procedures and the procedures for the coordination of national and EU-wide measures.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
Emergency response procedures
Each EU country must draw up an emergency plan for its own natural gas infrastructure. It must include:
a description of the measures to be taken to ensure gas is supplied to solidarity protected customers in the requesting EU country;
a list of the main actors involved in the emergency plan;
the information to be provided to the European Commission, the European Gas Coordination Group (EGCG) and the European Energy Community Contracting Parties (EECCPs).
The emergency plan must be drawn up by the competent authorities of each EU country and must be approved by the Commission.
The Commission must consult the European Parliament and the Council before taking any investment decision.
EU countries must ensure that:
the emergency plan is drawn up and approved by all the relevant national authorities;
information is communicated to the public, the media and the public is kept informed of the emergency situation;
all the necessary measures are in place of safety and security;
gas supplies are guaranteed to solidarity-protected customers are supplied to the requested EU country in accordance with their emergency plan.
In exceptional circumstances, the competent authority may take action deviating from its emergency plan if:
it considers that the emergency is not being prepared correctly;
there is a risk of serious damage to the infrastructure of the requesting country; or
it is not compatible with the national emergency plan or with other EU emergency plans.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE?
It entered into force on 1 November 2017.
BACKGROUND
 The regulation is one of a series of measures designed to protect the EU in case of natural gas shortages.
For more information, see:
‘Emergency gas crisis preparedness’ on the ENTSOG website.
* KEY TERMS
* Emergency gas: natural gas is used to meet the needs of industrial, electricity generation, district heating, residential and services, as well as for transport. It is also used to provide heating, cooling and other essential services in the EU.

Summary 20:
Energy efficiency of energy-related products and energy-saving appliances — EU rules (Regulation (EU) 2019/1369)
Energy efficiency and energy saving appliances — European rules (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 1369/2017 on energy efficiency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that energy-efficient products and appliances are sold throughout the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
energy-related appliances, such as boilers, supplementary heaters, temperature controls and solar devices;
suppliers of these products;
consumers and dealers;
the energy efficiency of the products themselves.
It lays down the rules on penalties and enforcement mechanisms applicable to infringements of this regulation and the delegated acts.
Scope
It applies to the sale, placing on the market or putting into service of:
a solid fuel boiler;
a heater;
an auxiliary heaters;
temperature controls;
solar panels;
alternative heating and cooling systems;
battery chargers; and
other energy efficient products.
Suppliers and dealers must ensure that:
the product is clearly displayed on the packaging and on the product information sheet;
inform consumers and dealers of the energy efficiency class and class of the product;
ensure that the label is clearly visible and clearly distinguishable from other product information on the label;
provide consumers with information on how the product complies with the regulation’s conformity assessment procedures;
make sure that the product meets the relevant EU legislation.
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts to amend the regulation if it considers that a product does not comply with the requirements of the regulation or that it does not conform to the regulation.
EU countries may adopt national measures to protect consumers, suppliers and dealers from non-compliance with this regulation. These measures may include:
temporary or permanent bans on the sale of a product if it is found to be in breach of the legislation;
restrictions on the use of certain energy efficiency classes;
ban on the placing on sale of products if the product is found not to meet the requirements;
introduction of a new class of energy efficient product to replace a class of product that is no longer being used;
implementing measures to prevent the placing in service of a non-energy-efficient product which is not complying with the regulations;
exemptions from the regulation for certain products such as:
boilers;
separate heaters (e.g. space heaters);
alternate heating and space-saving units;
cogenerators;
coffinage appliances;
gas heaters and heaters.
Exemptions are granted to certain products, including:
batteries;
fuel pumps;
access to boilers and separate heaters for heating, cooling and space heating;
extension of the range of products covered by the regulation;
energy efficient products for the production, storage and distribution of energy;
compulsory installation of heat and cooling equipment;
equipment to be installed in a residential building or in a commercial building;
installation of a solid fuel heater or supplementary heater.
Implementation
The Commission is to adopt a delegated act setting out the rules for the implementation of the delegated act.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is one of a series of measures designed to improve energy efficiency in the EU. It is part of the EU's energy efficiency package. The other measures are:
Regulation No 1025/2012 (see summary), which sets out rules on energy labelling and energy efficiency for energy products, energy efficiency standards, and the European Energy Agency (EEA).
For more information, see:
‘Energy efficiency’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Energy-related product: a product which uses energy to provide a service or to provide an energy service.
* Solid fuel boiler: a device which is used to provide heat and/or cooling to a residential or commercial building or to transport a person or household.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Implementing Regulation of 20 December 2017 laying down implementing measures relating to energy efficiency and implementing delegated acts relating to the Energy Efficiency Regulation (EU, Euratom) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2017/1370 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 21:
Protecting the rights and freedoms of natural persons in the processing of their personal data (Regulation (EU) 2018/1369)
Protecting natural persons’ right to the processing and storage of their data (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation on the protection of the right and fundamental freedoms of the natural persons of the European Union (European Union)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure a consistent and high level of protection of personal data and to remove the obstacles to flows of data within the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to:
the processing of data by EU countries’ law enforcement authorities;
processing of data in the context of criminal investigations; or
the activities of law enforcement agencies.
Rights and freedoms
Individuals have the right to object and to be involved in the decision-making process when processing their data.
Right to object
The right of individuals to object to processing is guaranteed in the following cases:
when the processing is carried out in a way that is contrary to their fundamental rights;
in a way which is likely to result in them being harmed;
when processing is not in accordance with the law of the EU country in which the processing takes place;
where the processing does not respect the fundamental rights of the person concerned;
at the request of a third party.
In addition, the right of persons to objecting to processing may be waived in the case of:
in cases where there is a risk to their rights or freedoms;
to be informed of the reason for the processing;
before a decision is made.
before the processing at issue is made, the person has the opportunity to express their opinion and to have their views taken into account.
EU countries must ensure that:
data protection impact assessments are carried out by the competent national authority;
the data protection impact assessment must be carried out at least once a year;
personal data protection information is made available to the public and to the European Data Protection Supervisor (EDSP);
the consent of the parties concerned is obtained before the processing begins.
The consent of third parties is granted if:
it is necessary in order to fulfil the obligations of the contract or the intention to enter into a contract;
there is no reason to believe that the processing will harm the rights of individuals;
it would be contrary to the interests of the public or to the legitimate interests of persons having a legitimate interest.
If the consent of an EU country is not obtained, the competent authority must take the necessary measures to prevent the processing from taking place.
Consultation procedure
The competent authority is assisted by a secretariat provided by the EDP. It is responsible for:
monitoring the application of the regulation;
preparing a code of conduct for processing activities in several EU countries;
carrying out a risk assessment;
assessing the impact of the processing on the rights, freedoms and security of the persons concerned.
It is also responsible for taking the necessary steps to ensure that data protection measures are in place to prevent, detect, investigate and prosecute infringements of this regulation.
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 596/2014
The implementing regulation sets out the rules for implementing the implementing regulation and its subsequent amendments.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DELEGATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2017/1269 has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Protecting personal data’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  б  ъранска барка (Commission) Delegated regulation on the Protection of the Rights and Fundamental Freedoms of the Natural Persons (European Commission)

Summary 22:
The harmonised index of consumer prices (HICP) and the harmonised methodology for calculating the weightings of HICP weightings
The Harmonised Index of Consumer Prices (HicP)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2015/849 — the harmonisation of the harmonized index of the consumer price index and the method for calculating its weightings and the methodology for its calculation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the methodology to be used by the European Commission and the European Central Bank (ECB) to calculate harmonised indices for the calculation of the Harmonised Consumer Price Index.
KEY POINTS
The index is based on a series of data collected by Eurostat (Eurostat’s statistical arm) on the prices of goods and services in the EU countries. It is the main tool used to assess the level of price stability in the European Union (EU).
The Commission and ECB use the index to monitor price stability and to determine the weighting of the various indicators used by EU countries to calculate the index.
The methodology is set out in Regulation (EC) No 2494/95 on the quality of the indices.
It does not apply to:
fees and fees charged by banks and post offices;
charges by businesses and other financial services;
services provided by public undertakings;
other financial services, such as insurance and investment;
non-food and non-food services.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEGISLATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘The Harmonized Consumer Price Indices’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Consumer price index (CPI): a measure of the annual change in the price level of goods, services and capital relative to the level at which they were purchased in the previous year.

Summary 23:
Dumping and injury in the internal market: EU anti-dumping and countervailing measures against non-EU countries (Codified Regulation (EU) 2019/856)
Dumping & injury in internal market (Codification Regulation (UPDATED)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 856/2009 on the application for an investigation into the alleged dumping and injury of certain goods and services by certain EU countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the application of the EU’s Anti-Dumping Regulation (ADR) to the EU countries’ national competition authorities.
It aims to prevent and combat the illegal dumping of goods and the unfair competition of goods between EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to imports and exports of goods from:
the EU countries;
the exporting country; and
the importing country.
Importers and importers must:
provide the Commission with all the relevant information, including:
a copy of the relevant export and import permits and authorisations;
a statement from the exporter or importer that the dumping margin has been reduced or eliminated;
an application to reduce or eliminate the margin;
information from the importer on the reasons for rejecting the application.
The Commission may accept voluntary undertaking offers submitted by any exporter to revise its prices or to cease exports at dumped prices, if it is satisfied that the injurious effect of the dumping is thereby eliminated.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCELEGAL PROCEDURE
The EU countries must inform the Commission of the date of entry into force of the investigation and the date on which they intend to initiate the investigation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLYEAR OF THE CONVENTION?
   
 
It entered into force on 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
The ADR was amended by Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 596/2008 on the protection of intellectual property rights.

* Importers:
exporters;
importers of goods;
consumers;
producers of goods.
