Summary 24:
Countervailing duties against imports of certain agricultural products
Countervailed duties against imported products
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — countervailing measures against imports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect the EU’s agricultural sector against the unfair competition of imports of agricultural products from non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules on the application of the principle of non-discrimination between EU countries in the field of trade in agricultural products.
It does not apply to:
subsidies for the production, import or export of products which are subject to an export duty;
the granting of a subsidy for the export of a product which is subject to a duty, provided that the export performance is not dependent on export performance;
exemption or drawback of import charges in excess of those levied on imported inputs that are consumed in the production of the exported product (making normal allowance for waste);
the exemption or reduction of the import charges on imports of inputs which are used in the export operations of the exporting company.
In addition, the regulation sets out the conditions under which the Commission may impose a provisional anti-subsidy or a refund of a duty.
Implementing measures
The Commission may:
impose a provisional or refund of an import duty if:
the imports are causing injury to the domestic industry;
there is evidence that the subsidisation is causing injury;
it is contrary to the interests of the EU as a whole;
in the case of imports, the subsidised imports are likely to cause injury; or
the subsidy is not compatible with the EU's interest.
If an EU country considers that the import of a subsidised product is causing an injury, it may decide to impose a duty on the imports.
The decision must be made within 5 working days from receipt of the request for a decision.
Where the Commission considers that an import is causing a harm to the EU, it must:
decide whether a provisional countervailed duty is to be imposed;
make a definitive decision on whether a refund is required;
consider whether a review or refund should be carried out;
conclude that the duty is not justified by the facts of the case.
A decision may also be taken on the basis of the following grounds:
evidence that the imports of the like product are causing damage to the internal market;
evidence of the existence of a legitimate national market for the imported product;
proportionate and proportionate compensation for the damage caused by the import;
implementation of the decision; and
the reasons for rejection.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Anti-subsidisation’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Countervailing duty: a duty which is levied against imports which are contrary to EU law and which is intended to prevent, restrict or distort competition in the market.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratom of 12 December 2004 on the establishment, application and application of certain provisions of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (Codified version) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2005, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2017/849 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 25:
Eligibility for EU financial support for climate change recovery and resilience plans (Recovery and resilience plan)
Eligible for EU funding for climate and climate change adaptation
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/849 — eligibility for financial support to countries facing climate change
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the eligibility criteria for the European Union’s financial support (EU financial support) for climate action and climate resilience plans.
It also sets out how the European Commission assesses the eligibility of EU countries to receive the financial support.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out eligibility criteria and how the EU finances the recovery and resiliency plans of the EU countries.
The EU financial aid is to be provided to EU countries in the form of grants, loans and grants for the following measures:
recovery and resilience measures;
investment in climate change resilience;
reforms and investment in climate action;
climate action and adaptation.
EU funding
EU countries are eligible for financial aid if:
the measures are:
fostered in a climate-resilient way;
foster the sustainable development of the economy;
contribute to reducing poverty and inequality;
reduce disparities between EU countries;
promote sustainable development;
improve the quality of life of the most vulnerable groups;
increase the resilience of coastal regions;
ensure that the most deprived areas of the country are better protected;
have a long-term economic and social stability;
protect the marine environment;
provide the most of the environment; and
reinform the public of the benefits of climate action.
Recohesion
EU financial aid must be available to all EU countries, irrespective of their size.
Elimination of the eligibility criterion
EU funds are not available to countries that have not adopted a recovery and resilient plan.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 February 2019.
BACKGROUND
  (European Commission)
  The EU’S Climate Action Fund (CIF) is the main instrument for providing financial support in the event of climate change. It is designed to support the EU‘s efforts to adapt to climate change and to adapt its policies to the changing climate.
For more information, see:
Climate action and resilience (European commission).
KEY TERMS
  * Climate action: the EU's action to adapt and adapt to the impact of climate and energy change.
* Climate resilience: the ability of an EU country to adapt itself to the effects of climate changes and to cope with the consequences of climate impacts.

Summary 26:
Technical support instrument for EU countries’ structural reforms
Technical support system
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/825 establishing a technical support instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a support system to support EU countries in their efforts to implement structural reforms.
KEY POINTS
The aim of the support system is to:
increase the effectiveness and efficiency of structural reforms;
contribute to the improvement of the quality of economic and social life in the EU;

The support system consists of a number of elements:
the European Commission, the European Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions;
the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs, which is responsible for coordinating and complementarity with the European Economic and Social Committee (EASCE) and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) countries;

coordinating and complementing the European cooperation and support system;
providing technical support to EU countries and EAFTERMARY

information, communication, public relations and publicity;
publication of the instrument;
exchanges of information between the Commission and EU countries; and
the communication activities carried out by the Commission, in particular through its communication activities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

The EU is in the throes of a transition to a post-crisis economy.

Summary 27:
Information and enforcement of passengers’ rights on the rail network (recast)
Information and enforceability of passengers' right on the railway network
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/849 — information and enforcement measures for passengers on the European Union’s rail network
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that passengers have the right to travel without discrimination on the basis of nationality, race, religion, gender, age, disability, sexual orientation or any other grounds.
It recasts and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to international and domestic rail journeys and services throughout the EU provided by one or more railway undertakings licensed in accordance with Directive 2012/34/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary).
It does not apply to international or domestic passenger transport services provided by companies operating in non-EU countries.
Passengers are entitled to:
information to passengers during the journey;
information on the carriage of passengers;
receiving a valid ticket;
the right of redress in the event of loss or damage to their luggage;
protection of personal data;
access to traffic and travel information.
Information to passengers
Railway undertakings and, where possible, ticket vendors and tour operators must provide the passenger with at least the following information during their journey:
the general conditions of carriage, including:
a description of the carriage and destination;
a map of the route;
an indication of the departure and destination of the train and the time of arrival in the country of origin and departure;
details of the number of stops and the departure of the passenger;
travel information on the number and duration of the journey and the means of transport.
The information must be provided in a language easily understood by the passenger.
Rights of passengers
The passenger has a number of rights, including the following.
Right of action
The right of action includes:
recovery of lost or damaged baggage;
right to be reimbursed for damage caused to the luggage or vehicle during the carriage;
refusal to use the vehicle or the carriage.
right of action arising from damage to personal data.
A passenger may request a report on the condition of the baggage or vehicle and the cause and amount of damage caused. The report must be prepared by an expert appointed either by the parties to the contract of carriage or by a court or tribunal. If the person entitled not accept the findings in the report, he may request that the luggage be handed back at the place of consignment on surrender of the luggage registration voucher and, if the General Conditions of Carriage so require, on production of the ticket.
In addition, the passenger has the right:
to be informed of the space available for bicycles;
to have their luggage checked and reimbursed if the luggage is damaged or destroyed; and
to receive a refund if the baggage is destroyed or destroyed.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending the regulation introduces a new right of recourse to be conferred on the passenger in the case of damage or destruction of their luggage. This right is based on the principle of ‘mutatis mutandis’. It allows the passenger to seek redress in a court of law if:
their luggage is destroyed, destroyed or damaged; or
their personal data is lost or destroyed, or they are unable to access it;
they are refused entry to the country in which the carriage is taking place.
This right is conferred on passengers who are not nationals of an EU country. It is also conferred on nationals of the EU country where the carriage takes place, but not on nationals who are nationals of that country.
Annexes to the amended regulation provide additional information to passengers about their rights in case of loss, destruction or damage. These include:
an explanation of the nature and nature of the damage or damage caused by the loss or destruction;
measures to be taken by the person concerned to prevent or minimise the damage to the property of a person who is entitled to compensation;
any right to be compensated for damage or loss to the personal data of the person who has been injured or destroyed by the carriage or carriage of a vehicle;
exclusions to the right of access to the information to be provided by the railway company and the information provided by ticket vendors.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The original regulation had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a series of measures to improve the functioning of the rail system in the light of the COVID-19 pandemic.
For more information, see:
Passenger rights and access to rail transport (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Passenger: any person who wishes to travel by rail.
* Carriage: the transport of

Summary 30:
Reporting of serious breaches of EU law to the competent authorities and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF): protection of whistle-blowers (Directive (EU) 2016/943)
Reporting of major breaches of Union law to OLAF: protection of whistleblowers
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on the protection of persons who report serious infringements of EU Union law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that persons who have been the subject of a serious breach of EU or national law are protected by the protection provided by the EU’s anti-fraud legislation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU citizens, whether or not they work in the private or public sectors.
It does not apply to:
persons who are not covered by Article 45(1) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) (see summary);
employers and legal entities that do not provide for internal reporting channels (e.g. legal entities in the public or private sectors);
legal entities that are not involved in the exercise of their legal profession.
Reporting channels
EU countries must ensure that the following channels are open to reporting:
to the European Commission, the European Court of Auditors, the Court of Justice of the EU or the European Economic and Social Committee (ECESC);
to EU countries’ competent authorities;
to civil society organisations, such as the European Network of Chambers of Justice (ENJAC);
the media, elected officials, civil society or trade unions.
to national authorities or judicial authorities.
The reporting channels must be transparent, easily understandable and reliable in order to promote and not deter reporting, and must be open to all interested parties, including:
the public through online platforms or social media, or to the media, the media or elected officials;
the European Parliament, the Council or the Commission;
national authorities, EU countries or EU-level bodies;
public bodies, such the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA), the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) or the national authorities of an EU country;
non-governmental organisations (NGOs).
Reporting also extends to categories of natural persons, who, whilst not being ‘workers’ within the meaning of Article 45 (1) TFEU, can play a key role in exposing breaches of European Union law and may find themselves in a position of economic vulnerability in the context of their work-related activities.
Protection against retaliation
Reporting persons must be protected against retaliation:
they must be informed of the measures to be taken in the event of any adverse reaction to their disclosure;
they are entitled to the right of appeal against any adverse action taken against them, including the right to legal aid;
any person who has been informed of a decision to take action against them is entitled to have their case heard by an independent arbitrator.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
    дантанка баканска  българка (OJ C 202, 29.6.2014, pp. 1-2)
  Еблаксанданна гакка, Бакца закранъкабарна,  Бъглана  хакта каклкачка and Ббракнайна (Codified version).
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to protect whistleblowers in the light of the COVID-19 pandemic.

Summary 31:
European integrated border management (EUROSUR) — common rules and procedures for the EU’s external border management system (2018/1725)
European Integrated Border Management (EurosUR)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2018/1825 on the European Union Agency for Border and Coast Guard Cooperation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets out common EU rules for the implementation and management of the common EU border management strategy.
It aims to improve EU countries’ cooperation and coordination at the external borders with non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to the Schengen area (non-euro area countries).
EU countries are responsible for their own internal border management.
The European Commission is responsible for assessing the needs of the EU countries and for coordinating with the other EU countries in order to implement the strategy. The Commission is assisted by the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy, Federica Mogherini, and the Commission.
EU Member States have the right to adopt their own national border management strategies, but the Commission must ensure that they are coordinated with the Commission and that the Commission adopts a common strategy for each of them.
Strategy
The strategy is designed to:
ensure that the EU is able to manage its external borders effectively and efficiently;
contribute to the prevention, control and return of illegal migrants;
reduce the risk of illegal immigration; and
improve cooperation between the EU and non-euro-area countries;
promote cooperation between EU countries on their external borders.
Strengthen cooperation between national border guards and border management authorities.
In order to achieve these objectives, the strategy includes:
a common approach to border management;
the creation of a common information exchange system and a common return plan for illegal migrants and asylum seekers;
a joint border control mechanism;
an operational plan for collecting return operations without delay;
coordination between the national border control authorities of the participating Member States;
implementation of the operational plan with the host Member State;
cooperation between the host and the country of return;
effective coordination between the Member States’ border guards;
exchanges of information and best practice;
assessment by the Commission of the need to improve cooperation between Member States in the field of border control;
clarification of the role of the European Migration Agency (EMA);
the establishment of a European Migration Observatory (EMO);
a practical handbook for implementing and managing the strategy (the ‘EurOSUR Handbook’).
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTING DECISION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve its border management, in particular:
Directive 2013/32/EU on common procedures for granting and withdrawing international protection (OJ L 180, 29.6.2013, pp. 1-8);
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018 /1725 on common rules for implementing the common migration strategy (see summary);
Decision (EU), Euratom (EC) 2019/1724 on the establishment of an EU Border Management Agency (See summary).
KEY TERMS
* Common migration strategy: a common EU approach to the management of migration.
ACT

Summary 32:
Information and communications technology (ICT) is one of the world’s fastest-growing sectors, and cybersecurity is essential for its long-term sustainability. This is why the European Union (EU) is establishing a European cybersecurity certification framework.
ACT
Decision (CFSP) 2017/856/CFSP of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 February 2017 establishing a framework for the certification of cybersecurity products and services in the field of Information and Communications Technology (ICT).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DECISION DO?
It establishes a framework to ensure that cybersecurity products, ICT services and processes can be used safely and securely in the EU.
It also sets out the requirements for cybersecurity certification schemes.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The framework applies to all ICT products and processes, whether they are new or used for the first time. It does not apply to existing products, services or processes that have been in use for less than 5 years.
Certification schemes are based on a set of criteria, such as:
the use of the products or processes by end-users;
the risks associated with the use of those products or services; and
the security of the information and communication technologies (ICTs) used by end users.
Evaluation of the cybersecurity certification scheme
The evaluation of the certification scheme is carried out by the European Network of Information Security Assurance Authorities (ENISA), an independent body set up under the auspices of the Commission.
EnISA is responsible for the assessment and certification of products, processes and services covered by the framework. It is assisted by a Management Board, which is composed of representatives of the Member States and chaired by an Executive Director. The Executive Director is appointed by the Commission and has a term of office of 4 years. The Commission appoints the Executive Director for a renewable 5-year term.
The Executive Director may sub-delegate certain powers to the Management Board.
Role of ENISA
The management board:
is responsible for:
assisting the Commission in the implementation of the framework;
advising and advising the Commission on market, cybersecurity certification, and standardisation;
providing the Commission with information and expertise on cybersecurity certification;
ensuring that ENISA is independent and transparent in its activities;
monitoring and evaluating the application of the EU cybersecurity certification system;
coordinating the activities of the management board and the Commission;
exchanging information and best practice on cybersecurity with the European Commission and other relevant bodies;
contributing to the development of a common approach to cybersecurity and the harmonisation of ICT security standards;
promoting cybersecurity by design and privacy-by-design;
supporting the work of the Europol Cybersecurity Centre (EC3) at Europol, CERT-EU and, where appropriate, the European External Action Service (EU INTCEN).
The Management Board:
has the power to adopt delegated acts;
is chaired by a Commission representative;
appoints one or more members of its Management Board;
has powers to:
decide on the number of members to be appointed;
make recommendations to the Commission, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee;
take decisions on the budget;
decides on the composition of its management board;
draw up and adopt the annual work programme;
approves the work programme for ENISA.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 28 June 2017 and had to become law in the European Member States by 28 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Cybersecurity certification framework’ on the ENISA website.
* KEY TERMS
* Cybersecurity: the ability of an ICT product or process to detect, prevent and respond to cybersecurity threats and to protect its users and users’ data.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force: Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member State

Summary 33:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 — the transmission of information on gross national income (GNI) data
Regulation(EU) 2020/848 — the transfer of information about gross national incomes (GNIs) to Eurostat
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/829 — the transposition of the European system of GNI data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the comparability, reliability and comparability of the Gross National Income* data collected by the European Commission (Eurostat).
KEY POINTS
Each year, EU countries provide Eurostat with information on their Gross National Incomes (GNI) aggregates and their components.
The data are used to compile the EU’s common statistical programme, the European System of Accounts (ESA 2010), which is the basis for the European statistical system.
Eurostat then transmits the data to the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC), which assesses the reliability, comparability and comprehensibility of the data and adopts the implementing acts.
EU countries must ensure that the data are reliable, exhaustive and comparable. They must also ensure that:
the sources and methods used are reliable;
the data are accurate and comparable;
data are produced in a way that is consistent with ESA 2010;
there is no duplication or overloading of data;
information is communicated at the same time and in a timely manner;
all GNI aggregates are comparable and comparable to those provided by the EU countries.
In addition information is provided by Eurostat to the ESA 2010 data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEVELATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 18 April 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
The European system is based on the Gross national income* and gross domestic product* (GDP) data collected in each EU country.
For more information, see:
‘GNI’ on Eurostat's website.
KEY TERMS
* Gross national Income: the sum of all the income and wealth of an economy, including taxes, subsidies, social benefits, taxes paid by households, social contributions and taxes on goods and services.
* GNI: the total value of all goods and income, including income from production, consumption and services, including capital, capital formation, investment, capital movements and capital outlay.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units following the withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the European Union (EU) — type approval of vehicles and systems
EU type–approval requirements after the UK withdraws from the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/849 — type–applicability of EU type–approved vehicles and components to the EU market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the application for type approval in the EU and the placing on the market, registration, sale or entry into service of new and existing vehicles.
It aims to ensure equal treatment of manufacturers affected by the withdrawal from the UK.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following types of vehicles:
vehicles, systems*, components*, separate technical unit* and separate components* (e.g. brakes, steering systems, brakes, exhaust systems, etc.).
It does not apply to:
cars and light commercial vehicles;
trucks;
motor coaches;
cabotage vehicles; and
trams.
Type-approvals are granted by the competent national authority of the EU country in which the vehicle is built.
Application for a type‑approval
Manufacturers must submit a request to the competent authority of an EU country.
The competent authority is responsible for granting the type-approved approval to a vehicle, system or component.
Applications must include:
a description of the vehicle’s technical characteristics and a description of its conformity assessment;
the type of the type of vehicle and its intended use;
a list of the types of vehicle, systems and components for which it has taken over the obligations of the UK authority;
an application for the approval of a vehicle or a component for which the UK has taken approval.
If the competent EU country has not granted the type‐approval, the manufacturer must submit an application to the European Commission, which, in turn, forwards it to the relevant national authority.
Where the Commission accepts the application, it must inform the other EU countries’ competent authorities and, if necessary, the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) of the reasons for its decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 March 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of delegated acts to ensure the smooth operation of the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘EU type approval’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Vehicle: any vehicle or system intended for sale or putting into service within the EU, whether it is new, second-hand, third-hand or used as a service vehicle, or used by a third-party service company.
ACT
 
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom of 29 March 2019 on type-approval requirements for vehicles (OJ L 168, 30.4.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules on the organisation and functioning of the European Police Office
Europol — rules governing the Organisation and operation of Europol
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/680 — rules establishing Europol (the Europol Convention)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the rules governing Europol, the European Union’s police office, and lays down the principles and procedures for its organisation and operation.
It aims to ensure that Europol is independent from national law enforcement authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and non-EU countries. It does not apply to:
the United Kingdom (1), Denmark (2), Ireland (3), Luxembourg (4), Norway (5), Sweden (6), Switzerland (7), the United States of America (8) and the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (UK);
the European Economic Area (EEA); and
the EU countries’ national supervisory authorities (Eurojust, Eurojust and OLAF).
It does not affect the powers of national police or judicial authorities (e.g. Eurojust or Eurojust).
Objectives
The purpose of the regulation is to establish Europol as a European police office (EPO) with the aim of:
ensuring the protection of the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons;
protecting the security and integrity of the internal market;
promoting cooperation between EU countries in the field of law enforcement;
exerciprocal access to information and cooperation between national police and judicial and judicial authorities;
providing the European Commission with information on criminal matters;
facilitating the exchange of information between national and international law enforcement and judicial bodies;
contributing to the prevention, detection and investigation of crime.
The EPO has a management board consisting of representatives of EU countries, the Commission and Europol. It is chaired by an Executive Director who is appointed by the Council of the EU and is responsible for the overall management of the EPO. The Executive Director is assisted by a Management Board which is composed of representatives from each EU country. The EDPO is also assisted by an Audit Committee and an Ethics Committee.
Management board
The management board is comprised of a director and a vice-president who are appointed for an 18-month term of office. The Director is responsible to the Management Board and the Commission for the implementation of the Regulation. The Vice-President and the Executive Director are responsible for:
monitoring and evaluating Europol;
assessing the effectiveness of the Europol programme and its implementation;
making recommendations to the management board;
approving Europol's annual report;
adopting the annual report by the Commission.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The Europol convention replaces the Convention based on Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, on the establishment of an EU police office.
For more information, see:
‘Europol’ on the European Parliament's website.
* KEY TERMS
* EPO: the European Public Prosecutor's Office.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 652/2016/680 of 12 December 2016 establishing the rules establishing a European Police Organisation (the Epol Convention).

Summary 36:
Financial assistance for Member States affected by the COVID-19 outbreak (2020-2022)
Financial assistance to Member States harmed by the impact of COVID‐19 outbreak
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 — application for financial assistance to a Member State in difficulties or seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by an exceptional occurrence beyond its control
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It allows the Council of the European Union to grant financial assistance in the event of an exceptional situation causing serious difficulties or serious threats to the economic and social stability of an EU Member State.
KEY POINTS
The aim of the regulation is to enable the Council to grant assistance to an EU country that is in difficulty or is seriously threatened to be in difficulties due to an exceptional event beyond the control of the Member State concerned.
The Member State may request financial assistance under the regulation where:
its actual and possibly also planned public expenditure has suddenly and severely increased as of 1 February 2020 due to national measures directly related to short-time work schemes and similar measures to address the socio-economic effects of the exceptional occurrence caused by COVID‑19 outbreak;
its expenditure has not been covered by its actual budget as of the date on which the request for assistance was made; or
its general public finances have not been in sufficient shape to cover the expected increase in public expenditure;
the Member State’s gross domestic product (GDP) has fallen below the level at the time the request is made.
In addition to the above, the Member States may also request assistance if:
the general public deficit has increased by more than 5 % of the Gross Domestic Product as of an average Member State; or if the gross government deficit is greater than the amount of the general government deficit as of that Member State as a whole;
a Member State is experiencing difficulties in its balance of payments or its gross national income as a percentage of the total gross domestic Product (GNP).
The financial assistance is granted in the form of a loan granted by the Union to the beneficiary Member State, which may be guaranteed by the participating Member States or by the European Central Bank (ECB).
Beneficiaries of the financial assistance must:
open a special account with its national central bank for the management of the loan;
notify the European Commission of the decision to grant the loan, the reasons for the request and the terms and conditions of the agreement;
allow the Commission to verify compliance with the prudential rules laid down in the regulation;
provide the Commission with a report on the application for the loan within 6 months of receiving the loan.
If the Commission considers that the application is not justified, it may adopt a decision to extend the period of availability of the instrument until 31 December 2022.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULARITY APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 8 May 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
The COVID–19 outbreak and the need for an urgent response to the consequences of that outbreak prompted the creation of the EU financial assistance instrument.
For more information, see:
‘Financial assistance’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Member State: any Member State which is in difficulties, seriously threatened, or seriously in difficulty due to the effects of an extreme event beyond its own control.
* Financial assistance: financial assistance provided by the EU to an individual or a group of individual Member States.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 749/2019 of 8 May 2019 laying down the application procedure for the grant of financial assistance pursuant to Article 122(2) TFEU of the Treaty establishing the European System of Central Banks (OJ L 157, 9.6.2019, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
 
  The European Central Banks adopted a communication on the basis of the Council Regulation (Euratom) No 696/2014 on the establishment of a facility providing medium-term financial assistance for EU countries’ balances of payments.

Summary 37:
Regulation (EU) No 1095/2010 — resolution of credit institutions and credit institutions (CCPs) (recovery and resolution of CCPs) (REFERENCES)
Resolution and recovery rules for credit institutions
Resolving and recovery plans for Credit Institutions (RECOVERIES)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the rules for the resolution and recovery of credit institution (CCP) credit institutions in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all credit institutions* (i.e. credit institutions) in the EU. It does not apply to credit institutions.
It applies to:
credit institutions (e.g. banks, insurance companies, investment firms, pension funds, investment funds, etc.) that are not credit institutions;
the clearing members* of clearing members’ financial instruments;
competent and independent credit institutions, such as banks and investment firms;
capital markets;
clearing members* and their clients* that are regulated by the EU’s Single Supervisory Mechanism (SSM);
the cross-border provision of credit services by credit institutions that are based in the same EU Member State.
The resolution of a credit institution is subject to the same rules as the recovery and resolution rules.
Resolutions
The rules for resolution are set out in the regulation. They include:
the right of the resolution authority to adopt a resolution plan and the right of shareholders and the board of the CCP to participate in the resolution process;
a right to vote on the resolution plan.
the rights of the shareholders and board to decide whether the resolution is to be brought under resolution;
rights of the board and the shareholders to decide on any measures to be taken by the CCP in the event of insolvency.
A resolution authority must communicate to the resolution college any measure proposed by the resolution. The resolution authority is responsible for:
preparing a recovery plan for the CCP;
monitoring the resolution procedure;
identifying any obstacles to resolution.
If the resolution fails to achieve the objectives of the recovery plan or if the resolution does not meet its objectives, it must submit a report to the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA). The report must contain:
a description of the reasons why the resolution has not been adopted;
an estimate of the value of the assets and liabilities transferred under resolution and the amount of the proceeds to be transferred from each of the CCs under resolution, as well as a description of how the assets will be transferred to the other CCP under resolution if the assets are transferred.
Amendments to the regulation
The amending regulation has been amended several times. The most recent amendments are as follows:
Amending Regulation (EC) No 648/2012 (see summary) introduces a new chapter in the definition of ‘credit institutions’, which replaces the definition in Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 806/2014 (see below). The new chapter introduces the concept of a ‘resolution authority’ and sets out the responsibilities of the authority responsible for resolving a resolution. It also introduces the requirement for the competent authority to take all necessary measures to prevent the CCP from undertaking any of the actions referred to in Article 1 of this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The original regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014. It had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to modernise and streamline the regulation, including:
Implementing Regulation (Euratom) 2019/856, which aims to improve the functioning of the single market and the transparency of the internal market;
Delegated Regulation (CFSP) 2019-858, which sets out rules on the establishment and operation of the Single Resolution Mechanism.
For more information, see:
Credit institutions (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Credit institutions: institutions that provide credit institutions with credit and investment services.
* Resolution authority: the person or body that decides whether to put an asset or a company under resolution or to put it under recovery.
ACT
Council Regulation (U.S.A. 2015/852/JHA of 12 December 2015 laying down the conditions for the application of the Resolution and Recovery Regulation and repealing Regulation (US) No 552/2009 (OJ L 347, 28.12.2015, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (USA) No 345/2009 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2013/29/EU on the European Year for the European Railways
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
 
   
It aims to promote the European Union’s (EU) single railway area (the ‘single European railway area’).
KEY POINTS
The directive establishes the year as the year where the rules agreed under the Fourth Railway Package will be implemented throughout the EU, namely on the opening of the market of domestic passenger services, on reducing costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the EU and on providing the European Commission with additional tasks that aim to lower technical barriers.
The objectives of the year are to:
raise the profile of rail transport in the EU;
raise awareness of the importance of rail in terms of economic, social and environmental sustainability;
promote the use of the rail network in the context of the recovery of the economy;
increase the number of passengers and freight carried by rail;
encourage the development of a single European rail area;
improve the efficiency of the European rail network;
ensure the smooth operation of the railway network and its smooth operation.
In addition, the directive sets out a number of objectives for the year, including:
promoting the role of the EU as a single market for rail transport;
establishing the single European market for passenger rail services;
raising the visibility of rail services in Europe;
reducing costs and the administrative burden of rail undertakings;
providing the Commission with a European label to promote goods and products transported by rail so as to encourage businesses to switch their transport to rail.

BACKGROUND

Summary 40:
Safe reuse of treated urban waste water for agricultural irrigation (reused water for drinking and animal feed)
Safe reuse (reclaimed water for agriculture)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/856 — safe reuse of urban wastewater for agricultural use
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that treated urban wastewater* is reused for agricultural purposes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out minimum requirements for the safe reuse* of treated municipal waste water.
The aim is to protect the environment, human and animal health and the free movement of agricultural products.
It does not apply to waste water intended for further specific uses, including reuse for industrial purposes, which is covered by Directive 91/271/EEC on the protection of groundwater against pollution and deterioration (see summary).
EU countries must ensure that:
reused urban wastewater is treated so as to ensure it is safe for use in agricultural purposes;
the water is treated and reused in accordance with the best available scientific knowledge and internationally recognised water reuse standards and practices;
there is no risk to human or animal health or the environment;
it is safe to use the water for the purposes for which it is intended.
Reused water may not be used for the production of milk or other dairy products, unless there is a risk to the health or safety of humans or animals, or to the environment.
In addition, EU countries must take appropriate preventive measures to prevent the spread of the pathogens Campylobacter, Rotavirus, Cryptosporporium, Salmonemoneus and Salmonella, as well as the presence of certain micro-organisms in the water used for farming.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLYORKER RECOMMENDANGELATION
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
The water reuse regulation is one of a series of measures designed to protect Europe’s water resources against the adverse effects of climate change and drought.

‘Reclaimed water’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 41:
Mobility of highly qualified workers from non-EU countries to the European Union (EU)
Mobility within the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/40/EU on mobility of highly skilled workers from third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to encourage highly qualified migrants to move freely between the EU and the European Economic Area (EEA).
It sets out the conditions for granting and withdrawing an EU blue card to highly skilled migrants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to highly qualified foreign nationals (i.e. those with a high level of education or training) who are highly skilled and who have the necessary skills to obtain a highly qualified employment contract in an EU Member State.
It does not apply to:
highly qualified workers who are not nationals of an EU country;
highly skilled workers who have been granted international protection (e.g. the protection granted by the United Nations Convention against Transnational Organised Crime (UNCTO) or the International Labour Organization (ILO);
workers who are in the process of obtaining a degree or training, but who have not yet obtained a degree, training or certificate of qualification in the Member State concerned.
Admission, refusal and withdrawal
The application for an EU Blue Card is made by a highly skilled worker from a non-European Union country who meets the following criteria:
a minimum age of 18;
a high school diploma or equivalent qualification;
an education level of at least equivalent to that of a Member State of origin or country of nationality;
the holder of a high-quality job;
degree of training or qualification in an equivalent degree in the subject of the application.
The Member State in which the application is made is responsible for determining whether the applicant should be admitted to the EU.
If an application is rejected, the applicant must submit a new application within 30 days of the original application being rejected. The application must contain the following information:
the applicant’s name, address, date of birth, nationality, place of residence, contact details and contact details, and the reason for the rejection;
details of the employer and employer;
where an application has been rejected, a reason must be given for the decision, including the reasons for the refusal and the possibility to apply for a new card.
A Member State may refuse to admit an applicant if the following conditions are fulfilled:
they are not highly skilled;
they do not have a high degree of education;
their qualifications are not equivalent to those of a national of the EU country in which they wish to work;
there is no possibility to obtain employment in another Member State for a period of more than 6 months after the end of the period of entry into the EU;
it is not possible to obtain an employment contract with the same employer in the same Member State as the one for which they are seeking admission.
Member States may refuse an application if the applicant:
does not meet the criteria for admission;
has not obtained a high quality job within the period covered by this directive;
is not qualified for employment in the EU as a whole; or
has been unemployed for more than 3 months.
Application procedure
A simplified procedure is in place for the application for a blue card. The Member State which receives the application must:
allow the application to be processed in a language easily understood by the applicant, in accordance with the law of that Member State;
accept the application without delay;
implement the necessary measures to facilitate the application;
ensure that the applicant is accompanied by a copy of the necessary documents, such as a travel document and a passport;
provide the applicant with all the necessary information, including details of the employment contract, the employer, the social security number and the contact details.
An application may be refused if:
it has not been processed within the time limit set out in the application,
the application is incomplete or incomplete;
in the absence of a job offer, the application has not yet been made;
for reasons of public policy or public security, it is not clear that the application would not be fulfilled.
In addition, Member States must take the necessary steps to ensure that applications are not rejected on grounds of national security or public order.
Family reunification
The conditions for family reunification are fulfilled and the decisions on the applications of family members are adopted and notified at the same time as the decision on an application for the blue card is taken.
Non-EU nationals who are members of the extended family of nationals of the Member States concerned are granted the same rights and obligations as nationals of their Member State, except in the case of the following cases:
family members are entitled to the same level of protection as their spouses and unmarried partners;
children under 18 years of age are granted equal protection under the same conditions as their parents;
unmarried partners are entitled the same protection as spouses or unmarried partners

Summary 43:
Family leave and parental leave: ensuring equal treatment between men and women
Family leaves and paternity leave: guaranteeing equal treatment among women and men
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/17/EU on family leave
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure equal treatment of mothers and fathers who are parents and carers at work.
It sets out minimum requirements for the protection of the rights of both parents and workers when it comes to family leave.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland and the United Kingdom (1), Iceland and Liechtenstein (1).
It does not apply to the EU countries that have not ratified the 1989 United Nations Convention on the Rights of the Child (UNCTAD) or the Protocol on the Status of Women in the Employment Relationships of the International Labour Organization (OLIHR).
Parental leave
EU countries must ensure that:
employers respect the right to parental leave and the right of workers to leave the labour market before the birth of a child;
parental leave is not subject to a minimum period of service;
the duration of parental leave is subject to the employer’s decision;
workers are entitled to a maximum period of 6 months’ paid leave;
a maximum duration of 12 weeks of paid parental leave, with the possibility of a maximum of 6 weeks of unpaid leave, and no more than twice as long.
Employers are required to take into account:
the age of the child,
the gender of the worker,
a minimum duration of the leave and
the nature of the working relationship between the worker and the employer.
Flexible working arrangements
Workers should be able to specify the period of leave they wish to take and the reasons for any refusal of such a request or for any postponement of such arrangements.
The employer is responsible for ensuring that flexible working arrangements are available to all workers, irrespective of whether they are mothers, fathers or carers. Employers are also responsible for taking into account the needs of children and their families.
Worker rights
The employee is entitled to:
protection from dismissal and the burden of proof
protection against discrimination
protection of health and safety at work
the right to working days and working hours.
Protection from dismissal
The right to dismissal is protected against dismissal, dismissal or any other form of dismissal, except in cases of serious injury or damage to the health or safety of the person concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Family leave’ on the European Commission's Directorate-General for Employment and Social Affairs website.
* KEY TERMS
* Parental leave: a period of time during which a parent or carer is absent from the work place to care for a child or to provide care for the child.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ебранска гаканка  баксанда блакка (Parental Directive) (OJ L 31, 1.2.2016, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Directive (EU)* 2016/16/EU have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

  (1)
  The European Commission adopts Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 on the protection and recognition of parental and paternal leave (see summary) and amends Council Directive 2010/18/EU with regard to the protection granted to workers who have a child under the age of 18 and to employees who have children under 18.
See consolidated version.

Summary 44:
Criminal offences and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the EU’s financial interests (Directive 2016/43/EU)
Criminal acts and sanctions relating to fraud affecting:
fraud affecting the Union's financial interests
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/42/EU on the definition of criminal offences and the imposition of criminal sanctions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the protection of the European Union (EU), its institutions, bodies, offices and agencies against fraud affecting their financial interests.
It lays down minimum rules relating to the definition and application of criminal and administrative sanctions in this area.
KEY POINTS
 
The directive applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland, Luxembourg and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to:
non-EU countries;
the European Commission; and
the Court of Auditors and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).
EU countries must take the necessary measures to establish their jurisdiction over the criminal offences referred to in the directive.
The offences covered by the directive are those that are punishable by a maximum sanction of at least 4 years of imprisonment or a maximum term of imprisonment for a maximum of 5 years, or for an advantage of less than EUR 10 000 or an advantage not exceeding €10 000.
In addition, EU countries are required to introduce measures to ensure that the maximum level of sanctions does not go beyond the level of financial sanctions applicable to the offences defined in this directive. These measures must be effective and proportionate to the seriousness of the offence and not exceed the maximum amount of imprisonment.
Where an offence is punishable by criminal sanctions, the maximum period of imprisonment should be at least 5 years from the time the offence was committed. However, if the offender is subject to the Staff Regulations at the time of the criminal offence, the limitation period should not exceed 5 years.
Limitation periods
The limitation periods for criminal offences affecting the financial interests are set out in the annex to the directive, as well as in its Annex II.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
 The directive is one of a series of measures designed to protect the EU against fraud. The other measures are:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 883/2013 establishing the European Commission's Office for the Protection of the EU's Financial Interests (Euratom) and repealing Directive 2005/60/EC (see summary);
Council Directive 2006/70/EC establishing a framework for cooperation between national authorities in the fight against fraud;
Council Regulation (EU).
KEY TERMS
* Fraud: any act or omission to commit or attempt to commit a criminal offence.
* Financial gain: any gain or loss by a person or entity which is not the result of the exercise of a legitimate right or duty of the person or of the body or body concerned.

Summary 45:
Ban on interchange fees for debit card transactions
Ban on the application of interchange fees
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1093/2010 on the prohibition of credit and debit card interchange fees in the field of electronic payments
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ban interchange fees* for debit and credit card transactions* in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all payment card issuers and acquirers in the EU.
It does not apply to payment service providers (i.e. the companies that process and process transactions for the payment card companies).
It prohibits the imposition of a per transaction interchange fee on all domestic debit card payments within each payment card scheme.
The cap is 0.2 % of the annual average transaction value of all domestic transactions within each EU-wide debit card scheme, which is equivalent to 0.
EU countries may decide to lower this cap to no more than 0.5 % for domestic credit card payments, provided that the weighted average maximum cap is respected.
In addition, EU countries may set a lower per transaction credit card interchange fee cap of 0.3 % of annual average credit card transaction value.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
  
It has applied since 9 December 2010 and had to become law in all EU countries by 9 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission published a report on the impact of the regulation on card acceptance, card usage and the development of the single market.
For more information, see:
‘Delegated Regulation (EU) on the European Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: a fee charged by a payment service provider to a credit or debit card issuer or acquirer to facilitate the acceptance of a payment instrument or payment application at a given point of sale.
* ATM: the interchange fee charged to a merchant to process a payment transaction between two or more payment card networks.
ACT

Summary 46:
Transparency of qualifications and competences in the European Union (Europass)
Transparency and mobility: Europass
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2019/852 — Europass — European framework for the transparency of qualifications, mobility and mobility of jobseekers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a single EU framework for qualifications and mobility for jobseekers and employers.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets up the Europass framework, which aims to improve the visibility of skills and qualifications in the EU and to facilitate jobseekers’ access to employment, training and mobility.
The framework is based on a set of principles and aims to:
facilitate access to the European job market for all jobseekers, employers and jobseekers;
ensure that jobseekers have access to information and guidance on qualifications, skills and mobility;
provide jobseekers with free assistance to move to another EU country and employers with information on how to recruit workers from other EU countries;
support jobseekers who wish to move abroad and employers in the process of recruiting workers.
Europass is open to all EU citizens, EU countries and non-EU countries. It does not apply to people with disabilities.
It does not replace existing national systems for the assessment and validation of qualifications or competences, but replaces them with an EU-wide single system.
This decision sets out the principles and features of the framework, including:
a single European information system;
a centralised information system for all EU nationals and EU citizens;
the Europass online platform, which is accessible to all job seekers and employers;
an assessment and verification system for qualifications;
access to training, retraining, apprenticeships and apprenticeships;
transparency of training, apprenticeship and retraining;
assessment and validation methods, including the use of standardised assessment methods;
identification and verification of skills, competences and qualifications.
In addition, the European Commission will:
monitor the implementation and future development of Europass, including through testing, and on information provided through the online platform;
cooperate on the development of web-based tools, including those developed by third parties;
informulate and implement guidelines and guidelines on the use and use of the information provided by Europass.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the decision.
For more information, see:
‘Europass’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Qualifications: skills, qualifications, training or training experience, including qualifications and training experience acquired in the context of an activity, such as a job, which are recognised and recognised by the competent authorities or bodies.

Statistics on the movement of goods in and out of the EU by sea (NUTS) and by land
Statistics on movements of goods within and between the EU and on land
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/849 — statistics on the movements of cargo in and between EU countries by sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules for the transmission of statistics on trade in goods between EU Member States by sea.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the transport of goods by sea between the Member States and to land.
It does not apply to:
goods not in containers and empty containers;
transport of goods transported by land;

Eurostat must send the Commission a report containing such information and data as it may request in order to verify the quality of the data transmitted.

The data transmitted by EU countries must be transmitted by means of the following methods:
the transmission of data by the EU countries to the Commission;
data transmitted by the Commission to the Eurostat via the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
transmission of data from the Commission’s Directorate-General for Economic and Financial Affairs;
telecommunications between the Commission and the Commission.

Summary 48:
Statistics on rail transport — EU countries’ rail transport statistics (Regulation (EU) 2020/2032)
Statistics on railway transport — European Union (EU).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down common rules for the production of railway transport statistics at EU level.
KEY POINTS
Each EU country must report statistics to Eurostat on the transport of passengers and freight by rail in the EU.
These statistics cover:
passenger-kilometres (passenger journeys) by rail;
transit transport (transport of goods and passengers by rail);
traffic on the rail network;
freight transport (freight and passenger-carriage);
other modes of transport (trams, coaches, semi-trailers, goods trains, etc.).
EU countries must provide Eurostat with:
the geographical coordinates and other data needed to identify and map each network segment as well as the links between segments;
information on the characteristics (including the capacity) of the trains using each rail network segment;
the characteristics of the rail infrastructure and the types of transport units used;
data on the number and types of passengers using the railway network.
Eurostat is assisted by the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC), which is composed of representatives of the EU countries and the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2016/2022 has applied since 1 January 2020.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EC) No 91/2003/2003.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘Rail transport statistics’ on Eurostat’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger km: the number of kilometres of passenger transport by rail over a distance of one kilometre (kilometre).
* Transit transport: transport of goods, passengers and other means of transport, including freight, by rail.

Summary 49:
Exemption from the visa requirement for non-EU nationals of non-Schengen countries
Extension of the Schengen visa-free travel period to non-European Union (EU) countries
Exemption of the visa exemption for nationals of third countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) 2019/856 — the extension of the period of visa exemption from the requirement for entry and transit through the EU to third countries without requiring a visa
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It extends the period for the granting of the temporary visa exemption to nationals of the non-euro area countries (non-EU countries) who do not hold the nationality of an EU country and who are holders of a travel document issued by that EU country.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following non-nationals:
nationals of new EU countries, nationals of new countries formerly part of and stateless persons and other persons who are not nationals of any EU country, who reside in an EU Member State and who have the right to travel without a visa.
Nationals of countries whose nationals are not part of the EU, but who are stateless or stateless and who reside outside the EU.
Non-EU country nationals who are resident in the EU but are not stateless.
The exemption does not apply to:
travel documents issued by the EU country in which the holder of the travel document is a national (i.e. an EU citizen);
travel document issued under the authority of the country in question;
the holder of a passport issued by a non-member of the European Union country;
any other person who is not a national of the Member State in which issued that travel document.
Exemptions may be granted by the Commission on the basis of a proposal from the European Parliament or the Council, or upon request by the Member States.
In addition, the Commission may adopt delegated acts to extend the duration of the exemption, subject to a number of conditions, including:
the number of days of validity of the passport;
a list of the countries for which the exemption is granted (Annex I to the regulation);
the date on which the temporary exemption is to take effect.
If the Commission considers it necessary to amend the regulation, it must consult experts designated by each Member State.
It must inform the Council and the Parliament within 90 days of its entry into force, taking into account the available resources in the consulates of the member countries and the information provided by the national authorities.
When the Commission adopts a delegated act, it is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of the Commission, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Visas: a visa is a visa issued by one or more EU countries to a country’s nationals for the purpose of entering or transiting the territory of another.
* Non-national country: a country which does not have a passport but is a party to an international agreement with the EU on the recognition of the validity of travel documents.

Summary 50:
Eurojust: rules of procedure and legal framework
Eurojust : rules of process
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 — Eurojust
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out Eurojust’s legal framework and sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the European Judicial Network (Eurojust).
KEY POINTS
The regulation is based on the Treaty on European Union (TEU) and the European Criminal Police Organisation (ECPO).
It establishes Eurojust as a European judicial network (EPPO).
The EPPO is responsible for the coordination of judicial cooperation between EU countries and Eurojust. It is also responsible for investigating, prosecuting and prosecuting criminal offences.
It is the first judicial network established in the EU and is the only judicial network in the European Union.
The purpose of Eurojust is to:
facilitate the exchange of information between national judicial authorities;
support the fight against organised crime;
ensure the protection of the rights of victims and victims’ families;
promote cooperation between judicial authorities in criminal matters;
provide a forum for the exchange and exchange of experience and best practice in the field of criminal law;
facilitating cooperation between national authorities in the fight crime; and
support Eurojust in its work.
In addition, Eurojust has a number of specific tasks, such as:
monitoring criminal offences;
collecting, analysing and analysing operational personal data;
assisting in the investigation of criminal offences in EU countries;
examining and investigating criminal offences, as well as in the prosecution of criminals.
EuroJustices
The European Commission is the body responsible for Eurojust, which is composed of national representatives of EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE INTO FORCE?
 
   
It has applied since 6 November 2018 and had to become law in the Member States by 6 November 2019.
BACKGROUND
  (European Commission)
The Treaty on the Functioning of the EU provides for the establishment of a judicial network.
For more information, see:
‘Eurojust and the EU Judicial Network’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Eurojust:
European Judicial Network: a network of national judicial and law enforcement authorities (e.g. police, prosecutors, judicial authorities, customs authorities, etc.).
* Operational personal data (OPD): the data that EU countries collect and store on the basis of their national police and judicial authorities’ national crime databases.

Summary 51:
Freezing and confiscation of instrumentalities and proceeds of crime: combating money laundering by means of criminal law (Directive (EU) 2015/849) (see summary)
Freezing/confiscating instrumentalities*
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/42/EU on the freezing/fiscally controlled instrumentalities (i.e. property derived from criminal activity)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to prevent and combat money laundering and to confiscate instrumentalities*.
KEY POINTS
The directive aims to:
facilitate cooperation between EU countries’ competent authorities;
improve cooperation in the fight against money laundering;
ensure effective, proportionate and dissuasive criminal penalties are applied;
protect the victims of crime;
remove financial incentives which drive crime.
It does not apply to property deriving from activities of a criminal nature, including in the absence of a conviction of a specific person or persons for those activities.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that the offences referred to in Articles 3 and 4 of the directive are punishable by effective and proportionate criminal penalties.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
the instrumentalities are frozen and the proceeds of criminal activity are confiscated;
the confiscation is carried out in accordance with the law of the EU country in which the instrumentalities or proceeds are derived.
The confiscation may take place in the territory of a non-EU country, subject to certain conditions, such as:
an EU country’s consent to do so;
a prior consent of the person or body;
an agreement between the person concerned and an EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Fighting money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Instrumentalities: instrumentalities: objects, goods or services that are used for the purpose of committing or attempting to commit a criminal offence. They are instrumentalities, goods, services or goods that are intended or intended to be used for a criminal purpose.

Summary 52:
Fishing for bluefin tuna: EU rules for the conservation of Atlantic Tunas (until 2021) (Regulation (EU) 2021/2021)
Fishing to conserve bluefin tunas: UN Convention
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 640/2010 — EU rules on fishing for certain stocks of highly migratory fish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for fishing to conserve the stocks of Atlantic tunas (‘bluefin tuna’).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels and traps fishing actively for Atlantic bluefin fish.
It does not apply to:
fishing for certain species of fish (e.g. sharks, skates, porbeagle, swordfish);
catch of incidental catches of fish weighing between 8 and 30 kg or with a fork length between 75 and 115 cm;
targeted incidental catches (i.e. fish caught by a fishing vessel or a trawler) of less than 5% of the total number of fish caught in the Atlantic.
The EU is party to the International Convention for the Conservation of Tropical Tunas, which aims to ensure the long-term conservation of the Atlantic tuna population and the sustainable management of its stocks.
EU countries are required to ensure that:
all fishing vessels are registered and registered in the EU;
the number of fishing vessels registered in each EU country is limited to a maximum of 10;
fisheries are registered in all EU countries;
there is no more than one fishing vessel registered in an EU country per fishing area.
Fisheries in the regulation are subject to the same rules as those laid down in the ICCAT Convention.
Each EU country must designate one or more flag countries to carry out the following tasks:
monitoring the fishing activities;
ensuring compliance with the regulation;
keeping a logbook of all fishing activities, including the date, time and positions, the quantities of fish taken on board, and the fishing vessel, farm or trap name he/she is operating in association with.
Monitoring the monitoring of fishing activities
Vessels and trawlers must keep a register of all the information they collect during their fishing activities. The register must contain the following information:
the name and registration of the vessel/trapper;
a description of the species and the species of tuna caught;
details of the number and type of fish transferred.
If a vessel/trap is found to be in breach of the regulation, it must inform the EU country in which it is registered and inform the other EU countries of the reasons for the non-compliance and the measures to be taken.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Tuna conservation’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fishing vessel: any vessel or trap used to capture, process or transfer fish. The term ‘fishing vessel’ is used to refer to any fishing vessel and/or trap used for the purpose of fishing for fish other than tuna, such as swordfish.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation(EU) 2018/2161 of 11 December 2018 laying down rules on the application of the Union rules relating to fishing to the conservation and management of certain stocks (Codification Regulation) of the European Parliament and of the Council (OJ L 347, 12.12.2018, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 2161/2018 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 54:
Cableway safety: EU declaration of conformity and certification of cableway safety components (until 31 December 2020)
CABLEWAY safety: CE conformity and certificate of conformity
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on the declaration of CE conformity of subsystems and safety components for cableway installations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s single market for the carriage of goods and services by means of cableways complies with the essential safety requirements set out in Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the design, construction and operation of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
cableway installations, such as funicular railways, aerial ropeways (cable cars, gondolas, chairlifts) and drag lifts;
subsystems* and components* used in these installations;
safety components* for these installations.
It does not apply to the following subsystems or safety components:
substances used in the construction, operation and maintenance of the cableway installation;
the equipment used to operate the installation; and
the components used to maintain the installation.
Conformity assessment procedures
The manufacturer must ensure that:
the CE conformity marking is affixed to each subsystem or safety component;
a random sample is taken from each lot of the subsystem or component; and the manufacturer affixes the CE marking to each lot.
the conformity assessment procedure is carried out by a notified body designated by the competent national authority.
The notified body carries out a conformity assessment of the whole or part of the sub-subsystem or component and verifies that it meets the essential requirements of the regulation.
If the notified body considers that a subsystem/safety component does not comply with the regulation, it carries out an independent conformity assessment and informs the manufacturer and the competent authority of the EU country in which it is located.
Importers and importers must check that the conformity assessment has been carried out correctly and inform the manufacturer of the results of the assessment.
EU market surveillance and control
The European Commission is responsible for market surveillance, control and monitoring of the market for these components and subsystems. It is also responsible for:
monitoring the safety and conformity of these components on the EU market;
assessing the conformity of sub-systems/safety components;
checking that the manufacturer has carried out conformity assessments correctly and that the relevant national authority has taken all the necessary corrective measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
Cableways are mainly lift systems used in urban areas, and include:
railways, including cable cars and gondola systems;
trams, trams and coaches;
levies, including escalators and escalators;
bicyclists;
motorways and cableways used in rural areas.
For more information, see:
Safety and health on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Cableway installation: any part of a cableway, including the equipment used in its construction, maintenance and operation.
* Subsystem: any subsystem, safety component or other part of an installation which is intended to be installed on the cable system.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratom and the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2012 on the notification of conformity, conformity assessment, conformity control and certification procedures for subsystems, safety components and safety systems intended for use on the Union market (Codified version) (OJ L 347, 12.12.2012, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 1070/2012 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
