Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services in the field of passenger transport
Assessment of the accessibility requirements for passenger transport — European Union legislation
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/36/EU on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the accessibility of passengers’ access to passenger transport services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that passengers travelling by public transport in the European Union (EU) have access to a wide range of transport services, irrespective of their physical, mental or sensory abilities.
It also aims to improve the availability of accessible products on the internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
passenger transport services;
electronic ticketing services, electronic tickets and interactive self-service terminals;
services provided by operators of public transport services or on their behalf;
mobile device-based services, including mobile applications developed or made available by operators;
information on accessibility;
accessibility of websites, mobile devices and mobile applications.
The legislation does not apply to the carriage of passengers by road, rail, air, sea, inland waterways or inland waterways.
Requirements
Products and services covered by the directive must comply with the following requirements:
the accessibility of the product or service;
the provision of accessible information and of information on accessibility to passengers;
a minimum level of accessibility to the information provided by the operator of the passenger transport service.
These requirements are set out in Annexes I and II to the directive.
EU countries must ensure that:
all products, services and websites covered by this directive are available to all EU citizens, regardless of their ability to see, hear, see or use them;
users of the service are able to make use of the information and information provided;
transit services are accessible to all users;
service providers are obliged to provide information on the accessibility and accessibility of information to passengers.
In addition, EU countries are required to take the necessary measures to ensure the safety and security of persons with disabilities.
Application of the directive
The European Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of EU countries and chaired by the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee.
Amendments to Directive 2014/35/EU countries
The Commission has adopted a number of amendments to the original directive. These have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 amends Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1025/2012 on the European Commission’s powers to adopt delegated acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The date of entry into force has not yet been determined.
BACKGROUND
The accessibility directive is one of a series of measures designed to improve accessibility in the EU. It is part of a package of measures to modernise EU law in the area of transport.
For more information, see:
Passenger transport and accessibility (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Accessibility: the ability of people with disabilities to use the means of transport, such as a wheelchair or a mobility device, and to communicate with others.
* Passenger transport services: services provided by an operator of a public transport service or on behalf of an operator.

Summary 56:
Supply chain due diligence: traceability of minerals and metals imported into the EU (Regulation (EU) 2015/856)
Supply Chain due diligence : traceability and traceability in the supply chain
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 — traceability requirements for the import and export of minerals, metals and related products from conflict-affected and high-risk areas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s supply chain* is traceable when it comes to the supply of minerals* and metals to the EU market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
mineral and metals* imported into or exported from non-EU countries;
precious metals* and precious metals* which are not listed in Annex I to the regulation.
It does not apply in situations where the EU importers’ annual import volumes of each mineral or metal concerned are below the volume thresholds set out in the annexes to this regulation (i.e. the volume does not exceed a certain threshold).
Requirements
EU importers must ensure that:
the minerals or metals they import are traceable and traceable;
they carry out due diligence* on the origin of the minerals and minerals they import;
the information they provide to the European Commission on their supply chain is recorded in a publicly accessible register.
The Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of EU countries and chaired by the European Economic and Social Affairs Committee (EASAC).
Imporporporters and importers are required to carry out audits of their own supply chain to verify that they comply with the regulation and that they have carried out their own due diligence.
Suppliers must also carry out independent third-party audits to check that they are carrying out their due diligence correctly.
EU countries may require that importers carry out the audits themselves or through an independent third party, subject to the conditions laid down in this regulation, but only if they are carried out by an EU country or by a third party authorised by the Commission.
Recognition of due diligence schemes
The European Commission is responsible for recognising and assessing the validity of supply chain due-diligence schemes.
If the Commission considers that a scheme meets the requirements of the regulation, it may adopt a decision to recognise the scheme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation(EC) 2016/852 has applied since 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat conflict and illegal exploitation of minerals.
For more information, see:
‘Responsible sourcing of minerals’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Implementing Regulation(EU) No 529/2014 of 5 March 2014 laying down traceability measures for the supply and export regulation (Regulating (EC, Euratom) No 1599/2014) (OJ L 183, 7.7.2014, pp. 1-8)

Summary 57:
Rules on origin of goods: customs status, placing goods under a customs procedure and releasing them for free circulation (Recast of Regulation (EC) No 596/63)
Rules on Origin of goods (Recasting Regulation (EU) No 696/64)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 555/63 — general rules on the customs status and placing of goods under the customs procedure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the general customs status* and placing-goods* rules applicable to the import, export and release of goods within the customs territory of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to goods originating in:
the EU;
non-EU countries;
the territories of the United Kingdom Sovereign Base Areas of Akrotiri and Dhekelia as defined in the Treaty concerning the Establishment of the Republic of Cyprus, signed in Nicosia on 16 August 1960 (United Kingdom Treaty Series No 4 (1961) Cmnd. 8679);
CYPRUS.
It does not apply to the territory of Cyprus or to the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia.
Customs status
The customs status of goods is determined by the customs authorities of the EU country of origin.
The rules apply to goods that are:
free circulation;
temporary admission; and
free movement;
storage;
specific use;
release for export.
Free circulation
The following the conclusion of a customs declaration, the declaration is valid for a maximum period of 30 days. It may be revoked if no later than 30 days after the end of the 30-day period of validity of the declaration or if it is no longer justified.
Released for export or free movement
The declaration may be issued by the holder of a valid customs declaration. It must be valid for 30 days from the date on which it is lodged. The declaration must contain certain information, such as the name and address of the holder, the date of entry into the EU and the country of destination of the goods, and the date for which the goods are released for export, as well as a list of the countries for which they are declared for export (e.g. Albania, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Bulgaria, Croatia, Greece, Hungary, Latvia, Lithuania, Malta, Poland, Romania, Serbia, Slovenia, Slovakia, Slovenia and Turkey).
Free movement of goods
The declarations are valid for up to 90 days after they are issued. They may be invalidated within 30 days of the expiry of the 90-day validity period, or within 200 days if they are not valid for more than 90 days.
Expiry of customs status
A customs declaration is invalidated if it has not been issued within 90 days of its expiry, or if the declaration has been issued by a third person instead of the debtor. It is also invalid if the debtor:
has not paid the customs debt;
is unable to pay the debt within 30 consecutive days of receiving the declaration; or
is not able to repay the debt in full or in part.
If the declaration does not comply with the above conditions, it is invalid if:
it is issued by an authorised economic operator for security and safety that is entitled to facilitate the holder’s activities;
it has been published in the Official Journal of the International Monetary Fund; or or
the declaration has not yet been issued.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 1964 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 1966.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Originating goods’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Customs status: the status in which goods are considered to be ‘customs’.
* Free circulation: the period of time during which goods can be moved from one point to another within the EU without any change in their customs status.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. the entry into force of the regulation.
  (OJ L 347, 1.12.1963, pp. 1-8)

Summary 58:
EU application procedure to combat counterfeiting of intellectual property rights (IPR) and counterfeiting in the internal market (until 31 December 2020)
EU application process to combat counterfeit intellectual property (IP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/97 — application procedure for the protection of intellectual Property Rights (IPRs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to protect intellectual property* rights (i.e. the intellectual property of creators, users or groups of producers) against counterfeiting.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all goods imported into the European Union (EU) from non-EU countries.
It does not apply to goods originating in the EU.
The application procedure
An application is submitted to the competent customs department of the EU country of destination of origin for the import of goods suspected of infringing an intellectual property right. The applicant must provide information on:
the nature and nature of the alleged infringements;
the goods concerned;
a description and/or coding of the goods;
details on movements of containers;
information on movements and movements of goods.
If an application is refused, the customs department may request the applicant to supply the missing information within 10 working days of receiving the request. If the information is incomplete, the applicant must inform the customs authorities of the reason for the refusal and provide them with a copy of the application.
A decision is then made on the basis of the information provided by the applicant and the reasons for its refusal.
Importers may refuse to accept an application if they consider that the applicant has not provided all the information requested or that is necessary for the purpose of the decision.
EU applications may be refused if the applicant:
does not provide all the necessary information;
is unable to provide the requested information within the time limit set out in the regulation;
has not provided sufficient information to justify the refusal of an application; or
is incomplete or does not contain the information required for the decision to be made.
An applicant may not provide the goods concerned if the goods are:
illegal;
imported into the EU from another EU country;
obtained illegally;
have been obtained by fraud or other illegal means;
originated in a country other than the EU;
are not protected by EU law;
may not be used for commercial purposes;
do not comply with the conditions laid down in EU law.
In addition, an application may not be refused on the grounds that:
it is incomplete;
it has not been submitted in a language easily understood by the competent national customs department;
in the absence of the required information, it is impossible to determine whether the applicant is a national or a national of an EU country.
Where the customs authority considers that an application has been refused, it may decide to refuse the application or to issue a non-renewable decision. It may also refuse to grant an application on grounds of public policy, public security or public health or if it is contrary to public policy or public security.
Applications may be rejected if they are incomplete or if they do not contain information necessary for its proper functioning.
Application procedure
The competent customs authorities may refuse an application for the following reasons:
they consider the applicant’s information to be incomplete or to be of insufficient value;
they believe that the information has been provided in a manner that is likely to prejudice the legitimate interests of the applicant or to cause prejudice to the interests of a third party;
an application is incomplete or incomplete because the applicant does not provide information necessary to the proper functioning of the procedure.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 15 December 1997 and had to become law in the Member States by 15 December 1998.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts in the context of the implementation of this regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Counterfeiting’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Counterfeiting: the practice of counterfeiting intellectual property by means of the sale of goods with the intention of infringing the rights of the creator or of the users of those rights.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 529/97 of 15 November 1997 laying down a procedure for applying for protection of Intellectual Property Rights in the Internal Market (OJ L 347, 15.12.1997, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 529/1997 have been incorporated in the basic text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 14.01.2020



Summary 59:
Customs programme: strengthening cooperation between EU countries’ customs and customs control authorities (2017-2021)
Customs Programme: strengthening the cooperation between the EU’s customs authorities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 — the Customs programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a new programme to strengthen cooperation between customs authorities in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The aim of this regulation is to establish a common framework for cooperation in the field of customs.
It aims to improve the coordination between the customs authorities of the EU countries in order to:
increase the effectiveness and efficiency of customs cooperation;
promote the exchange of information and best practice;
improve the efficiency and effectiveness of customs procedures; and
improve cooperation in all aspects of customs administration.
The regulation sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the Commission, which is responsible for the implementation of the programme.
Implementation
The programme is implemented by means of work programmes. The Commission adopts implementing acts to establish multiannual work programmes, which are aligned with the Financial Regulation on the European Stability Mechanism (see summary).
The work programmes are to be evaluated on the basis of information collected in accordance with specific monitoring requirements, while avoiding an administrative burden, in particular on EU countries, and overregulation.
Each year, the work programmes consist of:
exchange of information between the Commission and EU countries on customs matters;
exchanges of experience and best practices;
expenditure of resources;
training of officials;
visits and exchanges of staff;
working visits to EU countries to acquire or increase their expertise or knowledge in customs matters.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Customs programme entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The customs programme is one of the main pillars of the new EU strategy to modernise and modernise the internal market, which aims to reduce administrative burdens and improve the efficiency of cross-border trade.
For more information, see:
‘The EU Customs Programme’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Customs programme: a programme designed to improve cooperation between national customs authorities. It aims to increase the effectiveness, efficiency and efficiency in customs cooperation by:
developing customs cooperation with non-EU countries;
strengthening customs cooperation between countries in the area of customs control;
improving cooperation in customs administration;
reducing administrative burdens;
enhancing cooperation in border management;
encouraging the exchange and sharing of information;
increasing cooperation in areas such as:
trade facilitation;
customs information exchange;
the exchange of best practice in customs control equipment;
facilitating trade in goods and services;
implementing measures to combat fraud.

Summary 60:
Equal opportunities and access to the labour market, fair working conditions and social protection: the European Pillar 2021-2027
Equitable opportunities and equal working conditions: the social dimension Social Rights
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 — implementing the Pillar 2021/2027 — the European Investment Bank and the European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the implementation of the EU’s 2020–2027 social investment strategy (Pillar 2021/2020).
It sets out the key principles for the Pillar, which are based on equal opportunities, access to employment and equal opportunities for all, and fair working and equal protection for all.
KEY POINTS
The Pillar is based on three pillars:
equal opportunities: equal opportunities to participate in society and work, irrespective of sex, sexual orientation, age, religion or belief, racial or ethnic origin, gender, disability or chronic diseases, and children and elderly people;
fair working conditions;
social protection and inclusion: the protection of the most disadvantaged people, regardless of their sex, racial, ethnic or national origin, and irrespective of their age, gender or disability, and regardless of the level of education or training.
The EaSI consists of three strands:
1.
Equal Opportunities and Access to Employment and Social Inclusion (EaSI): the first strand is the main instrument of the Pillar. It aims to support the European investment bank (ESIB) to achieve the objectives of the Ea specific strand (e.
2.
Fair Working Conditions: the second strand aims to improve the quality of employment and working conditions for all workers and to improve their safety and health;
3.
Inclusion: the third strand aims at improving the social inclusion of disadvantaged people.
4.
Social Protection and Inclusion strand: the ESF+ strand under shared management (ESF+) is the specific strand for the Social inclusion policy area, which aims to promote social inclusion by:
promoting the socio-economic integration of non-EU nationals and minorities (including marginalised communities, such as Roma people, people with disabilities and people with chronic diseases);
improving the social protection for vulnerable groups (children, elderly people, children and vulnerable people);
encouraging the participation of women and men in decision-making;
promote the participation and participation of social partners;
providing financial support to territorial and local development strategies in order to implement the Pillar;
contributing to the creation of a European Solidarity Corps (ESC).
5.
Cooperation between the ESI and other EU instruments
The ESF and other instruments are co-financed by the EIB, the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) and the Cohesion Fund (CIFRD).
The European Commission is responsible for the coordination of these instruments.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
Regulation (EC) No 1060/2021 has applied since 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The 2020–2020 Pillar is one of the three pillars of the European Union's social investment programme. The other two pillars are the EU4Health (‘EU4Health’ and the ‘EU5’ programmes.
For more information, see:
‘The European investment strategy for social inclusion’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Equal opportunities: the conditions under which an individual or a group of individuals or groups can participate in the economic and social life of a society, including equal opportunities and the right to work and to be treated with respect to equal treatment under the law.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ебранска гарака (OJ L 183, 1.7.2021, pp. 1-2)

Summary 61:
European Voluntary Service (Evoluntary Service) 2020-2024 — EU-wide programme for volunteering
European Volunteer Service (EU-2020) — European Volunteer Service
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1288/2013 on the European Voluntary service
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to promote volunteering among young people in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the rules and objectives of the European Volunteering Programme (VSP) and the conditions for participation in the programme.
KEY POINTS
Participation
The VSP is open to young people aged 18 and over in the EU and in non-EU countries and territories. It aims to increase the number of volunteering opportunities for young people, especially in the fields of health, education and social inclusion.
The programme is funded by the European Social Fund (ESF), the EU’s budget for the period 2020-24.
It supports the following activities:
volunteering;
participation in solidarity activities;
solidarity projects;
networking activities for individuals and organisations participating in this strand;
quality and support measures.
EU countries may only participate in the VSP in its entirety and provided that they fulfil all the obligations which this regulation imposes on EU countries.
Eligibility for participation
The participation of young people is based on the following criteria:
they must be at least 18 years old;
they are not obliged to work in a public sector or in a private sector;
their participation must not be more than 6 months per year;
the duration of the volunteering must not exceed 6 months.
they do not have to take place in an EU country other than the one in which they are resident;
a minimum age of 18 is not required for volunteering in the country in which the volunteering is taking place;
an EU country may not restrict participation to volunteers under 18 years of age.
Organisations participating in the ESP are eligible for funding subject to certain conditions, including:
the number of volunteers and the duration of their participation; and
the quality of the projects and the support measures they undertake.
VSPs are open to all EU countries and non-governmental organisations (NGOs).
The ESPPs may not take part in the following actions:
organisations, individuals and entities established in EU countries or territories that are not covered by this regulation:
non-profit-making organisations;
public sector bodies;
agriculture, forestry, fisheries, aquaculture and fisheries;
social partners;
education and training;
health and long-term care;
research and development;
transnational organisations.
In addition, the programme supports the participation of:
young people in volunteering;
voluntary humanitarian aid corps (e.g. volunteers in disaster management);
young volunteers in the field of disaster management;
children and young people participating in volunteering in humanitarian aid;
people and organisations involved in the development and capacity-building of volunteers;
coaches, mentors and experts.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
    Еганска гранка  гландара барка, годарна какодна  ълака and гаккабаларскога данна (OJ L 347, 28.12.2013, pp. 1-8)
BACKGROUND
The European Volunteervest Programme (EVP) is one of the main instruments of the EU's social cohesion policy. It is designed to encourage young people to give back to society by encouraging them to participate in volunteering. Volunteering is seen as a way of showing solidarity with one another in the face of current and future societal challenges, which young Europeans are willing to help address by expressing their solidarity in practice.
For more information, see:
European Volunteering (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Volunteer: any person, organisation or body that is actively involved in volunteering, whether or not it is a public or private sector body.
* Humanitarian Aid Corps: a group of volunteers working together for the benefit of the general public or the local community. They provide volunteers with advice, guidance and assistance in the context of a crisis or humanitarian crisis.

Summary 62:
European Globalisation Adjustment Fund (EGAF)
European globalisation adjustment fund (EGA)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/1796 on the European Globalisation Fund (2014-2020)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an EU-wide mechanism to provide financial support to beneficiaries of the global adjustment fund for the period 2014-2020.
KEY POINTS
The EGF is the main instrument of the EU’s common foreign and security policy (TFSP) and is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Social Fund (ESF).
Eligible beneficiaries
Beneficiaries of the EGF are companies and individuals in the EU and non-EU countries that have been affected by the global financial and economic crisis (GFC) and/or by the impact of the COVID-19 pandemic.
The European Commission is responsible for managing and monitoring the programme and for the benefit of the beneficiaries.
It is also responsible for monitoring the implementation of the programme.
Beneficiary criteria
The beneficiaries of EGF assistance are companies, individuals and other entities that are affected by:
the GFC or the effects of the GFC;
the EU countries’ gross domestic product (GDP) as a whole; or
the countries of the European Economic Area (EEA); or
any other EU country that is not a member of the EFTA.
Specific eligibility criteria are set out in the implementing act (Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 883 (see summary), which sets out the categories of beneficiaries and the eligibility criteria for each category of beneficiary.
In addition, the Commission may grant EGF co-financing to companies or individuals that are:
operating in an EU country or in an EEA country;
employed or self-employed;
exposed to an economic or social crisis;
failing to meet their employment or social needs;
experienced in a particular sector;
in the context of an economic crisis; or experiencing an economic downturn;
preventing, limiting or limiting the use of public procurement;
implementing measures to improve the quality of life of workers and their families;
contributing to the creation of jobs that require lower qualifications;
supporting the development of a knowledge-based economy;
protecting the environment;
encouraging innovation and creativity;
promoting the creation and diversification of the labour market;
ensuring the protection of intellectual property rights;
providing financial support for research, development and innovation;
developing innovative solutions to address social and economic challenges;
raising awareness of the needs of vulnerable groups;
exchanging information and best practice;
creating and disseminating information to the general public;
facilitating the exchange of best practice in the field of employment and social policy;
establishing a European information society;
assessing the need for early intervention;
monitoring and evaluating the results obtained;
sharing best practice and best practices;
cooperating with other EU programmes and bodies;
taking the necessary measures to ensure the proper application of the rules and procedures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2018/852 has applied since 1 January 2019.
Amending Regulation (EC) No 1299/2014 has applied from 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The GFC is one of the main instruments of the TFEU, the European Union's main instrument for combating poverty, inequality and discrimination.
For more information, see:
‘The European Investment Fund’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Global adjustment fund: the European investment fund (EGF).
* GFC: the EU's common financial instrument.

Summary 63:
Registration of permanent representations in the European Union’s register of permanent representatives
Registration of Permanent Representations
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the EU and the European Economic and Social Committee on the establishment of a register of EU permanent representations
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — conclusion of the Agreement between the European ESI and the Council of the European Atomic Energy Community on the registration of permanent representation in the register of the permanent representatives of the Member States
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that permanent representations (representatives of EU countries in the EU institutions) are registered in a publicly accessible register.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Registration
The register is open to all EU countries’ permanent representations. It allows them to participate in the exchange of information on EU policies, laws, regulations, programmes, policies and programmes.
Eligibility
Persons wishing to become a permanent representative of an EU institution are eligible for registration if they meet the following criteria:
they are EU nationals;
they have a valid residence permit;
their permanent representation is valid for a period of 5 years;
the EU institution is based in an EU country.
Applicants for registration must provide the following information:
their name, nationality, date of birth, place of birth and place of residence;
a description of the activities they wish to engage in;
an application form;
documents relating to their permanent representation.
If an applicant is unable to provide all of these information, the applicant must provide a copy of the application form.
An applicant may be refused entry into the register if they do not fulfil the following a number of conditions, including the following:
the applicant is not a national of a non-EU country,
the information they provide is not sufficient for the register to be established;
there is a risk that the applicant will be declared ineligible if they fail to comply with the code of conduct (‘non-observance’);
the registrant has not complied with the requirements of the register;
in the light of information that the registrant may be ineligible, the register may be closed down.
Registration is voluntary.
Non-EU countries may decide to make certain activities targeting their permanent representations conditional upon registration, or of any complementary transparency measures that they take.
A management board is set up to implement the agreement. It is composed of representatives of all EU institutions and is chaired by a Commission representative.
It is responsible for:
coordinating the coordinated approach with regard to the activities covered by the agreement;
setting out those covered activities that are conditional upon the registration;
monitoring the implementation of the agreement and taking appropriate measures to ensure its proper application;
ensuring that the agreement is respected;
examining the application process and the eligibility of applicants;
taking appropriate action in the event of non-fulfilment of its obligations.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Agreement entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Registering Permanent Representants’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Permanent representative: a person who holds a permanent representation of one or more EU institutions in a Member State and has the right to express his/her views on EU policy decisions.
ACT
Council Decision 2017/884/EU of 11 December 2017 on the conclusion of an Agreement between The European Economic Social Committee (ESI) and the Union of the Eastern Mediterranean and its Member States on the Registration of Permanent Representatives in the Register of the Permanent Representatives of the Union (OJ L 157, 12.12.2017, pp. 1-8)

Summary 64:
Budgetary agreement between the EU institutions and the Commission on the management of the EU’s financial resources (MFF 2021-2027)
Budgetarian agreement between EU institutions, the Commission and the European Parliament and the Economic and Social Committee
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on budgetary discipline between the European Institutions and the Council of the European Union on the implementation of the annual budgetary procedure and cooperation between the Institutions on budgetary matters
Decision (EU) 2016/123/JHA — conclusion of the Agreement on better fiscal management between the Council and the institutions on the application of the budgetary procedure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve the functioning of budgetary discipline, to improve cooperation and to ensure sound financial management.
The decision adopts the agreement on behalf of the Union.
KEY POINTS
The EU institutions are committed to sincere and transparent cooperation and the work towards the introduction of new own resources over the period of the multiannual financial framework for the years 2021 to 2027.
Under the agreement, the institutions undertake to:
implement budgetary discipline;
cooperate in order to ensure the sound management of EU finances;
ensure that the EU budget is implemented according to the principles set out in the financial regulation (the Financial Regulation);
monitor the financial position of the institutions;
monitor and monitor the financial situation of the Member States;
promote transparency and the exchange of information between the institutions.
Cooperation
The institutions are also committed to ensuring that:
the budgetary procedures are transparent;
the budget is adopted by the institutions in line with the financial rules laid down in the Financial Regulation;
a common set of documents is made available to the public;
budgetary procedures are followed;
budgetary procedure before the conciliation procedure is established;
there is an independent audit of the budget and the annual legislative work programme;
regular meetings of the Joint Committee on Budgetary and Fiscal Affairs are held at least once a year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS AGREAGEMENT APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission is responsible for the management and implementation of EU budgets.
For more information, see:
‘Better fiscal management’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 65:
Economic recovery in the aftermath of COVID-19: EU financial instruments and mechanisms (Regulation (EU) 2021/2023)
Economic recovery within the EU: EU funding mechanisms and mechanisms
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — economic recovery in response to COVID‐19
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out how the European Union (EU), the European Commission and the European Central Bank (ECB) will support the economic recovery of the Member States affected by the COVID‑19 crisis in order to ensure a sustainable and resilient recovery throughout the EU.
KEY POINTS
The COVID–19 crisis has caused significant disturbances to economic activity and has had a significant impact on employment, social conditions, poverty and inequalities. It has led to a sharp decline in gross domestic product (GDP) and the unemployment rate.
The measures taken by the EU and the ECB to cope with the impact of the crisis have resulted in a significant deterioration in the economic situation of the EU Member States.
In response to the crisis, the EU is providing financial support to the affected Member States and the EU countries. This support is in the form of loans, grants, loans, loans and grants, and is based on the basis of a fixed annual inflation rate of 2% per year.
To ensure the sustainability and resilience of the recovery, the Commission is using the EU’s own resources (i. This is known as the ‘own resources’, which are:
the EU budget,
the European Stability and Growth Pact,
The European Stability Mechanism (ESGP),
the Structural and Investment Funds (ESIF) and
the Cohesion funds.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The crisis is the result of a combination of factors:
unprecedented measures taken in response by Member States, which were beyond their control;
a lack of coordination and coordination between the EU institutions and the Commission;
the absence of a common economic policy framework.
For more information, see:
‘Economic recovery’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Economic recovery: the period of time during which an economy is in a state of crisis or is experiencing a significant disturbance to its economic activity, which is reflected in a steep decline in Gross Domestic Product and in employment.

Summary 66:
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2015/849 on the enforcement of budgetary surveillance in the euro area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down general rules for the effective enforcement of Regulation (EC) No 1466/97 on the application of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) on the surveillance of national economic policies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation allows the Council to adopt specific measures which go beyond the general rules applicable to all EU countries, for the purpose of ensuring the proper functioning of the economic and monetary union.
It does not apply to:
the euro area as a whole;
the countries participating in the Stability and Growth Pact;
non-euro area countries; or
the Member States of the single currency area.
Penalties
The Council has the power to impose fines on EU countries that fail to comply with the requirements of the regulation.
Imposing a fine
The maximum amount of a fine is set by the Council and is calculated on the basis of the following criteria:
a country’s gross domestic product (GDP) in the preceding year;
its gross national income (GNI);
the amount of the deficit over the reference value; and
the percentage of GDP in the previous year.
If the Council decides to impose a fine, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for its decision.
The Commission may, on grounds of exceptional economic circumstances or following a reasoned request by the EU country concerned addressed to the Commission within 10 days of adoption of its decision, recommend that the Council reduce or cancel the fine or cancel it.
In the case of exceptional circumstances, the Council may adopt a decision imposing a fine on the maximum amount.
Investigations
If an EU country is found guilty of an offence, the Commission is responsible for the investigation of the alleged infringements and for conducting the investigations.
Where the Commission concludes that the infringement has not been detected, it may decide to launch an investigation into the alleged infringement. The Commission must report its findings to the Council, which will then decide whether to adopt an act or not to impose the fine.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 849/2015 has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The Regulation is one of a series of measures designed to ensure the effective implementation of the TFEU and the stability of the euro.
For more information, see:
Safeguarding the euro (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Fiscal surveillance: the multilateral surveillance system organised by the European System of Central Banks (ESCB).
* Excessive deficit: an excess of a government deficit over its reference value.
* Government deficit: the amount of public debt that a country has overspent or underspent.
ACT
Council Regulation (Euratom), Euratom of 14 December 2014 on the effective application of Regulation [Official Journal L 347 of 29.12.2014].

Summary 67:
Population and housing statistics
Population and Housing statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 — population and housing data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the transmission of data on the European Union’s (EU) population, family, social, economic and housing characteristics to Eurostat.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It covers the population and demographic, social and economic characteristics of persons, families and households, as well as on housing at a national, regional and local level.
The data are transmitted by the European Commission (Eurostat) to the European Statistical System System (ESS) and the national statistical institutes (NUTS) in each EU country.
Each EU country must transmit data on its population and on its housing population, its families, its households and its non-residential housing units to the ESS and NUTS in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that the data transmitted by Eurostat are comparable to those collected in other EU countries and that they meet the essential features of the European statistical system, i.e. they are comparable in quality, reliability and comparability.
Data sources and methodology
Eurostat is responsible for the selection of the data sources and the programme of transmission of the statistical data and the metadata.
National and regional statistics institutes must provide Eurostat with a report on the quality of the information transmitted by EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMigration and Integration statistics are transmitted?
From 1 January 2010.
REFERENCESMigration statistics
Each year, EU countries send Eurostat data on their nationals’ migration status, their families, their households, their non-member households and their housing units.
Housing statistics
From the date of entry into force of the census.
For each year, Eurostat transmits data on:
the number of inhabitants of the EU;
the total number of households and the number of non-members of households;
total number of types of housing units (conventional and non-conventional);
number of households (non-conformity);
the average age of the population;
average number of children (under 5 years old);
average age of a household (under 15 years old).
The information transmitted includes information on the following categories of persons:
persons (e.g. children under 18 years old),
children (under 18),
young people (under 16 years old) and
people (over 65 years old):
children under 18,
young women (under 65),
women (under 75),
non-married women,
women over 65,
people over 75,
non‑married women and men (over 75, unmarried men and women):
age of the average household and the average number of persons in the household;
age and sex of the household and of its members;
ages of the main family members,
relationships between household members, and
tenure status of households.
Non-derived data
Data are transmitted on the basis of the following sources:
statistical data from the national and regional data sources,
data from Eurostat,
the European Social Fund,
national and regional surveys,
other sources, such as Eurostat and the European Statistics System (ESS), and
the statistical data collected by the Commission.
Implementation
The European Commission has the power to adopt implementing acts to implement the regulation and to adopt delegated acts. It is assisted by the Statistical System Committee, which is composed of representatives of the national Statistical System Administrations and the Commission, and is assisted in this by the Committee of the Regions.
Amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/856
Regulations (EU and Euratom), (EU/2020/EC) and (EU), (Euratom) 2018/2111 have been amended to take account of the adoption of the United Nations Convention against Transnational Organised Crime (UNCTAD) and of the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY) as regards the accession of the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia to the Community and the application of the Rome Statute.
BACKGROUND
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure the comparability of EU statistics with those of the non-EU countries in order to improve the quality and reliability of EU statistical data.
In addition, the regulation provides for the exchange of information between the Commission and Eurostat on the methodology, definitions and programme of data transmission.
This regulation complements Regulation (EC, EC) No 176/2008/2008 on the collection and transmission of statistical data by the Member States.
* KEY TERMS
* Population: the population of an EU country and its main family,

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE RULES OF PROCEDURE?

Summary 69:
European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) — rules and responsibilities
Eurostat: rules and procedures for the establishment of the EU’s independent data protection supervisor (EDP)
European Union (EU) has established an independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons, and their right to data protection, with respect to the processing of personal data, are respected by EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies.
ACT
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1725 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 February 2018 establishing the European Data Protection Supervisory Supervisor and repealing Decision No 541/2014/EU and Decision No 966/2012 (OJ L 193, 30.3.2018, pp. 1-8)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
   
 
The regulation establishes the role and responsibilities of the Eurostat, the European Union's (EU's) independent authority, which is responsible for the protection of the rights and fundamental freedoms of the natural persons (e.g. the right to privacy, the right of access to information and to have their personal data protected and to be informed of their rights and obligations).
KEY POINTS
The role of the EDPS
The EDPS:
is responsible for:
ensuring that:
data protection is respected by the EU institutions and bodies, bodies and agencies;
protecting personal data;
monitoring and evaluating the application of EU data protection rules;
assisting the European Commission and the European Court of Justice;
collecting, analysing and disseminating the results of the data protection and data protection activities;
providing the Commission with information on data protection issues;
advising the Commission and other EU institutions on the use of data protection policies;
coordinating and coordinating the work of the Commission, the Commission's Directorate-General for Employment, Social Affairs and Inclusion (EDSA), the European Economic and Social Committee (EPSC) and the Committee of the Regions (CER);
monitor and evaluating data protection measures taken by the ESR and the EDPBudget and administration
The European Commission:
has the power to adopt and adopt the budget and the annual work programme for the period 2018-2027;
is the competent authority for the implementation of these budget and annual work programmes;
has powers to adopt the policy documents corresponding to its tasks and powers;
may adopt delegated acts to carry out the tasks and tasks delegated to it by the European data protection Supervisor;
can adopt decisions on the basis of a proposal from the Commission.
The Director is assisted by a Director-General, who assists the Director in the performance of his/her tasks. The Director reports to the Director and assists him/her in the execution of the duties of the Director.
In addition, the Director assists and assists the EPS Supervisor in the exercise of the responsibilities delegated to him or her by the Director, as well as on any other tasks delegated by the EDP Supervisor.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 1 March 2018.
BACKGROUND
 The European Union has established a new independent authority to ensure the protection and respect of the fundamental freedoms and fundamental rights of all natural persons.
For more information, see:
‘Data Protection Supervisor’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* European Union institution, body, office or agency: an EU institution or body, body or agency, or any other body or body that has a legal personality and has the authority to adopt, adopt, monitor and evaluate the laws, regulations, decisions, decisions and administrative acts of the Union.

Summary 70:
The EU’s system of own resources (OOR)
The EU's system of its own resources
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2019/852 — the system of the EU's own resources and its annual appropriations for payments
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
  
It sets out the rules for the use of the European Union's (EU) own resources.
It aims to ensure that the EU is able to meet its financial obligations, even in times of economic downturn.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision applies to all EU countries and the Commission.
The own resources allocated to the EU are:
1.40 % of the gross national income (GNI) of each EU country.
2.5 % of GNI of the Member States’ GNIs.
3.1 % of gross domestic product (GDP)* of each Member State.
4.5% of the Gross National Income of each non-EU country, including Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
5.7 % of GDP of non-Union countries, Iceland, Norway, Switzerland, Switzerland and Austria.
6.8 % of their GNI.
7.8% of GNIs of the non-union countries.
8.9 % of EU countries’ GDP.
9.1% of their Gross Domestic Product.
10.7% of all the GNI* of the Union.
11.7%.
The amount of own resource allocations to the Union is:
The total amount of appropriations to be made by the EU to cover the EU budget is not less than the sum of the GNIsland, sea, air, sea and inland water resources of each of the 28 EU countries;
the amount of the own resources to be used by the Commission to cover appropriations for payment:
a maximum amount of €1.5 billion;
a ceiling of €40 billion.
Each year, the Commission must communicate to the European Parliament and the Council the amount that it may annually call from a Member State for appropriations.
This amount must not exceed the amount of money that it receives from the EU country concerned.
If the amount is higher than the ceiling, it must be adjusted on the basis of a fixed deflator of 2% per year.
A maximum amount may be called from any Member State, subject to certain conditions.
Member States may continue to retain, by way of collection costs, 10 % of amounts referred to in point (a) of Article 2(1) of Directive 94/62/EC on packaging and packaging waste (OJ L 86, 5.3.2005, pp. 1-2).
The Commission may also call from any other Member State that is not covered by this decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The OOR is the main source of revenue for the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Own resources’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Own resources: a system of economic resources that is used to finance the activities of the Commission and the EU as a whole. It is based on the principle that economic growth is the primary driver of economic development.
ACT
Council Decision of 27 January 2019 on the establishment of a system for the management of the resources of the Community and repealing Council Decision 2007/436/EC, Euratom of 7 June 2007 on the system to be established by the European Communities’ own resources [See amending act(s)].

Summary 71:
Tyres — fuel efficiency and tyre abrasion and mileage (re-treaded tyres and wet grip tyres)
Tyres – fuel economy and tyre fatigue
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on the labelling and labelling of tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the safety and environmental performance of motor vehicles and their trailers.
It sets out rules on the content and format of the tyre label and on the information to be included in technical promotional material.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to tyres intended exclusively for racing vehicles. It does not apply to tyres designed only to be fitted on vehicles designed exclusively for road racing or to vehicles intended only for racing with a maximum speed rating of less than 80 km/h, or to tyres used exclusively for non-racing purposes.
The regulations apply to:
new tyres;
rereaded tires;
second-hand tyres; and
second‐hand tyres, unless such tyres are imported from a non-EU country.
In addition, the regulation sets out the responsibilities of tyre suppliers, importers and distributors of tyres, as well as of the European Commission, which has the power to adopt acts in accordance with Article 290 TFEU (the Treaty on European Union’s Framework Decision on the Functioning of the EU).
It also lays down the obligations of tyre manufacturers and importers to ensure that:
the tyre label is clearly visible on the vehicle;
the information on the tyre is clearly legible and legible;
inform the public of the fuel efficiency class, the wet grip class and the tyre fatigue class, and
instructions are clearly visible;
information on the tyres’ characteristics, such as the tyre type and type of use, is clearly indicated on the label;
all tyres are marked with the manufacturer's name, registered trade name and registered trade mark, and the postal address of the importer or importer;
any other information on a tyre is written in a language easily understood by the public.
EU countries must ensure that the regulation is implemented in their national legislation and that it does not impede the free movement of goods and persons within the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLYAPPLY?

Summary 72:
Funding for the modernisation of the EU’s external borders to fight fraud and serious crime: EU-wide programme for cooperation in the field of customs (EU-2020/2027) (see summary)
Finance for modernising the internal market for customs: the EU-2020-2027 Programme for cooperation
The European Union (EU) 2021/444 establishes a European Union-wide framework for cooperation for the purchase, maintenance and upgrading of customs control equipment. It aims to protect the EU and its Member States’ financial and economic interests while ensuring the availability of modern and reliable control equipment at the external borders.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/856 of 20 July 2019 establishing a European framework for the procurement, maintenance, upgrading and use of customs-related equipment for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the objectives and measures to be taken by the EU in order to improve the effectiveness and efficiency of customs controls.
The aim is to:
increase the efficiency and effectiveness of customs inspections;
improve the effectiveness of the detection of fraud;
ensure the protection of human health and safety;
fight against serious crime.
KEY POINTS
The objectives of the programme are to support:
the development of new customs control technology;
the use of innovative detection technology; and
the harmonisation of customs procedures.
To achieve these objectives, the EU countries must:
implement the objectives set out in Annex I of the regulation;
inform the European Commission and the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the measures they intend to take;
support the joint procurement and joint testing by two or more EU countries;
promote cooperation between the customs union and customs authorities.
Eligible actions
EU countries are required to take the necessary measures to implement the objectives of this regulation, including:
promoting cooperation between their customs authorities;
encouraging the use of new technologies;
providing the Commission with a list of equipment that may be used to achieve the objectives;
establishing an evaluation committee to report on the progress towards the achievement of the objectives.
In order to respond appropriately to evolving policy priorities, threats and technologies, the Commission has the power to adopt acts in accordance with Article 290 TFEU in respect of amending the indicative list of goods and equipment to be used for the customs control purposes and the list of indicators to measure the achievement.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to modernise its internal market, in particular:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 596/2013, which establishes the European Union Customs Code (the ‘Customs Code’);
Regulation (EU), which sets up the Customs programme (the 'Customs programme’) to support the EU Customs Union and the customs authorities (the Customs programme);
Decision (CFSP) 2017/849, which sets out a framework for EU-2019/2028 for the establishment of a European Customs Information System (ECIS);
Commission Decision (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Funding the EU's external borders’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 73:
Electricity price harmonisation and transparency in the electricity market
Electricity temperature harmonisation
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on electricity price harmonization and transparency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to improve the transparency and efficiency of electricity markets in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The directive sets out rules on:
the transmission system operator’s (TSO) role and responsibilities;
the role of the regulatory authority;
public participation in the development of the system;
market participants’ rights and obligations.
It also aims to ensure that electricity consumers are better informed about the benefits and potential price risks of dynamic electricity price contracts (DPCs).
It does not apply to:
transmission system operators (TSOs);
system operators*;
system users;
energy storage* and demand response* services;
distributed energy resources*; and
other energy-related services.
Scope
The legislation applies to all EU countries except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1), Ireland and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA).
Regulation (EU) 2019/1788 amends the legislation to take account of the fact that the European Commission has adopted a new law on energy efficiency and transparency.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The original directive, which entered into force on 1 January 2014, had to be amended by 1 February 2020. The new rules had to apply by 31 December 2018.
For more information, see:
‘Electricity Price Harmonisation and Transparency in the Energy Market (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: the body in charge of the transmission system of electricity in an EU country.
* System operator: a natural or legal person responsible for the operation of the EU electricity system and for the transmission of electricity to the consumer.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  (EU Directives 2014/62/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2014 on electricity market harmonisation (recast) and repealing Directive 2004/36/EC (OJ L 347, 31.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Directive 2014/61/EU have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 74:
EuroHPC Joint Undertaking for supercomputing and data infrastructure — building a federated and interconnected supercomputer and infrastructure in Europe (until 2023)
EuroHPS Joint undertaking for sub-exascale and petascale supercomputers (until 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1488 — EuroHPC joint undertaking for the construction and operation of federated/infrastructural infrastructure in the field of high-performance computing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a joint undertaking under Horizon 2020, the European Union’s (EU), to provide and manage access to federated* and interconnecting* high performance computing (HPC) infrastructure and its services.
It aims to support the development of an integrated and interconnected HPC ecosystem in Europe.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following projects:
the creation of a European supercomputer joint undertaking (Euro-HPCJUB)* and the acquisition and ownership of quantum computers and quantum simulators;
the establishment of a hosting consortium (HUB) to acquire and own the mid-range and high-precursor precursors to exascale (i.e. high-powered computers) and the building of an infrastructure to support their use;
acquisition and ownership by the HUB and the establishment of the hosting consortium.
Objectives
The HUBJUB aims to:
provide access to the HPC infrastructure to users of the participating participating countries to enable them to participate in the development and use of the pre-extended and ex-ascale* high-performing supercomparallelic computing systems;
contribute to the creation and development of the infrastructure to enable users to benefit from the benefits of the access to these systems, such as:
access to the infrastructure for research and development;
facilities and services for training facilities and skills development opportunities.
Cooperation with the Partnership for Advanced Computing in Europe
The JUB will be able to cooperate with the partnership for advanced computing (PRACE) for providing and managing access to a federation-wide and inter-connecting supercomputer infrastructure, as well as with PRACE for the provision of services and training facilities.
Joint Undertaking
JUB will operate under the following conditions:
participating countries are required to grant the JUB access to their national supercomputer sites and to the participating countries the right to use the infrastructure, subject to certain conditions;
participation in the joint undertaking is based on the following criteria:
membership is open to all participating countries, with the exception of Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
The participating countries are responsible for:
contributing to the total cost of the acquisition, ownership and management of the high-proprietary systems and infrastructure;
providing the hosting entity with the necessary financial resources to acquire the high and mid-performance systems and the infrastructure necessary to operate them;
cooperating with the PRACE to develop the infrastructure and to identify priorities for collaborative research;
promoting the use of advanced computing in Europe, in particular in the areas of:
research and innovation;
supporting the creation of an inter-disciplinary supercomputer network;
developing and managing the infrastructure in line with the objectives of Horizon 2020;
encouraging and facilitating the sharing of best practice and best practice in the area of advanced research.
Functioning of the joint undertaking
The Governing Board is composed of a Research and Scientific Advisory Board and an Infrastructure Advisory Group. It is chaired by the Executive Director and comprises representatives of participating countries. It adopts a list of decisions adopted by its members, and is assisted by a Scientific and Technical Advisory Board.
The Joint Board is responsible for the implementation of the regulation, including:
approving the work programme for a given year;
adopting the annual work programme;
drawing up, adopting, adopting and implementing the work programmes for the following years;
setting up and adopting the annual working programme for the period from 1 January to 31 December;
drafting and adopting implementing acts and implementing implementing acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Horizon 2020 — Joint undertaking (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Supercomputer: a supercomputer or supercomputer-related infrastructure.
* HPC: a system or system-based infrastructure that supports the development, use and use-making of high performance computers.

Summary 75:
Climate-neutrality objective for the EU by 2040: 2020-2050 (2020-2040)
Climate- neutrality objective: 2020–2040 (2020–2050)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1119 — EU-wide climate target for 2040
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s long-term objective to achieve a climate-neutral* economy by 2020.
KEY POINTS
The objective is to achieve the climate neutrality* objective by 2020, which is to be achieved by 2050.
To achieve this objective, the EU is committed to stepping up its efforts to tackle climate change and to deliver on the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’).
The EU is also committed to tackling climate change in a cost-effective, equitable and sustainable way.
The regulation sets out how the EU will achieve the objective and how it will adapt to climate change, taking into account different national circumstances in terms of starting points.
In 2020, the Commission will set out a common framework for the development of the EU's climate policy. This framework will include:
a common approach to climate and energy policy;
the establishment of an Advisory Board to advise the Commission on climate policy; and
the creation of a Climate Neutrality Advisory Board, which will support the Commission in its climate policy development.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMEMBER DOCUMENT APPLACE INTO FORCE
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
 The regulation has been amended several times.
For more information, see:
‘Climate neutrality’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Climate neutrality: the objective of achieving a climate neutral economy by 2050 by which the EU aims to reduce its greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by at least 30 % below 1990 levels compared to 1990 levels. This is achieved by investing in a low-carbon economy and reducing the emissions of GHG emissions from energy, transport, agriculture, forestry and fisheries, as well as in energy efficiency, energy efficiency and energy efficiency in buildings, buildings and infrastructure.

Summary 76:
Health and well-being: EU4Health programme
Health and Well-being : EU4health programme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 — EU4H programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the general principles and guidelines for the implementation of the EU’s Health and Wellbeing Programme (EU4H).
KEY POINTS
The programme aims to improve the quality and safety of human health in the European Union (EU), in particular by:
improving access to and access to quality health care;
reducing the risk of communicable diseases;
promoting access to affordable, high-quality, safe and accessible health care in the EU;
ensuring that all EU citizens have access to safe and quality healthcare;
providing a high level of health protection to all citizens, irrespective of their nationality, religion, ethnic origin, gender, age, disability or sexual orientation.
It also aims to:
improve the quality of health care and the availability of health services;
improve access to health care for all citizens;
contribute to the prevention, control and eradication of preventable and curable diseases and to the development of effective preventive health care systems;
strengthen cooperation between EU countries in the field of health and social protection;
increase the participation of the private sector in the health sector;
encourage the participation and participation of women in the decision-making process;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice;
raise awareness of the importance of the role of the European Commission in health policymaking.
The regulation also lays down the following objectives:
to:
promote access to high quality, safe, accessible and affordable health care, including access to preventive healthcare for all;
support the development and implementation of quality health services, including through the creation of a European Health Information System (EHIS);
improve health promotion and dissemination of information on health and safety;
fight against the spread of infectious diseases, such as HIV/AIDS, tuberculosis, hepatitis C, sexually transmitted diseases, and the common cold;
establish a European health information system (EHS);
ensure the protection of children, adolescents and vulnerable groups;
protect the right to life and health of the elderly, vulnerable groups and persons with disabilities.
To achieve these objectives, the programme is funded by the general budget of the Union.
EU4Health is implemented by the Commission, which is assisted by a Commission Advisory Committee. The Commission is responsible for the overall management of the programme and for its implementation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 			
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to implement the programme.
For more information, see:
‘Health and wellbeing’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Health and wellbeing: a term used in the Treaty on European Union to describe the quality, safety and effectiveness of all EU policies and activities.
* Human health: the health of people, including their health, safety, security and wellbeing.

Successive amendments to Regulation (EC), No 851/2019 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

European institute of innovation and technology
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/695 — strategy for a more innovative Europe
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EIT’s strategic objectives for the period from 2021 to 2027.
It also sets out its key actions and the priorities for this period.
KEY POINTS
The EIT aims to:
strengthen innovation ecosystems by continuing to support existing knowledge-based research centres (KICs) in addressing global challenges through the integration of the knowledge triangle at EU, national, regional and local level;
promote the sharing of best practices and best practices between KICs;
contribute to the development of knowledge and innovation-based jobs and growth in Europe;
support research and innovation in the areas of creativity, cultural and linguistic diversity, European identity and cohesion, European employment, economic resilience and smart growth, and Europe as a global actor.
The strategy is based on four pillars:
1.
Strengthening innovation ecosystems and relevant actors of the know-how triangle (i.e. for education and training, research, innovation and entrepreneurship) on a broader scale.
2.
Enhancing participation and strengthening the European Research Area (ERA) in the ‘Widening Participation and Strengthening the EU Research Area’ part of Horizon Europe, including the teaming and twinning activities and the outreach activities that it supports.
3.
Encouraging the participation of non-EU countries in the ERAIN-funded research and development projects.
4.
Supporting the development and growth of knowledge ecosystems in Europe, particularly in the fields of creativity and cultural diversity.
5.
Promoting the participation and competitiveness of EU countries in research, education and innovation (R&I).
6.
Investing in research and education (R & I).
7.
Strategies and priorities
The SIA must set out the priority fields and the strategies for the seven-year EIT for each of these fields in accordance with:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 — setting up the European Innovation and Entrepreneurship Agency (EIA) (see summary);
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 6/2014 and (EC), No 7/2014 establishing the European Science and Innovation Foundation (ESIF) and (EU), as amended by Regulation (Euratom) 2019/856 (see below);
Decision (EU, EU) 2020/852 laying down the rules for the establishment of the EIA and the EIF;
Decisions (EU and EU countries) 2020-2023 setting up a European Innovation Network (see above);
Council Decision (EU/2020/1) setting up an Innovation Network for the European Union (See below).
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.

Summary 79:
Aromatised wine products — geographical indications and labelling of certain aromatised wines (Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011)
Aromated wine products: geographical indications
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2015/1169 on the definition, description, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of certain aromatic wine products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition and description of certain categories of aromatisation (i.e. the production of wine with aromatic characteristics) and the presentation of these products.
It aims to protect the geographical indications (GIs) of organic wines produced in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
wines produced in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 834/2007 (see summary);
wine-product cocktails (e.g. wine-product cocktail);
salt-based wines;
sour wine; and
wine with a fruity or fruity flavour.
Products covered by the regulation must comply with the following requirements:
the product must be produced in compliance with the regulation’s Annex I;
the production processes and methods of analysis must be carried out according to the rules laid down in Annex II;
a description of the products must be provided for in Annex III;
an indication of the geographical origin of the product is provided in Annex IV.
The product must bear the name of the producer or the place of origin and the country of origin indicated in Annex V.
An indication of origin must be given in Annex VI.
Labelling
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts in order to establish the conditions under which product specifications may include additional requirements to those referred to in the annexes to the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DELEGATION APPLACE?
   
Regulation(EU) 2016/1239 has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to modernise EU rules on organic wine production and marketing.
For more information, see:
‘Wines and spirits’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Aromatisation: the production and presentation of wine products with an aromatic character. Aromatic characteristics refer to the presence of aromatics in the wine, such as aromatics, aromatics and fruity aromatics. The term ‘Aromatic wine’ is used to describe wine with an aromatic character. It does not refer to a specific wine or a specific category of wine product.
ACT
Commission Delegation (EU), Euratom of 12 December 2015 on the interpretation, application and enforcement of Council Regulation (EEC, No 868/2011 of 28 November 2015 relating to geographical indications in the field of aromatic wines (OJ L 183, 1.1.2016, pp. 1-8)
