Summary 80:
Fishing season 2019-2020: landing obligation for certain species of fish and by-catches in the Baltic and the Faroe Islands (see summary)
Fishing period 2019-2021: laying down landing obligation and bycatch limits for certain fish and other species
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/2021 laying down the landing obligation (TAC) for certain fishing species in the Faroes, the Canary Islands, the Azores and the South Atlantic
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s landing obligation* and the by-catch limits* for certain fishery species in 2019 and 2020.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fishing for:
the following species:
Horse mackerel,
greater silver smelt,
pelagic fish,
coccod,
sharks,
whiting,
fish aggregating devices (FADs),
targets,
fisheries,
target species,
catch limits and
by-catch values.
It does not apply to certain fish stocks, such as:
cod and whiting;
the swordfish;
crocodile;
tarpon;
fish aggregation devices;
marine mammals and crustaceans;
molluscs;
spotted fish;
catches of certain species; and
marine species.
TACs are set out in the following consultation with the European Commission, the European Fisheries Management Organisation (EFMO) and the European Maritime and Fisheries Authority (Eurostat).
By-catcher values are based on the total allowable catches* (tACs) of the species and on the number of fishing vessels.
Limits
EU Member States may not exceed the TACs set out by the regulation for the following species, sub-zones and sub-Zones:
Coccod (precautionary TAC);
cropaceans (precutionary and indicative TAC)
great white shark (pre-convention TAC),
great blackfin tuna (preconvention CACs);
great blue shark (Pre-Convention Area C);
spat sea breams (Preconvention Area D); and
spaice (prevention Area E).
The EU Member States are responsible for ensuring that:
all fishing vessels are registered and registered with the Commission;
all vessels are licensed and registered in the Member States;
there is no transfer of fishing authorisations between Member States and the Commission is responsible for the management of the fishing season.
The Member States must take the necessary measures to ensure compliance with the regulation, including:
keeping a record of all catches;
ensuring that fishing vessels comply with the catch limits;
keeping an inventory of all the fish caught;
establishing a register of vessels registered in each Member State;
monitoring the compliance of vessels with the quotas;
taking the necessary steps to ensure that vessels do not overfishing;
notifying the Commission of any infringements of the regulation.
Specific rules apply to the following fish stocks.
Fisheries
The following species are excluded from the regulation:
carpaceans,
spirosa,
dolphinidae,
catfish,
sea breams,
mackerel and associated by-catch values;
great greyfin tuna,
the fish aggregating device,
common porbeagle,
tropical fish, and
cetaceans.
In addition, the regulation sets out:
limits for the catch of certain fish aggregated devices (CADs) in certain Member States’ territorial waters;
limits on the amount of fish caught by vessels in certain fishing areas;
limit on the quantity of fish taken by vessels using FADs;
a limit on the quantities of fish landed by vessels operating in certain parts of the sea.
Member States must ensure that the following measures are in place to ensure the conservation of the stocks of the fish in question:
prevention measures are taken to protect the stocks;
precautions are taken for the protection of the populations of fish species in question;
measures are taken in the event of a decline in the abundance or abundance of fish aggregates;
reciprocal measures are put in place for the conservation and management of fish stocks in the areas covered by this regulation;
targeted measures are adopted to protect fish stocks and to encourage the sustainable use of fishing gear and equipment.
Bycatch values
The European Commission may set maximum catch values for fish and/or bycatch values for specific species, based on:
total allowable catches,
total bycatch value,
bycatch values and
the number of vessels operating;
total catch values per tonne of fish, per species, per sub-zone, per

Summary 82:
Convention on the conservation of Atlantic Tunas: rules for the fishing of Mediterranean swordfish in recreational fisheries (until 2021) (Regulation (EU) 2021/2195)
Convention of the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tuna (ICCAT Convention)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 596/2010 — rules for fishing of the Mediterranean sword fish in recreational waters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure the conservation and sustainable management of the marine environment in the Mediterranean Sea by establishing minimum standards for fishing vessels and technical characteristics of fishing gear.
It also aims to protect the marine ecosystems of Atlantic tunas, a group of fish that includes swordfish, by establishing a common framework for the management of Mediterranean and Atlantic swordfish fishing.
KEY POINTS
  
The regulation applies to fishing vessels operating in the Atlantic Ocean and to recreational fishing vessels using harpoons or pelagic longlines. It does not apply to:
fishing effort;
landings and transshipments;
designated ports;
the number and type of vessels that can operate in an EU country’s exclusive economic zone (EEZ);
the type and number of vessels authorised to fish in an EEZ; and
the types of fishing effort.
Management measures
EU countries must:
ensure that:
the fishing effort is managed according to the minimum standards set out in the regulation;
fisheries are carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in Annex I to the regulation (see summary);
recipients of the fishing effort are informed of the measures to be taken;
all fishing gear and equipment used in the fisheries are inspected by qualified inspectors;
ships and transhipments are accompanied by a certificate of authorisation issued by the competent authority of the EU country in which the vessel is registered.
Inspections
Each year, EU countries must carry out an inspection of all fishing vessels in their EEZ and submit a report to the European Commission on their implementation of the regulation.
The report must include:
a description of the type and quantity of fish caught;
a list of the number and types of vessels and the number of persons on board;
information on the number, type and weight of swordfish caught by each vessel;
details of the types and quantities of fishing equipment used.
Monitoring and control measures
Each EU country is responsible for:
monitoring and controlling fishing effort and fishing gear;
ensuring that vessels comply with the management measures;
checking that equipment and equipment is in good working order;
carrying out regular inspections of fishing vessels;
keeping a register of all the fishing gear used;
taking the necessary measures to prevent, detect and report any infringements of the regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2019/2196 has applied since 1 January 2021.

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework for the EU’s financial stabilisation mechanism (MFF) (2014-2021)
Multiannational financial framework (MFS) for the European Union’ s financial stabilising mechanism (2014 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 establishing a multiannual framework to provide financial support for the stabilisation of the EU's financial system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the establishment of the European Financial Stabilisation Mechanism (EFSM).
It aims to ensure that the EFSM provides financial support to the EU in the event of a natural or man-made disaster.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out:
the duration of the MFF from 2014 to 2020;
the own resources ceiling for the period 2014-2020;
a technical adjustment for the years 2015-2020.
The MFF is funded by the EU countries’ contributions to the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The European Commission is responsible for the operation of the Fund and for its management.
It is financed by the EIB, the ESI Fund and the EAFRD, and is managed by the European Commission.
Eligibility
The ESI is open to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
Each year, the Commission determines the amount of funding to be made available to each EU country, subject to certain conditions, such as the following:
EUR 7 billion for 2014-2019: €7 billion;
€9 billion for 2020-2025: €10 billion for the same period;
Eur 10 billion for each of the following years: 2021-2027: €8 billion;2028-2029: €9 billion;2020-2031: €11 billion; and
2032-2033: €5 billion.
In exceptional cases, the European Parliament and the Council may decide to provide additional financial support, in particular in the case of natural disasters.
If the amount is not sufficient to cover the total amount of assistance provided by the Fund in the year of occurrence of the disaster, and if the Commission proposes that the difference be financed through the annual amounts available for the following year. The Commission may propose that the amount be financed by:
revenue to the Contingency Margin: the maximum amount that can be recovered by the Commission from any given year, in accordance with the own-resource ceiling;
recourse to the own resources margin: the amount that is not covered by the own resource ceiling, but which is calculated to be equivalent to the difference between the executed payments and the annual MFF payment ceiling of the year n-1.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
The financial stability mechanism was set up in 2010.
For more information, see:
‘The European Financial Stability Mechanism’ on the Commission's website.

* Multiannual Financial Framework: a financial framework set up for a period of 7 years by the Council and the Commission. It is designed to provide the EU with the financial resources it needs to prepare and implement medium-term investments.

Fisheries and aquaculture in the outermost regions of the ocean: EU funding for the sustainable management of fisheries
Fishers and aqua-fisheries in the outsidemost regions: European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFAF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 laying down the priorities and specific rules for funding the European Mediterranean and Faroe Islands Fisheries Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EMFAF’s priorities, its budget and rules for providing EU funding, complementing the general rules applicable to the EU Fisheries Fund.
KEY POINTS
Funding
The regulation sets out:
the priorities and the specific rules to be followed by the EU to support the EU-led fisheries sector;
the budget and the eligibility of entities and activities covered by this regulation;
a list of eligible entities, activities and costs to be supported;
specific rules for the allocation of EU funding under shared management.
The EMFA Fund is funded by the European Union (EU), the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Fund (ESF).
It is managed by the Commission, which is assisted by the Council of the EU. It is responsible for:
monitoring and evaluating the implementation of its activities;
providing the Commission with the results and output indicators set out in Annex I of the regulation; and
assessing the impact of its actions on the EU fisheries sector.
Eligible entities
The following entities are eligible for funding under this regulation:
fishing vessels, including:
small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs);

fishery-fishing-related businesses;
operators of fishing vessels;

individuals and groups of individuals;
agriculture-related entities;
non-governmental organisations;
professional associations;
research and development bodies;
specialised research institutes;
scientific and technical organisations.
Support
The EU supports the following activities:
promoting sustainable management and sustainable use of the marine environment;
protecting marine ecosystems;
improving marine ecosystems and marine biodiversity;
encouraging the sustainable development of marine ecosystems, including the protection of marine protected areas;
contributing to the conservation and management of marine life;
ensuring the sustainable exploitation of marine resources;
supporting research and development in the field of marine environment and marine environment policy;
facilitating the exchange of information and best practice in marine environment management;
developing and disseminating scientific and technical information on marine environment policies and programmes;
exchanging information and expertise;
sharing best practice and best practices in marine environmental protection;
cooperating with non-EU countries;
participating in the management of the fisheries sector, in particular through the participation of EU countries.
Budget
EU funding is divided into 2 categories:
Direct management:
€1.5 billion for direct management, which covers:
financing the activities of EU Member States;
spreading the financial support between the Member States under direct and indirect management. The Member States are responsible for determining the amount of the direct management budget.
Indirect management: funding is shared between the EU and the EU countries for the following:
support for the establishment, management and operation of the European Marine Environment Facility (EMEF);
contribution to the establishment of a European Fisheries Facility (EFF);
support to the development of a marine protected area (MPA);
assistance for research and innovation;
assessment of the impact on marine ecosystems.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS

Convention on the International Trade in Tuna and Blackfin tuna (CITTO) — EU fishing vessels and the International Association for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (IATTC) (until 31 December 2020)
Conservation and management of marine species (until the end of 2023)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 520/2007 on the EU fishing vessel register and the Convention relating to the International Agreement on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fish Species
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing of bigeye tuna and other marine species by establishing a Community system to prevent and deter such fishing.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to Union fishing vessels used or intended for use for the purposes of fishing in the area covered by the Convention.
It does not apply to:
fishing vessels flying the flag of a non-EU country;
non-EU fishing vessels using or intended to use for fishing in an EU country’s territorial waters;
unmanned fishing vessels;
ships used by non-Union fishing vessels under the authority of a country other than the EU country.
EU countries must ensure that:
all fishing vessels are registered in accordance with the regulation;
the vessel registry is kept up to date and up-to-date;
there is a register of all fishing vessels registered in the EU countries;
fisheries carried out under the regulation (Regional Vessel Register (RVR) is maintained by EU countries.
The register is updated by the European Commission every 5 years.
Monitoring of fishing activities
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring the fishing activities of EU countries’ fishing vessels.
Observers
The Commission appoints one or more observers per EU country to monitor the activities of the IATTC observer programme.
Each year, the Commission assesses and, where applicable, forwards a request for an observer to participate in a fishing operation to the Commission. If the Commission considers that an observer is not required, it informs the Commission of the reasons for the decision.
If an observer withdraws from the programme due to a lack of satisfactory information, it must inform the Commission within 30 days of receiving the request. The Commission must assess and, if applicable, forward the request for reconsideration of the request to the IACT Secretariat for consideration by 1 month after the cessation of the cause of the force majeure.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2009.

Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiative register — information exchange between the Commission and EU countries on legal acts and citizens’ initiatives (CIRCA)
Citizens' initiative register – information exchange among the Commission, EU countries and citizens
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — information sharing between the European Commission and the European citizens‘ initiative register
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a system for the exchange of information between the EU countries’ competent authorities and the organisers of citizen initiatives* on the legal acts adopted by the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The aim of the regulation is to improve the transparency of the EU’s legal acts.
It allows citizens to directly approach the Commission with a request inviting it to submit a proposal for a legal act of the Union for the purpose of implementing the Treaties similar to the right conferred on the European Parliament under Article 225 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Community (TFEU) and on the Council under Article 241 TFEU.
Organisations of initiatives are required to collect statements of support from EU citizens. These must be submitted in paper or electronic form and must be sent to the competent authorities in the EU country in which the initiative is being organised.
The organisers of initiatives must ensure that the information they collect is accurate, complete and complete. They must also ensure that they respect the confidentiality of personal data and that they do not disclose personal data, such as names, addresses and contact details.
In addition, the organisers must respect the right of the Commission to keep a register of all the information provided by the signatories to the initiative.
Registration of information
The register is maintained by the Commission. It is open to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
The Commission is responsible for the operation of the register and for ensuring that it is up-to-date and accurate.
Information exchange between EU countries
EU countries are responsible for:
ensuring that the register is up to date and accurate;
keeping a record of the number of signatories and of the countries in which they are located;
establishing a point of contact which provides information and assistance to the organisers;
providing the organisers with the necessary information to verify the validity and verifications of the statements they have received;
assessing the suitability of the online collection systems for the purposes of this regulation;
checking that the organisers are complying with the requirements of the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?

It has applied since 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Citizen initiative: an initiative by an individual or group of individuals or groups of individuals, organisations or groups to obtain information from the Commission or the EU institutions.
* Legal act: an act adopted by an EU country to implement a Treaty, Convention or other legal instrument of the Community.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) No 550/2004 of 12 December 2004 on the information exchange system between the Community and its Member States on the implementation of legal acts (OJ L 183, 1.1.2005, pp. 1-8)

Summary 88:
Pesticides and plant growth regulators: statistics on the use of pesticides in the EU
Pesticide pesticides and their use in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1107/2009 — statistics on pesticide use
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of data on the EU’s statistics on pesticides used in the production and use of plant protection products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to pesticides used for plant protection.
It does not apply to:
pesticides intended for the production or use of food or feedstuffs;
plant protection products intended for use on food or foodstuffs other than food or animal feed;
other products intended to protect plants against disease or pests.
The scope of the regulation is limited to pesticides which are:
plant growth regulators* (PGR1, PGR2 and PGR3);
other plant-growth regulators*;
phytosanitary agents* (e.g. pesticides used to prevent, control or reduce the growth of certain plant diseases and pests);
plant-protection products*; and
molluscicides.
Data collection
EU countries must collect and transmit data on:
the total number of pesticides applied to each selected crop;
the number and types of pesticides used;
a description of the types and quantities of each type of pesticide used.
These data must be collected and transmitted to the European Commission (Eurostat).
Eurostat, the European statistical authority, is responsible for the transmission of the data to Eurostat and for the preparation and dissemination of the results of the statistical analyses.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
From 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts in the field of statistics, including this regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Use of pesticides’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Pesticides: any substance used to control, prevent, reduce or control plant diseases or pests, such as nematodes, parasitic and phytoplanktonic diseases, parasitic protozoa, and nematoms.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1079/2009 of 21 October 2009 concerning the placing of pesticides on the market and the statistical collection, transmission and processing of data relating to their use (recast) (OJ L 183, 28.10.2009, pp. 1-8)

Summary 89:
Protection of human and animal health and the environment from the adverse effects of genetically modified organisms (GMOs) in plants and plant products (Regulation (EC) No 596/2004)
Protection against the adverse effect of genetically modifying organisms (GMIs) in plant and plant-based products
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/849 — plant protection products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities are able to authorise and monitor the safe use of products intended for human or animal health or the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
plant protection products* and active substances* intended for use in or on plants or plant products, edible animal products, drinking water or elsewhere in the environment and resulting from the use of a plant protection product, including their metabolites, breakdown or reaction products;
safeners* and synergists* intended to protect the health and safety of humans, animals or plants.
It does not apply to products intended to be used for the production, storage, transport, storage or use of food or feed.
Authorisations are granted by EU countries on the basis of harmonised criteria.
Approval
EU countries must ensure that:
the product meets the following criteria:
it meets the essential safety requirements;
it is safe for human, animal or plant health;
there is no risk to the environment;
the substance has neither an immediate or delayed harmful effect on human health nor an unacceptable effect on the environment, nor is it likely to have an adverse effect on animal health.
If an EU country considers that the product meets all of these criteria, it grants an authorisation.
The application for approval is submitted to the competent authority of the EU country in which the product is to be placed on the market. The competent authority is assisted by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and the European Medicines Agency (EMSA).
The competent authority examines the application and decides whether to grant or reject the application. If the application is rejected, it must inform the applicant of the reasons and the reasons for its decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO ENTRY INTO FORCE?
It entered into force on 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into their national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The legislation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the safety of food and feed in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Plant protection products’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Genetically modified organisms: organisms in which genetic material has been altered within the meaning of Article 2(2) of Directive 2001/18/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 12 March 2001 on the deliberate release into the environment (see summary).
* Active substance: any substance that has been genetically modified and which has the potential to have a potentially harmful effect.
ACT
  барканска гранка: a substance that is present in, on or in contact with food or foodstuffs, and which is likely to present a risk to human health, the environment or the health of animals, plants or animal products.

Summary 90:
Interoperability Information Management Information System (IMI) (until 31 December 2020) (Regulation (EU) 2020/2195) (see summary)
Integrated information management information system (IIMI)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 2195/2013 on the establishment of the Information Information Management System (IMI)*
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
  
It sets out the rules for the establishment and operation of the information management system (i.e. IMI)* in the European Union (EU).
It aims to facilitate the exchange of information between EU countries and non-EU countries in order to improve the functioning of the EU’s internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the use of IMI by EU countries’ competent authorities to:
facilitate the exchange and sharing of information on EU citizens’ movements;
ensure the protection of their personal data;
improve the efficiency and security of the internal market; and
improve consumer protection.
It does not apply to the processing of personal data by the EU countries themselves.
Implementation
Each EU country is responsible for setting up a national IMI coordinator.
The national coordinators are responsible for:
registering IMI users;
designating a central point of contact for each IMI user;
establishing and maintaining an IMI network;
coordinating the information exchange between IMI coordinators;
monitoring the functioning;
providing technical support for IMI;
assisting the Commission in implementing the regulation.
Participation of the Commission
The Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of EU countries, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EASC) and the Committee on the Functioning of the European System of Central Banks (COSCB).
The European Commission may take part in the decision-making process of the IMI Coordinators.
Rights and obligations of data subjects
Each competent authority must ensure that data subjects can exercise their rights in accordance with the relevant IMI rules, in cooperation with the Commission.
Data subjects have the right to have their data processed and to be informed of the reasons for processing their data, including:
the reasons for the processing;
the nature and content of the processing,
the purpose for processing the data, and
the conditions for their access to the information;
their rights as data subjects, including the right of access to information and to make a request for information.
EU countries must also ensure that:
data subjects have access to their own data processing activities performed by the competent authority under their authority;
they have the rights to access the information they are entitled to receive and to communicate with each other;
information on their rights and obligations is made available to them by means of electronic means;
personal data are stored and processed only where they are necessary to carry out the tasks of the competent authorities;
data subject rights are respected;
participation in IMI is subject to the same conditions as in other EU data protection legislation.
National use
National authorities may use IMI for the following purposes:
exchange of information and information exchange with other EU countries;
exchanges of information, including information on persons and organisations;
transmission of information to the Commission;
implementation of the regulation;
processing of data by IMI actors;
sharing of information with other competent authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
The IMI regulation is part of the implementation of the ‘SOLVIT’ initiative, which aims to modernise the EU internal market by establishing a single market information system.
For more information, see:
‘Integrated Information Management Systems’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Information management system: a system that allows users to exchange information on their movement, movement of goods and services and the conditions under which such information can be exchanged. It is based on a set of principles, known as the SOLVIT principles, which are set out in the Commission Recommendation of 7 December 2001 on principles for using the Internal Market Problem Solving Network (5): Chapters I and II.
ACT
Commission Decision 2013/856/EU of 12 December 2013 laying down the legal basis for the creation of an Information Management Society (IMSI) (OJ L 165, 14.12.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Decision (EU, Euratom) 2015/939 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 91:
Fishing in the Mediterranean and Black Sea: common dolphinfish fishery (GFCM Agreement)
Fisheries in the greater Mediterranean and the Black Sea (GMC Agreement) — common fishing fishery
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on the common fishing agreement between the EU and its Member States and the countries participating in the GFCM agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the rules for fishing in the Great Coasts of the Mediterranean (the Mediterranean, Black Sea and connecting waters) in order to ensure the sustainable management of the common fishery in this area.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels operating in the area covered by the agreement.
It does not apply to:
fishing vessels that are not covered by this regulation;
fisheries that are engaged in commercial fishing activities outside the agreement’s scope;
vessels used exclusively for recreational purposes.
Authorisations
Fishing vessels authorised to participate in the agreement are granted a fishing authorisation in accordance with Article 7 of Regulation (EU) No 596/2013 and are included in a list containing the name of the vessel and its EU fleet register number, which the EU country concerned must provide to the Commission.
Register of authorised vessels
The Commission is responsible for maintaining a register of all EU-registered fishing vessels.
The register contains the following information:
the name and registered registration number of vessels;
the number of fishing vessels in the vessel is authorised to take part in the fishery;
a list of the countries which have been granted an authorisation and the country in which the fishing vessel is registered
the country of registration
the authorisation number is entered in the register of EU registered fishing vessels and the number of authorised fishing vessels is entered into the register.
Registration of vessels
Vessels registered in the EU are subject to the same rules as those laid down in the common fisheries agreement (CFA Agreement), except for the following exceptions:
small-scale vessels with engine less than 6 metres in length (LOA) with engine.
Small-scale small-scale fishing vessels (LOAs) are not subject to registration and are not required to keep a register;
LOAs that are registered in a non-EU country are covered by a separate regulation, Regulation (Euratom) No 696/2014.
Monitoring
EU countries must take the necessary measures to prohibit fishing, retention on board, transhipment and landing of fish and shellfish caught in the territory of the agreement if they suspect that it is being illegally fished. They must inform the Commission and the International Commission for the Prohibition of Unlawful Trade in Migratory Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (ICPNF) of any such fishing activity.
EU country authorities must ensure that vessels carrying out fishing activities in their territory comply with the rules laid down by the regulation.
If a vessel is found to be fishing in violation of the regulation, it is subject to a prior authorisation issued by the competent authority of an EU country. If the vessel does not comply with this regulation, the vessel may be subject to prior authorisations issued by a country other than that of the one in which it is registered.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts are adopted by the Commission on the basis of a proposal from the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee. The Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts, subject to certain conditions.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Common fisheries agreement: a multilateral agreement which aims to promote the development, conservation, rational management and best utilisation of living marine resources in Mediterranean and coastal waters. It is based on the principle of sustainable use of marine resources and is designed to ensure that fish stocks are maintained at levels which are considered sustainable and at low risk of collapse.
* GMC Agreement: an international agreement signed between the European Community (now the EU) and the governments of the Member States, the European Free Trade Association (EFTA), the World Trade Organization (WTO), the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and non-member countries of the Gulf Cooperation Council (GCC) to conserve, conserve and manage the marine environment and the marine ecosystems of the Greater Coasts and Black Seas.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ебранска гланка  гарка, гълганна   барна карака and годанца (OJ L 183, 1.7.2009, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments



Summary 92:
Uniform patent protection within the EU
Uniform application of patents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2012 — creating a European patent with unitary effect
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that patents granted by the European Patent Office (EPO) are applied uniformly throughout the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to patents granted in accordance with Article 143 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European System of Patents (TFTP) (see summary).
It does not apply to:
patents granted by national courts;
pending patent applications;
patent applications that have not yet been granted; and
possessions of intellectual property rights.
It is the first step towards the creation of unitary patent protection in the EU.
Eligibility for EU countries
EU countries are free to decide whether they wish to participate in the implementation of the regulation or not.
The European Commission is assisted by a Select Committee, which is composed of representatives of the participating EU countries and chaired by the High Representative of the Union for Industry, Entrepreneurship and SMEs. The committee is responsible for examining the application for the protection of the patent and assessing the suitability of the application. It is also responsible for the evaluation of the validity of the protection granted under the regulation.
Beneficiaries
The patent holder is the holder of a patent, i.e. a company or individual, which has the intellectual property right to use the patent. The patent in question is protected by a single European patent.
Application for protection
The application for protection is made by an individual or a group of individuals, companies or groups of companies. The application is submitted to the EPO for protection by a person or a collective of individuals or groups. The applicant may apply for protection in one or more EU countries at the same time.
Protection is granted for a period of 10 years from the date on which the application is made. The period of validity is extended for a further 10 years if the applicant wishes to extend the period of protection for a longer period of time. The duration of the extended period is determined by the Commission’s Decision 2014/856/JHA.
Licensing
The protection granted to a patent holder in one EU country does not affect the rights granted to the holder in another EU country. However, it does affect the right of the holder to use a patent in another country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
 * European patent: a patent granted by a national patent office to a company with the aim of ensuring the proper functioning of that patent, consistency of case-law and hence legal certainty, and cost-effectiveness for patent proprietors.
* Unitary patent: the patent is granted by an EPO to a person, company or group of companies with the objective of ensuring that the patent has the same legal effect throughout the EU, irrespective of the EU country in which it is granted.
ACT
Council Regulation (EPC) No 550/2012 of 11 December 2012 laying down the general principles and procedures relating to the creation, application and enforcement of the unitary European patent (OJ L 157, 12.12.2012, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 550/12 have been incorporated into the basic text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 93:
Ensuring the enforcement of intellectual property rights in the internal market: European Observatory for the Protection of Intellectual Property Rights (OJ L 195, 2.7.2014, pp. 1-2)
Ensuing the enforcement and enforcement of Intellectual property rights: European Organisation for the Prohibition of Transnational Organised Crime (OLPC)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 207/2009 establishing the European Observatory on the Protection and Enforcement of Intellectual Products and Services (OLPP)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s OPP, a centre of excellence on information and data relating to counterfeiting and piracy.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The OPP is to provide the European Commission with reliable and comparable data on counterfeiting, piracy and the illegal trade in counterfeit goods and services in the EU.
The tasks of the OPP are to:
collect, analyse and disseminate information on infringements of copyright, trademarks, patents, designations, designs, designs and trademarks;
collect and analyse data on the legal status of copyright and trademark applications;
provide the Commission with the information it needs to carry out its tasks;
assist the EU countries in their enforcement and reporting of infringements;
cooperate with the national intellectual property offices in order to share information and best practice.
Tasks
To fulfil its tasks, OLPP is responsible for collecting, analysing and disseminating information on:
counterfeasants’ activities;
illegal trade in counterfeiting* and piracy*;
the illegal trade of counterfeit products and services; and
the illicit trade in intellectual property.
It is also responsible for:
monitoring and analysing counterfeiting;
checking that counterfeiting is being detected;
identifying and prosecuting counterfeiting offences;
sharing information with other EU countries and with the Commission;
carrying out the tasks assigned to it by this regulation;
coordinating with the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAFRIC) and the European Centre for the Suppression of Counterfeiting and Piracy (ECAC);
collecting and sharing information on the activities of the national Intellectual Property Office (IPO);
assisting the Commission in its work with the OLPP;
organising meetings of the Observatory;
preparing a management report for the European Parliament, the Council, the Commission and the Administrative Board of the European Union (Europa).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 March 2010 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 March 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect intellectual property (IP) rights, including:
Directive 2004/48/EC on intellectual property protection;
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2015/849 on the protection of intellectual assets and their management;
Decision 2014/856/EU on the establishment of OLPC.
For more information, see:
‘Enhancing the enforcement & enforcement of IP rights’ on the OLPC's website.

Summary 94:
International trade in services: protection of the EU’s rights under international trade agreements (trade in services) (Regulation (EU) 2019/1369)
International trade on services: protecting the EU's rights under International trade agreements
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 1369/2013 on the protection of EU rights following dispute settlement in the area of international trade agreement in the field of services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure the effective exercise of the Union's rights to protect its economic interests in international trade by ensuring that the EU is able to exercise its rights under trade agreements concluded with non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
international trade agreements to which the EU has committed itself to applying in its trade with third countries by virtue of its participation in the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organization (WTO);
trade agreements between the EU and non-European Economic Area (EEA) countries;
trade in goods and services between EU countries and the EU as a whole.
It does not apply to bilateral agreements concluded by the EU.
The EU has the right to suspend, modify or repeal any trade concessions or other obligations that it has concluded with a third country or a separate customs territory.
EU countries must ensure the application within their respective territories of the relevant agreements.
In the case of trade in goods, the EU countries must:
ensure that:
the EU's action is aimed at ensuring the maintenance of a substantially equivalent level of concessions, as laid down in the relevant international agreements;
the level of nullification or impairment of the benefits accruing to the EU under the agreement is reduced to a level equivalent to the level set out in the agreement;
a dispute settlement mechanism is in place to find a positive solution to any disputes arising from the dispute.
Where the EU suspends or repeals a trade concession or other obligation, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for its decision. The Commission must take the input received into account.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts under this regulation.
For more information, see:
Trade in services (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* International trade agreement: a trade agreement between a country, a customs territory or a group of countries, including regional or bilateral agreements, which aims to prevent, restrict or restrict imports of goods or services.
* Dispute settlement mechanism: an international dispute resolution mechanism set up by the WTO to settle disputes between the parties to an international agreement.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?

Summary 96:
Creditors’ rights in cases of over-preservation of bank accounts in another EU country — preservation order (OPPO) (European Union)
Crediting parties in civil and commercial matters must be able to obtain a ‘preservation order’ in the event that their bank account in one EU country has been preserved by a creditor in another.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 596/2006 of 25 June 2006 on the application for a preservation order in civil matters in the Member States
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The regulation allows a creditor to obtain an ‘OPPO’ (in personam order) in the case of a bank account which has been overpreserved by a debtor.
KEY POINTS
The creditor is required to notify the creditor of any overpreservation in the bank account of the amount of the preserved funds. The creditor must provide the creditor with all the necessary information, including:
a copy of the original bank account;
a list of all the banks concerned;
the name and address of the creditor and the creditor’s representative in the other EU country;
documents relating to the preservation order;
an explanation of the reasons for the preservation of the funds;
proof that the bank is solvent and that the funds are not being used for the purpose for which the order has been granted;
evidence of the existence of the bank and the identity of the person or bodies responsible for its preservation.
The order is valid for 30 days from the date of entry into force.
If the creditor is unable to provide all the information required to obtain the order, he/she must submit a request for the release to the competent authority of the EU country of enforcement. The request must contain the following information:
the bank account number and the name of the persons or companies responsible for preserving the funds,
the date and place of establishment of the account and the number of persons responsible for maintaining that bank account,
a description of the nature and nature of the security and the reason for preserving it, and
the amount of money preserved.
Where the creditor does not comply with the above conditions, the creditor may apply for an “opPO” in the following cases:
where the creditor has not complied with the conditions laid down in the regulation:
implemented the conditions set out in the annex to the regulation;
obtained the order by means of an advisory procedure adopted by the European Court of Justice;
in the event the creditor fails to comply with these conditions, he or she has the right to apply for a court order in the same EU country.
In such cases, the court will issue its decision without delay once it has received the information provided by the creditor, provided that any security required has been provided by that creditor by that time.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation (EU) 2016/856 has applied since 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve judicial cooperation in cross-border civil matters.
For more information, see:
‘Cross-border judicial cooperation’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Preservation order: an order issued by a court to prevent or restrict the preservation or withdrawal of funds from a bank.

Summary 97:
European Border Surveillance System (Eurosur) — common rules for joint border surveillance and maritime operations (2014-2020) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 establishing the Eurosur border surveillance system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes common EU-wide rules for the joint operation, pilot project or rapid intervention of EU countries and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBSA) to prevent illegal border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for Eurosur, a joint surveillance system between the EU countries, the EBSA and the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR).
Eurosur is designed to prevent and combat illegal border crossing by means of joint operations, including:
interception, rescue at sea and disembarkation of suspected illegal immigrants and non-EU nationals;
the exchange of information between EU countries’ border surveillance units;
joint operation planning;
coordination with the International Coordination Centre (ICC) of the International Maritime Organization (IMO).
Participation in Eurosur
Each participating unit must include at least one person with basic first aid training.
Each EU country’s national border surveillance unit is responsible for:
collecting and reporting information about any vessel suspected of being engaged in illegal activities at sea, which are outside the scope of Eurosur;
carrying out joint operations;
operating in the contiguous zone of an EU country;
sharing information on the relevant jurisdiction and legislation in the geographical area where the operation takes place;
participating in a joint operation or pilot project, including references to EU and international law regarding interception, rescue, and rapid intervention;
ensuring that the operational plan is approved by the national authorities of the EU country in which the operation is taking place.
Participating units must:
ensure that:
the information they collect and transmit to the ICC is accurate and up-to-date;
identify the nationality of the vessel and its flag;
cooperate in the event of a vessel being found to be in danger of sinking;
notify the identity of the person or persons on board the vessel;
assess whether the vessel is capable of operating on the high seas;
use all available means of communication, including radio and satellite communication, to communicate with each other and with other EU countries;
take all necessary measures to ensure the safety and security of persons and property on board.
The measures must not exceed what is necessary to achieve the objectives of this regulation.
EU countries may not:
take measures to prevent, detect, search or rescue persons or vessels on board a vessel which is suspected of having committed an act of illegal immigration;
interfere with the activities of vessels operating in the territorial waters of a Member State that is not participating in the sea operation without the authorisation of that Member State.
If a vessel is found in danger, it must be brought to the attention of the competent authority of the Member State in which it is operating.
Interception on the High Seas
The following measures may be taken:
when:
information has been received indicating that the operating efficiency of a person or vessel is impaired, but not to the extent that a distress situation is likely;
a vessel is in danger;
an individual or a group of persons has been identified as being involved in an illegal immigration activity;
there is reason to believe that the vessel may be carrying out illegal immigration activities.
Rescue at sea
If:
a person or a vessel has been found in a danger, but is not in a state of distress;
it is likely that a vessel will not be able to operate in the area where it is located;
information on the identity and nationality of a persons or vessel on board has been obtained;
in the event that a person has been declared missing or is presumed to be missing or presumed to have been lost or presumed lost;
if the vessel has not been found.
In addition, the Member States may take measures to:
prevent, detect and rescue persons and vessels which are likely to be involved in illegal immigration or illegal migration activities;
conclude a search and rescue operation;
decide on the number of persons to be rescued;
arbitrate a vessel if it is likely to sink or to be lost or to become lost.
Coordination between the participating units
The participating units:
are responsible for the coordination and coordination of the operational plans;
are assisted by:
an Interpol representative;
representatives of the Agency, including the Coordinating Officer of the IMO, and
the representatives of the European Commission, the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy, the Commission and the Council.
FROM WHEN

Summary 100:
Protecting people and goods against transnational threats: enhanced actions in the fight against organised crime
Protecting citizens and goods from transnational risks: enhanced action in the field of crime
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 — enhanced actions at EU level to combat organised crime, corruption and money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities in order to combat transnational crime and corruption.
It sets up an enhanced action fund to support the work carried out by EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The Regulation sets out the eligibility criteria for actions to be supported under the enhanced action framework. Actions are eligible for funding under the Regulation.
The actions supported are those that:
contribute to the prevention, detection, investigation and prosecution of crime;
promote cooperation between law enforcement and judicial authorities;
improve cooperation between national law enforcement agencies;
support the training of law enforcement officials;
encourage the use of new technologies, transnational projects, networking and exchange of best practices, monitoring of the implementation of relevant EU law and Union policies and actions in relation to and in non-EU countries; and
support cooperation between the European External Action Service (EEAS) and the European Commission.
Eligible actions
The European Commission and the EU countries, together with the EAS, must ensure coordination as regards actions in and in the EU’s external action area. Actions supported under this Regulation are based on the priorities identified in the Regulation (i.e. in line with the General Programme on Security and Safeguarding Liberties, the Specific Programme ‘Prevention of and fight against crime’, and the specific programme for the period 2014-2020 (see summary).
The EAS and the Commission are responsible for:
coordinating the actions supported;
monitoring the implementation;
assessing the effectiveness of the actions;
providing emergency assistance in the event of an emergency;
exchanging best practice and best practice;
identifying areas of cooperation where cooperation needs to be strengthened.
In addition, the EEA and EU countries are required to provide the Commission with information on:
the types of actions eligible under the regulation;
the number of EU countries that are eligible to benefit from the funding;
a list of eligible actions in each EU country.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The fight against cross-border crime is one of the main challenges facing the European Union (EU). It is also essential to protect the economic and social interests of the EU and its citizens.
For more information, see:
‘Fight against crime: enhanced EU action’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Transnational crime: any illegal activity that takes place outside the EU.
ACT

Summary 101:
Non-sustainable fishing in the marine environment: EU action plan against non-EU countries
Non-Sustainable fishing within the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 — EU action to combat non-sustainability in the fishing of certain species of fish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
It aims to ensure that the EU’s common fisheries policy (CFP) is applied in the field of marine conservation and sustainable fishing.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the rules for the identification of countries that are not complying with the CFP and the measures that may be taken against them.
It does not apply to non-member countries of the European Economic Area (EEA).
The European Commission is empowered to adopt implementing acts in the event of non-compliance with the regulation.
The Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of the EU countries and the European Environment Agency.
National authorities must notify the Commission of any country that is identified as a non-signatory to this regulation. The Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for this identification and the possible measures that could be taken in relation to it.
If the Commission considers that a country is not a signatory to the regulation, it may adopt an implementing act in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 8(2) of Regulation (EC) No 101037/2013.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The CFP was adopted by the European Commission on 1 July 2018.
For more information, see:
Fisheries (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Non-Sustainability: the ability of a stock of fish to replenish itself with high probability while allowing high yield fisheries on it;
Sustainable: the boundaries of the size of the stock within which the stock can be replenished.
* Sustainable fishing: fishing that is not sustainable in the context of the conservation and management of living marine resources.

Summary 102:
Investment agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries (Bilateral Investment Agreements)
Investment agreement agreements between the EU and third countries (FDI)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2009 on bilateral investment agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the conditions under which EU countries are empowered to amend or conclude Bilateral investment agreements (BIs) with non-European Economic Area (EEA) countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to investment agreements concluded before 9 January 2013.
Amending or concluding a bilateral investment agreement
An EU country is authorised to enter into negotiations with a third country to amend an existing or to conclude a new BIs. It must notify the European Commission of its intention to do so within 90 days of receiving a notification from the other party.
The Commission is assisted by the Commission’s Committee for Investment Agreement, which is composed of representatives of the EU countries, the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) and is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
In the event that the Commission finds that the negotiations have not resulted in an agreement which fulfils the requirements of this regulation, it may authorise the EU country to sign and conclude such an agreement. The agreement must include a copy of the agreement.
Participation of the Commission in negotiations
In negotiations, the Commission is kept informed of the progress and results of the negotiations and may request to participate in the negotiations throughout the different stages and may also request to be involved in the discussions concerning investment.
Authorisation to sign or conclude a BIs
A BIs must be amended or concluded in accordance with the conditions laid down in this regulation. If the agreement does not meet these conditions, the agreement may be terminated.
If an EU country decides to terminate an existing BIs, it must inform the Commission of the reasons for its decision. The Commission must take its decision within 30 days of receipt of the notification.
Where the Commission considers that an amendment or a new agreement is not necessary, it will consult the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) and, if necessary, the Committee for Economic and Financial Affairs (EASA).
The EESC will report to the European Parliament and the Council on the outcome of the consultations and will adopt a recommendation on the basis of this report.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The BIs entered into force on 9 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has exclusive competence with respect to the common commercial policy of the EEA (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘Bilateral investment agreement’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Investment agreement: a bilateral agreement between a country and another EU country concerning investment between the two parties.
* FDI agreement: an investment agreement between 2 or more EU countries on the one hand, and between a company or a group of companies on the other.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) No 550/2009 of 9 January 2009 laying down conditions for the amendment or conclusion, on behalf of the European Union, of a bilateral Investment Agreement between a Member State and a third party (OJ L 157, 9.5.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 596 / 2009 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED ACTS
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 1296/2008 and (EC), Euratom No 1297/2008 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States meeting within the Council of 11 December 2008 on the exercise of the exclusive competence of the Union with regard to investment and on the application of Article 3(1)(e) of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU) (Official Journal C 202, 29.12.2008, pp.).

Summary 104:
Exports of firearms, their parts and essential components and ammunition: export control and control measures
Export controls and controls measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2005 on the export control of firearms and their parts, their components, ammunition and ammunition
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacture, import and export of firearms.
It lays down the rules on export authorisation, customs formalities and controls and import and transit measures.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the export of:
firearms;
firearm parts and components;
essential components or ammunition;
ammunition.
Authorisation
EU countries must ensure that:
the exporter of the items concerned has the necessary authorisation to export them;
the person who has the power to determine the sending of the item out of the customs territory of the EU has the authority to grant the authorisation;
authorisations are issued by the competent customs authority of an EU country and are valid for a period of 5 years.
The exporter must notify the customs authority in the EU country of which the item is to be exported. The exporter is required to provide proof that the export has been authorised by the customs office in the country of destination.
Exporters must inform the customs authorities of the country in which the export is to which they intend to send the item.
When granting export authorisations, the exporters must indicate the nature of the export and the name and address of the person who is responsible for the export authorising the export.
Export authorisation forms must be clearly visible and clearly on the form.
If an exporter fails to provide the required information, EU countries must take all necessary measures to ensure that the exporter’s name, address and contact details are clearly visible.
Importers are required to notify the competent authorities of their country of transit within 30 days of receiving a request for no objection to the transit of an item. In exceptional circumstances and for duly justified reasons, that period may be extended to 30 days from the day of the written request.
Where the customs official of the non-EU country in question has no objection, the consignee must submit a written request to the consul-general of that country for the approval of the transit. The consul general of the consular country may refuse to approve the transit if it considers that the consignment does not comply with the export conditions laid down in the regulation.
In exceptional circumstances, a consul may refuse the transit in the absence of a consular declaration of no objection. However, in such cases, the competent authority may grant the transit without prejudice to the conditions set out in the export declaration.
Controls
The competent authorities in EU countries are responsible for:
implementing the control measures laid down by the regulation;
monitoring and controlling the movement of firearms;
ensuring that firearms are not trafficked illegally within the EU;
taking all appropriate measures to prevent and combat the illicit trade in firearms and related products.
Penalties
The European Commission has adopted a list of penalties applicable to infringements of this regulation. These include:
effective, proportionate and dissuasive penalties for infringements;
a ban on the sale, import or export of certain firearms and certain parts and accessories;
restrictions on the supply of certain arms and ammunition to persons involved in the illegal trade;
suspension or withdrawal of the right to possess, manufacture, export, import, export or transfer of firearms or related products;
preventing, limiting or limiting the sale of certain ammunition and parts;
ban on the import of certain parts, accessories and ammunition intended for use in criminal activities;
prohibitions on the transfer of certain articles intended for the use of criminal offences.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
 
    баканска ганка  гранданна кародна, гълара бракса варка and глагарна  ханъкабанта даника (OJ L 347, 30.12.2005, pp. 1-2)

Summary 105:
Identifying, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime at the external borders of the European Union (EUROSUR)
Identifying and combating illegally entering and staying at the EU’s external borders (Eurosur)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 — strengthening situational awareness and reaction capability at the internal borders of EU countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBRCA).
It aims to improve situational awareness* and reaction capabilities of national border control authorities to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration.
KEY POINTS
The regulation provides for the establishment of a common pre-frontier intelligence picture to be shared between EU countries’ competent authorities and the EBRCA.
The common intelligence picture may contain information which is relevant for air border surveillance and checks at external border crossing points.
EU countries are required to establish a national coordination centre which is responsible for:
sharing information on all incidents at their external borders;
coordinating with each other and with the Agency;
sharing the information required to detect and prevent illegal immigration;
assessing the impact of incidents on border security;
collecting and disseminating information on incidents at the border;
exchanging information on the causes and consequences of incidents;
ensuring that information is shared with other EU countries and the Agency.
National coordination centre
The national coordination centres must operate twenty-four hours a day and seven days a week.
All incidents are shared with the agency, including:
information on the origin and destination of migrants;
data on the number of asylum applications;
the number of illegal immigrants and asylum seekers;
information about the number and nature of illegal border crossings;
a common border surveillance picture.
Information exchange
The Agency is required to exchange, process and store non-classified sensitive and classified information in the communication network in accordance with Article 11d of Regulation (EU) 2016/977/JHA and the relevant national provisions on data protection.
It is also required to:
ensure that information exchanged between the Agency and EU countries is protected by the principle of non-refoulement;
provide the Agency with the appropriate financial and human resources to adequately fulfil the additional tasks assigned to it under this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
EurOSUR is the common EU border control system for the protection and saving of the lives of migrants.
For more information, see:
External borders (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External borders: the external border of an EU country where there is no official border with another EU country.
* Illegal immigration: illegal immigration by land, sea or air.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 of 27 December 2008 establishing a common framework for the identification, prevention and control of illegal immigration, and of illegal crossing of the borders between the Member States of the Union (‘External borders’)

Summary 107:
Registered names of designations of origin and geographical indications and traditional specialities guaranteed (until 31 December 2020)
Regulation (EU) 2020/856 — registered names of designs of origin, designations and protected geographical indications (until 1 January 2021)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out rules on the registration and use of trade marks, trade marks* and designations* of origin* in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure the protection of traditional speciality* products and to improve the visibility of these products on the market.
KEY POINTS
Trade marks
The regulation sets out the conditions for the registration of trademarks and trade marks.
Trademarks are those which are used to mark products, such as:
traditional specialities* guaranteed*,
prepared meals*,
beer,
chocolate and derived products,
bread, pastry, cakes, confectionery, biscuits and other baker’s wares,
beverages made from plant extracts,
pasta,
salt.
Protected geographical indications are those that are protected by EU countries’ laws, regulations or administrative acts.
Registered names
The registered name of a protected geographical indication (PGI) must be used to describe a product which is produced or marketed in an EU country and which is protected by a protected name of origin.
The protected name must be accompanied by:
a geographical indication,
a trade mark,
an indication of the place of origin of the product,
the name of the producer or the place where the product is produced, marketed or intended to be marketed, and
a description of the geographical indications of the protected name.
A protected name is also protected if:
it is used in the context of the production, presentation or sale of products intended for sale in the EU country in which it is registered;
the protected name has been used for more than 10 years;
it has not been used in more than 5 years in the same EU country;
a protected name does not appear on a product other than in the product itself.
Traditional speciality guaranteed
The protection of a product is guaranteed if the following conditions are met:
the product is not intended for direct sale in another EU country,
it does not originate from a non-EU country and does not have a geographical origin;
in the case of prepared meals, the name is protected if it is:
originated in the country of origin; or
originating in a country other than that of the country where it is produced;
produced or marketed by a person or business established in that country.
In addition, the product must be guaranteed if it:
is:
made or produced in the territory of the EU;
consists of products originating or produced by EU farmers and producers;
has not been marketed in the territories of any other EU country (except in certain circumstances); or
is not intended or intended for the consumption of persons outside the EU.
EU countries may exclude from the scope of the regulation certain products which are protected under national law, regulations, administrative acts or administrative rules.
Trade mark protection
The European Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts, in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 552/2006, laying down the conditions of use for trade marks and protecting geographical indications. The Commission may also adopt implementing acts laying down derogations from these conditions.
Exceptions
The Commission is also empowered to take into account natural constraints affecting agricultural production in mountain areas, in order to take account of natural constraints relating to the use of mountain areas.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
    данскаганка гара барка  бълакована кака,
баланца Бакканна  хакса варнак,
какчакна (кашка),
кша накраква йкалка (начка) and
куккънабка(на) (traditional specialties guaranteed).
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect traditional and protected products.
For more information, see:
Protection of traditional products (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trade mark: a trademark or trade mark used to identify a product.
* Protected geographical indication: an indication of a place where a geographical or a national origin is indicated.

  годнанграндка: a protected



Summary 108:
An accelerated phasing-in scheme for the application of the double-hull or equivalent design requirements of MARPOL to single-Hull oil tankers (until 20 July 2012)
An accelerated phase-out scheme for applying MARPOL (see summary)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2010 — an accelerated phase out scheme for application of certain maritime safety and pollution prevention requirements of the International Maritime Organization’s Convention on the Law of the Sea (MARPOL)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a scheme to reduce the risk of oil spills in the European Union (EU) by reducing the number of oil tanker trips to and from EU ports.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following categories:
Category 1: crude oil tanker;
Category 2: oil tanker carrying heavy grade oil, irrespective of its flag.
It does not apply to Category 2 and Category 3 oil tanker.
Exemptions from the regulation are granted to Category 1 and Category 2 oil tanker operators who comply with the requirements of Regulation 19 of Annex I to MARPOL 73/78.
Limitations on the entry into the ports or offshore terminals under the jurisdiction of an EU country of other Category 2 or Category 3 and Category 4 and Category 5 and Category 6 and Category 7 and Category 8 and 8 and Category 9 and Category 10 and 10 and 11 and 12 and 11 tanker operators are also excluded from the scope of the regulation.
A ban on the transport to or from ports of the EU countries of heavy-grade oil in Category 1 or Category 1, 2, 3 and 4 heavy-duty oil carriers is in force from 20 July 2011.
The ban applies to:
category 1 and 2, Category 3, Category 4, Category 5, Category 6 or Category 7 oil carriers;
category 8 and 9, Category 10, Category 11 and Category 12 oil carriers and Category 13, Category 14 and 14 and 15 and 16 and 17 and 18 and 19 and 20 and 20 light-duty crude oil carriers.
In addition, the regulation prohibits:
the entry into or departure from the EU ports of a Member State of category 1,2,3,4,5 and 6 oil carriers of Category 2,5,6 and 7 categories; and
category 9 and 10 oil carriers with a maximum gross tonnage of 1.5 million tonnes.
Penalties
Member States may impose penalties for infringements of this regulation if:
a tanker is found to be in breach of the regulations;
a vessel is found not to be complying with the regulation; or
the vessel is a vessel of Category 3 or 4.
Compliance with the regulations is assessed by the European Commission, which is assisted by the Committee on Safe Seas and the Prevention of Pollution from Ships (COSS).
The Commission has delegated the power to adopt the delegated acts referred to in Article 10(1) to the COSS for a period of 5 years.
Amendments to Regulation (EC) 2015/856
The amendments to Regulation(EC) 2016/908 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Maritime safety and maritime pollution prevention (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* MARPOL: the Convention relating to the law of the sea and its enforcement.
* Heavy-duty tanker: an oil tanker of more than 500 gross tonnes, carrying more than 1 million tonnes of crude oil.

Summary 109:
European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority (EOPPA)
European Insurance & Occupational Pension Authority (eOPPA)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1092/2010 on the establishment of a European Supervisory Authority
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European supervisory authority (e.g. the EOPPA).
It aims to ensure that the prudential supervision of financial institutions in the European Union (EU), as well as the stability of the EU’s financial system, is ensured.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU Member States’ insurance and occupational pension (i.e. life, pension, health and long-term care) institutions.
It does not apply to banks.
The EPPAA has the following powers:
to adopt and adopt the rules applicable to it;
to make recommendations to the European Systemic Risk Board (ESRB);
to provide advice to the ESRB on the exercise of its supervisory powers; and
to act as a mediator between national supervisory authorities.
Role of the EPPA
The authority is responsible for:
monitoring and assessing the risk posed by financial institutions;
ensuring that all parties concerned are informed of all the measures taken by the authority;
assessing the risks that may arise in situations of stress;
providing advice and assistance to national authorities in the event of a serious disturbance to the financial system;
advising the European Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) on the application of the regulation;
adopting and implementing the rules of the European Financial Stability Facility (EFSA), the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM);
assisting the European Banking Authority with its tasks;
taking the necessary measures to protect the interests of the general public and of the Member States.
Implementation and control of the budget
The European Commission may, under certain conditions, grant a discharge to the authority for the implementation of the annual budget comprising revenue from the General Budget of the Union and competent authorities for the financial year N.
