Summary 110:
Equal treatment of national and non-national workers in the European Union
Equitable treatment of nationals and nationals of non-EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2005 on the equality of treatment of workers and nationals who are employed in the territory of another EU country
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to guarantee equal treatment to nationals of different EU countries when they work in the same place.
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (Euratom) No 1082/93.
KEY POINTS
   
The regulation applies to all workers, whether they are nationals of an EU country or nationals of another.
Equality of treatment
EU countries must ensure that:
nationals enjoy equal treatment as regards membership of trade unions and the exercise of rights attaching thereto, including the right to vote and to be eligible for the administration or management posts of a trade union;
workers wishing to move can also be regularly informed of living and working conditions.
Nationals enjoy the same treatment as nationals of the EU country in which they are employed, irrespective of their place of residence or where they wish to live or work.
Employment and equal treatment
Employers are required to provide workers with equal treatment in respect of:
the conditions of employment, including:
health and safety;
compulsory training and education;
the right to health and safety, including protection against sickness and long-term care;
social security and protection against discrimination;
protection against discrimination based on sex, race, colour, ethnic origin, religion or belief, disability, sexual orientation, age, disability or sexual orientation;
a minimum age of 18;
equality of treatment with regard to working hours and dismissal;
equal treatment with respect to working conditions, including working hours, rest and rest;
access to education and training;
recognition and recognition of the qualifications and qualifications of workers’ representatives;
identification of employers’ qualifications and the recognition of their qualifications;
employment and employment contracts;
membership and membership of the trade unions;
participation in the administration and management of a union.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation. It is assisted by an Advisory Committee and a Technical Committee.
Amendments
Amending Regulation (EU) 2019/849 introduces a new type of technical committee to deal with:
changes in the EU’s labour market, such as the creation of new jobs or the introduction of a new form of social security;
changes to the rules relating to employment contracts and employment agreements;
new rules on the protection of workers against discrimination on grounds of nationality;
measures to protect the interests of the environment and human rights;
adjustment of working hours to the needs of the labour market.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The original regulation has applied since 1 January 2006 and had to become law in all EU countries by 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
The Euratom Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) has been amended several times.
For more information, see:
‘Employment equality’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* National: a person who is a national of a Member State and who is employed in another Member State is entitled to equal treatment under the law of the Member State in which he or she is employed.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еълганска гранда барка (Codified version) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2006, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC), No 598/2005 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 111:
Ozone-depleting potential of certain substances
Ozone degradation of substances (Ozone Degradation Regulation)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the reduction of ozone depletion potential
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect human health and the environment against the adverse effects of the production and use of substances that deplete the ozone layer.
It recasts and repeals Council Regulation (EEC) 98/879/EEC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the production, placing on the market or use of certain chemicals, as well as to the import and export of these substances. It does not apply to certain substances listed in Annex I to the regulation.
The substances covered by the regulation are:
hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HFCs);
nitrous oxide (NOx);
chlorinated biphenyls (COs); and
nitrosamines (NOXs).
It does not cover certain substances, such as hydrocarbons, which are banned in the EU because of their harmful effects on human health, the environment and the human respiratory system.
Production and use
Producers of HFCs and NCOs are subject to a number of conditions, including the following:
they must comply with the following conditions:
the production of the substances is limited to the limits set out in Annex II to this regulation;
the quantities of each substance may not exceed the limits laid down in Annex III;
they are not to be placed on the European Union’s internal market;
there is no limit on the quantity of substances which can be produced;
each producer must ensure that each producer ensures that the quantities produced are not exceeded by more than 5 % of the total volume of the substance;
in addition, each producer must keep a register of the quantities of substances produced and the quantities exported to the EU.
In addition, certain substances may be excluded from the scope of the regulation if they have been authorised by the competent authority of an EU country.
There are also certain exceptions to the above conditions. For example, certain chemicals may be exempted from the above requirements if they are used in the following the approval of the EU country in which the production is taking place.
Imports and exports
Importers and exporters of substances may not place on the EU market or to use for their own account substances that are controlled by EU countries.
Exports and imports of substances to non-EU countries may be authorised if they comply with certain conditions, in particular that they do not endanger human health or the environment, and that they are not used for the manufacture, import or export of substances other than those listed in the annexes to this Regulation.
Limitations on imports and exports are laid down by Regulation (EU) 2017/852 (see summary).
The limits on the quantities that can be imported and exports of substances are set out by the Regulation (EC).
Imported and exported substances may only be imported into the EU if they meet the following criteria:
there are no restrictions on imports or exports of the following substances:
conformity with the laws and regulations of the Member State in which they are being imported or exported,
the substances are not intended for human consumption,
they have not been identified as being hazardous to human health (except in the case of nitrous oxide);
they do not have a significant effect on the environment or on the health of animals, plants or human beings;
imports or exports do not pose a risk to public health or to the environment.
Non-refillable containers may not be used to store or transport substances, except for laboratory and analytical uses.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2004/96/EC has applied since 1 January 2005 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
   Â   бансканка гандалка (OECD) is one of the most widely traded chemicals in the world and is a major source of greenhouse gases.
For more information, see:
‘Ozone depletion potential of substances’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Nitrogen oxide: a substance that depletes ozone by absorbing ultraviolet radiation.
* NCO: an organic substance with a high degree of ultraviolet (i.e. no warming effect).
* Hydrochloric acid: a naturally occurring substance that is produced in the troposphere. It is a constituent of hydrochloric acids and is used in refrigeration and air conditioning equipment. It has a low degree of toxicity and has been shown to have a depleting effect in the atmosphere due to its formation in the presence of ultraviolet rays.
MAIN DOCUMENT



Summary 112:
European Union Agency for the Protection of the Environment (EUREA) (European Environment Agency) (see amending acts)
European Union agency for the protection of the environment (EureA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 596/2008 establishing the European Union Agency to protect the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets up the European Environment Agency (ESA), a European Union (EU) body responsible for collecting, analysing and disseminating information on the EU’s environment.
KEY POINTS
The ESA is responsible for:
collecting and analysing all the information necessary to assess the present and foreseeable state of the EU's environment;
monitoring and evaluating the environmental situation;
providing information to the European Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (Euratom).
It is also responsible for the coordination of EU environmental policy.
Eurea is funded by the EU budget.
It has a budget of €2.5 billion for the 2014-2020 period.
The Eurea has a management board comprising representatives from each EU country. It is chaired by an EU country and is assisted by a Management Board.
Its tasks
ESA is responsible, in particular, for the following:
ensuring that:
the information it collects and analyses is accurate, reliable and up-to-date;
facilitating the exchange of information between EU countries;
coordinating the information between the EU countries on the environment and the EU;
carrying out the tasks assigned to it by the Commission and the Council;
assisting the Commission in the preparation of the multiannual work programme;
developing and implementing EU environmental policies;
contributing to the development of the environmental information network;
examining the environmental impact of EU policies and programmes;
collects, analyses and disseminates information on:
environmental policy;
the quality of life of the population and of the natural and human resources;
consulting with the public on the impact of environmental policy; and
assessing the environmental impacts on the economy and social aspects of environmental protection.
In addition, ESA is involved in the following activities:
coordination of the work of the European Network for the Assessment of the Environmental Impact of Physical, Scientific and Technological Developments (ENISA) and the Network of European Reference Materials and Measurements (NARME);
exchange of information on environmental issues;
organisation and coordination of the activities of the EUREA;
cooperation with the European Monitoring Centre (EMC);
the European Network of Scientific and Technical Experts (Eurostat); and
exchanges of information and expertise in the field of climate change and energy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2009.

Summary 113:
Assessment and verification of environmental compliance
Assessment & verification of Environmental compliance
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2016/852 — assessment and verification requirements for organisations relating to the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve environmental compliance in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the conditions for organisations to be accredited and validated to ensure that they comply with EU environmental legislation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU-registered organisations, whether or not they are based in the EU.
It does not apply to companies operating in non-EU countries.
Requirements for organisations
To be accredited or licensed, an organisation must:
establish, implement and maintain an environmental management system (EMAS) and an updated environmental statement (Annex I);
provide an environmental statement, in accordance with Annex II of the regulation;
prepare an environmental assessment and an environmental verifier;
ensure that the information in the EMAS and the environmental statement are accurate, complete and up-to-date;
implement and maintain a programme to monitor and monitor the environmental aspects of its EMAS;
assess the verifier’s competence in the light of the elements set out in Annexes III and IV.
Accreditation and licensing bodies
An accreditation or licensing body is responsible for assessing whether an organisation meets all the requirements for the recognition of an EMAS or the environmental assessment.
An environmental assessment is carried out by an accredited body or by a licencing body.
The accreditation body is assisted by a competent body. The competent body may be national, regional or local.
Environmental verifiers
Certificates of environmental verifiers are issued by the competent body and are valid for a maximum of 5 years. They must meet the following criteria:
be based in an EU country;
have the necessary qualifications and experience;
be able to carry out the tasks of the accredited body;
meet the requirements laid down in Annex IV;
demonstrate that they have the necessary skills, knowledge and experience to ensure compliance with the requirements;
carry out their tasks in a manner that is appropriate to their role.
Certificate of compliance
The certificate of compliance is valid for 5 years and can be renewed for a further 5 years if the organisation does not fulfil the following conditions:
the environmental assessment has been validated;
the organisation has been accredited or licenced by an accreditation and/or licensing body and has carried out the verification and validation procedures correctly;
it has been published in the Official Journal of the EU;
in the case of a non-validated environmental assessment, it has been issued by a recognised body. If an organisation is found to be in breach of the requirements, it must take corrective action to prevent or reduce the impact of the non-conformity.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULAR REGULATING AND VERIFICATION APPLY?
The delegated regulation entered into force on 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Assessing and verifying environmental compliance’ on the European Commission's website.

  барсканка гранданскога нарка (гродна какоданна)

Summary 114:
Rules on the definition, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks (Directive 2006/13/EC)
Rules on defining, describing, presentation, labelling and safety of spirit drink ingredients
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 — rules on spirit drinks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules to define, describe, present and labilise spirit drinks.
It aims to protect the geographical indications of certain spirit drinks, while taking into account traditional production methods.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to spirit drinks which are:
made from ethyl alcohol of agricultural origin, with a maximum methanol content of 5 grams per hectolitre of 100% vol. alcohol;
made by means of the fermentation of agricultural products or products listed in Annex I to the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and which are not alcoholic;
produced in the territory of an EU country, subject to the conditions laid down in Annex II to the TFEU;
consisted of spirits obtained from the following fruits or berries:
plum (Prunus domestica L.),
coconut (Coconut),
pomace (Pomace),
sugar (Sugarcane),
chardonnay (Sugar),
malt (Maltese),
virgin olive oil,
salt (Sulphur),
wine (Spirate),
liqueur (Luxembourg),
Liqueur,
pomegranate,
vodka (Vodka),
zinfandel,
Liquor (Vienna),
Vincière,
Vinho verde verde,
Vienna,
Pernoden,
Brandy/Weinbrand,
Czechoslovakia,
Estonia,
Greece,
Helsinki,
Hungary,
Iceland,
Latvia,
Slovenia and
Romania
Lithuania,
Serbia,
Slovakia (Lithuanian liqueur) and
Spirits produced in the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia,
Bulgaria, and
Vietnamese liqueurs.
Distribution and marketing
The legislation does not apply to:
alcoholic drinks which meet the definition of ‘spirit drink’;
wine-based drinks which do not meet the requirements for inclusion in categories 1 to 46 of Annex II;
spirits intended for consumption by the general public (e.g. sparkling wine, sparkling cider, sparkling rum, sparkling wine-based beer, etc.).
However, spirit drinks intended for the consumption of the general population may be marketed as such, provided that they do not exceed the following limits:
the maximum alcoholic strength by volume of a spirit drink is not more than 37.5% by volume;
the minimum alcoholic strength per 100 ml of fruit spirit is not less than 35.5%;
a spirit drink does not contain more than 5% alcohol by volume.
Labelling
A spirit drink must bear the sales denomination ‘Spirit Drink’.
The sales denomination must be preceded by the name of the fruit, berry or vegetable, such as: ‘Cocoa’, ‘Pompey’ or ‘Luxemi’ preceded by ‘Fruit Spirit’ and ‘cocoa or Liqueur’ (in the case of sparkling wine);
the sales denomination of a fruit spirit must be ‘fruit spirit or kirsch’ followed by the names of the fruits, berries or vegetables used in the preparation of the spirit drink.
Annex III to the regulation sets out the rules for the presentation of spirits.
Directive (EU) 2016/852 amends the regulation to take account of the fact that spirit drinks may not be marketed under trade marks, brand names or fancy names.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2007 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Spirit drink: a spirit is defined as:
an alcoholic drink made from the alcohol obtained by the fermentation, distillation and/or blending of the raw material(s) used.
* Cocoa: a sparkling wine or sparkling cider which does not have the properties of an alcoholic drink but still retains the aroma and taste of the wine or wine-product drinks, and which is not intended to be consumed as such.

Summary 115:
Common rules for safeguarding civil aviation safety and security of passengers, cargo, mail and mail (until 31 December 2020) (Regulation (EC) No 596/2006)
Common rules on safeguarding the security of civil aviation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation 2006/512/EC — common rules for the protection of persons and goods in the European Union (civil aviation)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect passengers and goods against acts of unlawful interference with civil aviation.
It sets out common rules to protect the safety, security and quality of the transport of persons, goods and mail within the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
passengers, cargo and mail;
aircraft;
cargo;
mail;
passenger and mail.
EU countries must ensure that:
the safety of passengers and cargo is guaranteed;
the security of the aircraft and of the passengers and mail is ensured;
there is a high level of protection for the safety of the cargo or mail of passengers;
security measures are in place to ensure that passengers and freight are not exposed to risks that could jeopardise their safety; and
airport security checks are carried out by the competent authorities.
The European Commission is assisted by a Committee composed of representatives of EU countries and the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA).
National quality control programmes
Each EU country must establish a national quality control programme for the carriage of goods and passengers. The programme must allow for the swift detection and correction of any deficiencies in the programme may detect and correct any deficiencies. It must be submitted to the competent national authority, which may decide to take further action if necessary.
A security programme must be established by the entity applying aviation security standards. This programme must contain the following information:
a list of the entities responsible for applying security standards;
a description of the measures taken by each EU country to ensure compliance with the security programme;
an assessment of the effectiveness of the programme by the authority responsible for implementing the programme.
If the programme is found to be deficient, the competent authority must take the necessary measures to correct the deficiencies and inform the European Commission of the reasons for the deficiency.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the security and safety of air transport in the EU, including:
rules on the identification and screening of persons;
rules for the certification of airlines;
measures to ensure the safe transport of mail and cargo by air.
For more information, see:
Civil aviation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Passenger: any person, organisation or enterprise, other than an operator of an aircraft.
* Cargo or mail: any item of mail, whether it is intended for transport by air, sea or land.

Summary 116:
Freezing of funds and economic resources of individuals and entities involved in serious human rights violations and abuses in the EU
Freezing and restricting the movement of funds
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the freezing and restriction of access to frozen funds
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect the EU’s financial and economic interests against the illegal activities of individuals, entities and bodies responsible for, providing support to or otherwise involved with serious human-rights violations or abuses, as well as those associated with them.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
individuals, entities or bodies listed in Annex I of the regulation;
the territories of the EU countries to which the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TEU) is applicable, under the conditions laid down in the TEU;
any natural or legal person, entity or body acting through or on behalf of one of the persons, entities listed in the annexes.
It does not apply to funds or economic resources belonging to, controlled by, held by or controlled by any EU country.
Procedures for the freezing
The freezing of funds is based on a number of factors, including the following:
the nature of the individual or entity concerned, the nature of its activities and the extent to which it is connected to the EU or the EU as a whole, and the nature and duration of its involvement in those activities.
The person or entity covered by the regulation must be identified by the competent national authority.
National authorities must inform the other EU countries and the European Commission within 2 weeks of receiving an application for an authorisation.
EU countries must ensure that the competent authority of the country concerned informs the Commission of any authorisation granted under the regulation and informs the European Parliament, the Council, the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy and the Committee of the Regions of the reasons for the decision.
If the Commission considers that an individual or body is not complying with the regulation, it informs the national authority of that the person or body concerned is subject to an asset freeze or asset freeze.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2017/852/JHA (see summary) sets out the measures to be taken by the EU in response to the threat posed by the Russian Federation and the threat of its military intervention in the internal market.
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom (EC) No 596/2013 and Decision (EU/2017/849/EU) 2019/848 (see consolidated version)
Regulations (EU and Euratom), (EC), (Euratom) No 496/2014, (EC, EFTA) No 1099/2014 and (EU).
KEY TERMS
* Asset freeze: the freezing or restriction of funds, economic resources or any other means of obtaining, using or attempting to obtain financial or financial resources.
* Financial resources: any financial, economic or material asset, such as cash, securities or other assets.
ACT
Commission Implementing Decision 2017/884/JAI of 25 November 2017 concerning the freezing, restriction and restriction on the movement, freezing or restricting the movements, holding, use or possession of frozen funds and the freezing of economic resources in respect of persons and entities associated with, or directly or indirectly involved in, any of the above listed individuals and bodies.

Summary 117:
European Union military operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFORCE IN THE MIDMARY: EUNATIONAL MEDICAL OPERATION IN THE MARITIME (EUNAM MEDI)
European Union Military Operation in the MEDIAM MEDINI (EUNAM MED)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 — on a European Union Military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to launch a military operation to combat human smuggling and trafficking in the eastern Mediterranean.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the operation’s mandate and its objectives.
The operation is based on the EU’S common security and defence policy (CSDP) and the common foreign and security policy.
It aims to:
contribute to the prevention, detection and eradication of illegal migration;
combat the smuggling of migrants, human trafficking and illegal arms trafficking, as well as to the illicit export of illicit oil and gas from Libya;
ensure the protection of EU nationals and the safety and security of EU citizens and nationals of non-EU countries;
reduce the risk of human smuggling, including by:
ensuring a high level of protection for EU nationals;
contributing to disruption of the business model of illegal immigration and trafficking networks;
providing a deterrent to illegal migration.
In addition, the EU will also:
supporting the efforts of the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTYT), the Organisation for the Prohibition of Transnational Organised Crime (OCT), the fight against organised crime, and terrorism, and combating the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDMOs), as well-known as WMDs;
promoting cooperation between EU countries and the International Maritime Organization (IMO);
support the Libyan authorities in their fight against trafficking in human beings;
cooperating with the United Nations High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (HR), assisted by the European External Action Service (EEAS).
The High Representative will act as the primary point of contact with the UN, the authorities of the countries in the region, and other international and bilateral actors, including NATO, the African Union and the League of Arab States. The High Representative is also authorised to release to the UN classified information up to ‘REST UE/REREREVISED’ level.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The Council authorised the EU to launch the operation on 31 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been involved in a number of military operations in Libya in recent years, including:
Operation SOPHIA (2014-2020) and
Operation MIRACLE (2015-2020).
For more information, see:
‘EU military operation against illegal migration’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 118:
Facilitating the exchange of information on authentic documents and on known methods of falsification between EU countries’ authorities (FADO)
Facilitating cooperation between Member States in the fight against document and identity fraud (FADS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 establishing the European Agency for the Protection of the Internal Market against Document and Identity Fraud
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the Facilitating the Exchange of Information on Authentic Documents and on Known Methods of Falsification of Documents and Identifications of Persons in the Schengen Area.
KEY POINTS
The FADO system was set up to facilitate the exchange and sharing of information between Member State authorities in order to prevent and combat fraud.
The Agency is responsible for ensuring the proper and reliable functioning of the FADSO system and for providing support to the competent Member States' authorities in the detection of false documents.
FADSO is a centralised information exchange system for the sharing of authentic and false documents between the competent national authorities of the Member States.
It is based on the information provided by the national authorities and is managed by the European Commission, which is assisted by the Agency’s Directorate-General for Internal Market and Competitiveness (DG-EMC).
The European Commission is the competent authority for the implementation of delegated acts. The Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts by the Council and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The purpose of the regulation is to establish a system to facilitate cooperation between national authorities to prevent, detect and combat the production and use of false or fraudulent documents and to identify the methods used to obtain them.
For more information, see:
‘Facilitating information exchange’ on authentic and falsified documents (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Document and identity* fraud: any act or omission which is likely to result in the loss or damage of a person's identity or cause damage to their physical, mental or social integrity.
* FADSOR: a system of information exchange between national and EU-level authorities for the purpose of preventing, detecting and combating the production, use and falsification of false and/or fraudulent documents, as well as for the identification and verification of authentic documents obtained by fraudulent means.

Summary 119:
Access to regulated professions
Access to unregulated professions
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/43/EC — access to regulated profession
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to ensure that professionals who are not covered by national legislation have the same access to the regulated professions as consumers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to the following professions:
physicists, dentists, optometrists, architects, architects and engineers;
veterinary surgeons;
vocational trainers;
consultants and architects;
legal advisors;
business managers;
professional trainers.
It does not apply to any other profession.
Regulation
The regulation of a regulated profession is based on the principle of non-discrimination and on the principles of proportionality. It does not preclude the introduction of additional requirements to attain the public interest objectives of the legislation.
The European Commission has adopted a summary report on the implementation of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It entered into force on 1 January 2010. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union (‘the Charter’) guarantees the freedom to choose an occupation, as well as to conduct a business.
However, in the absence of specific rules on access to a regulated professions, it is up to each EU country to decide whether and how to regulate them.
For more information, see:
‘Access to the profession’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Regulated profession: a profession in which the activity of a person is regulated.
MAIN DOCUMENT
* Directives:
Council Directive 2009/42/EC on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the regulation of the regulated profession and the access to that profession, and repealing Directive 2005/36/EC.

Summary 120:
Management of fish stocks in the North Sea and the Faroe Islands
Migratory fish stocks
Migration of migratory fish in the north-east Atlantic
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1224/2009/2014 on the common fisheries policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the management of fish stock and functional units (FMSY) in the northern and faroe islands.
It aims to restore fish stocks to levels that are capable of producing sustainable fish stocks (MSY).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to fish stocks of:
fisheries exploiting stocks of plaice and sole (plaice, sole, molluscs, clams and clams);
fishing opportunities for stocks of clams (e.g. oysters, swordfish, mussels, clammed mackerel);
fish stocks of certain species of fish (cetaceans, porbeagle, bonito, skates, porpoises, porcine, sea breams, oysters);
seabirds (sailfish, sharks and skates).
It does not apply to stocks of fish belonging to the stock or functional unit listed in the regulation’s Annex II.
Fishing opportunities must be fixed in such a way as to:
prevent variations in fishing opportunities between consecutive years to not more than 20 %;
limit variations in the number of fishing opportunities per year to no more than 5 %; and
limit variation in fishing mortality to not less than 5% per year.
limit the total annual fishing opportunities (TAC) per FMSY per fishery.
The plan must contain objectives, quantifiable targets with clear timeframes, conservation reference points, safeguards and technical measures designed to avoid and reduce unwanted catches.
In addition, the plan must provide for additional management measures to ensure the sustainable management of MSY.
Management measures
The management plan must be based on the best available scientific and technical information and must contain:
a list of objectives and targets for the return of the stock to levels above those capable of production MSY;
a description of the measures to be taken to achieve these objectives;
measures to prevent and reduce the risk of unwanted catches;
the list of measures to prevent or reduce the impact of recreational fishing on MSY, including measures to limit the quantity of recreational catches and/or to restrict fishing opportunities;
targeting the maximum number of fish caught per year for each species of fished fish;
specific measures to protect the marine environment and the marine ecosystems, including the establishment of a marine protected area (MPA).
The Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts to adopt the management plan.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
The Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) is the EU's fisheries policy and is implemented by the EU and its Member States. It aims to protect and restore the stocks of marine mammals, birds, reptiles, amphibians, crustaceans and fish stocks.
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Migratory species: fish that migrate between the North Atlantic Ocean and the Atlantic Ocean.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 676/2007 of 11 June 2007 establishing a multiannual plan for fisheries exploiting stock of Plaice (OJ L 157, 19.6.2007, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 672/2007 have been incorporated into the basic text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 122:
Controls on cash entering and leaving the EU
Countries must introduce measures to prevent and combat money laundering and terrorist financing. This regulation lays down rules to ensure that controls on cash are effective and efficient.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) 2016/794 of 25 April 2016 on controls of cash entering or leaving the European Union.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a system of controls with respect to cash entering the EU to complement the EU’s legal framework for the prevention of money laundering.
KEY POINTS
Controls are based on a threshold of €10,000 per person, per day, per country.
The limit is set by the European Commission and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).
It does not apply to:
cash belonging to individuals;
money belonging to companies;
prepaid cards;
cash used for the transport of goods or goods intended for personal consumption;
the proceeds of criminal activities.
Information exchange between EU countries
EU countries must:
ensure that the competent authorities of the EU country in which the cash is being brought into or out of the country have access to all the relevant information;
share this information with the competent national authorities of other EU countries if there are indications of criminal activity;
inform the Financial Action Task Force (FATF) of all the information they have obtained;
provide information on suspected criminal activities;
identify any persons or entities involved in money laundering, terrorist financing or money laundering;
assess by 1 June 2019 the need to establish a common mechanism for the exchange of information between the relevant national authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
This regulation is one of a series of measures designed to strengthen the fight against money laundering in the EU. The other measures include:
the establishment of a common EU-wide system for the control of the movement of cash within the EU;
measures to prevent the reintroduction of illicit proceeds into the economy and the diversion of money to finance illicit activities, as well as measures to combat the financing of terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Controls of cash’ on the OLAF's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Cash: cash belonging to a natural person or to a business.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 123:
Integrated national energy and climate plans for the energy union (2014-20) and beyond
Integmented national energy & climate plans (2014 - 20)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/2284 on the energy Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out the EU’s energy union and the framework for its implementation.
It aims to improve energy security, reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions and tackle climate change.
KEY POINTS
The energy union aims to:
improve energy security;
improve the internal energy market;
reduce greenhouse gas emissions;
decarbonise;
increase energy efficiency; and
improve research, innovation and competitiveness.
The directive sets out a framework for the implementation of these objectives. It also sets out how the EU plans to meet these objectives and how they should be aligned with the Paris Agreement on Climate Action (see summary).
The framework is based on the Energy Union Framework Directive (2014/40/EU), which aims to ensure a high degree of mutual trust between the EU and its Member States, while limiting administrative complexity. It is also designed to ensure that national energy plans are consistent with the objectives set out in the framework directive.
Scope
The legislation applies to all EU Member States. It does not apply to the EU countries’ energy security and climate policies.
National energy plans must cover ten-year periods and should provide an overview of the current energy system and policy situation. They should also provide an assessment of the energy situation and future energy needs of the EU, climate and climate situation, as well into the future.
In addition, they should include:
a description of how the energy system is currently functioning,
a breakdown of the national energy security framework and a description of the future energy system;
a summary of the measures to be taken to achieve the energy security objectives;
an assessment of how climate change is likely to affect energy security over the next 10 years.
Cooperation between Member States
Member States are required to cooperate with the European Commission, which is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation and implementation of the plans;
assessing the impact of energy policies and measures on climate change;
sharing information on the climate and energy situation;
developing and implementing climate action plans.
Implementing the framework
The Commission must:
inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the progress it has made in implementing the framework;
draw up a report every 5 years on the progress made by Member States on implementing the EU energy union;
provide the Commission with a report on how the framework is being applied;
make recommendations to the Council on how to implement the framework.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The decision has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The Energy Union is one of the pillars of the European Union (EU). It is the single energy market and is the main source of energy for the EU.
For more information, see:
Energy Union (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2014/42/EU of 14 December 2016 on the integrated national energy union, amending Directive 2003/35/EC and repealing Directive 2001/81/EC (OJ L 344, 17.12.2016, pp. 1-24)

Summary 124:
Chemical substances and hazardous waste: EU action plan
Chemicals and hazardous wastes: EU actions plan
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 — EU action plans for the prevention, control and elimination of hazardous substances and waste
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to protect human health and the environment against the risks associated with the production, manufacture, placing on the market and use of hazardous chemicals and waste.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances, mixtures and articles which may present a risk to human health or the environment.
It does not apply to substances listed in Annex I to Directive 2008/98/EC (see summary).
It applies to the following substances:
polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs);
polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs);
pentabromodiphenyl ethers;
chlorinated naphthalenes;
decaBDEs and hexabromide;
piperazine.
The list of substances covered by the regulation is amended by Regulation (EU) 2019/856, which sets out the list of chemicals and mixtures which are subject to the EU’s maximum limits for their presence in mixtures or articles.
Control
The European Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the substances and articles covered by this regulation;
checking that substances are safe for human health;
assessing the safety and environmental impact of substances;
establishing an EU-wide database of substances and their mixtures.
checking the conformity of substances to the relevant EU legislation.
EU countries must ensure that substances listed on the European Chemicals Agency (ECA) and the European Forum for the Protection of Life and Health of the Environment (EFHA) are listed on their national lists of banned substances.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 16 July 2008 and had to become law in the EU countries by 16 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
 The regulation is one of a series of measures designed to protect the health and environment of the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Hazardous substances’ on the ECA's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Hazardous substance: any substance or mixture of substances with a high potential for causing, or likely to cause, serious adverse effects on human health, the environment or the health of animals, plants, plants or other organisms, or which has the potential to do so, if it is present in a quantity exceeding the limits set out in the regulation.

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products and fishing gear — addressing marine litter (SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2010/65/EU — combating marine litter
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce plastic pollution in the European Union (EU) by reducing the amount of plastic waste that ends up in the marine environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to plastic products that are found the most on beaches in the EU, as well as fishing gear containing plastic and products made from oxo-degradable plastic.
It does not apply to:
plastic products used in medical devices, such as medical devices and implants, which are not designed for re-use or can be recycled;
plastics used in toys, toys and household products;
fishing gear which is made from plastic and which is not intended to be reused or re-used;
single-use products which are found in the sea or in the air and which are intended for use only once or once only.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that the plastic products listed in the directive are not placed on the market. They must also ensure that producers of these products are responsible for the costs of cleaning up marine litter.
Producers of plastic products are required to cover, in addition to the cost of waste collection and treatment of waste, the costs associated with the waste generated by public collection systems and the subsequent transport and treatment by the EU country in which the product is placed on sale.
The legislation does not cover:
the costs of waste from the manufacture, manufacture and sale of the products listed above, or from the disposal of waste in landfills or landfishing vessels;
the cost of the waste collection of waste fishing gear.
Implementing acts
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts laying down the format for reporting data and information in accordance with the directive’s Annex I. These must be transmitted electronically to the European Environment Agency (EEA) by 1 January 2022.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2010. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
The plastic waste directive is one of a series of legislative measures designed to tackle plastic pollution. The other measures are:
Regulation (EC) No 1342/2008 on plastic waste (see summary), which aims to protect the environment and human health by reducing plastic waste in the environment;
Council Directive 2008/98/EC on the prevention, control and elimination of marine litter and repealing Directive 2000/59/EC (see overview);
Directives 2000/60/EC and 2008/56/EC, which aim to protect marine ecosystems and human life by reducing marine litter;
Directions 2010/59 and 2014/34/EU on the protection of marine environment and the sustainable use of plastic;
Decision 2014/35/EU establishing a framework for the implementation of the marine litter directive (See summary).
KEY TERMS
* Single-use product: a product which is intended only to be used once or only for a limited period of time and is not designed to be recycled or reused or reused.
* Fishing gear: a piece of fishing gear that is intended for only one purpose and which can be used only once, for example, to catch fish or to catch marine mammals.

  (OJ L 151, 6.6.2010, pp. 1-8)

Summary 126:
Registration of EU trade marks
Registration of European Union trade marks (EU trade marks)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the registration of the EU trade mark
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Union Office for Harmonisation of Trade Marks (EUTM), the EU’s official register of trade marks.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the registration and functioning of the EUTM.
It also lays down the conditions under which trade marks can be registered and the procedures for their enforcement.
Registration
The registration of a trade mark is carried out by means of a certificate of registration issued by the Eutm.
The register is valid for a period of 5 years from the date on which the application is made. It may be renewed for a further 5 years if the applicant does not meet certain conditions.
A trade mark may be registered in one EU country or in another EU country if it is:
recognised by a national trade mark registered in that country;
obliged by national law;
applied for in an EU country; or
obtained in an international application.
An application for registration may be made by:
a person or organisation established in one or more EU countries;
a company established in a non-EU country; and
a business established in the EU country.
If an application is accepted, the holder must:
submit a copy of the application to the trade mark to the office of the Office;
apply for a licence to use the trade marks;
submit the application in a language other than English or French;
provide all the information necessary to obtain the registration;
pay a fee to the Office for the purpose of obtaining the registration certificate;
notify the Office within 3 months of receiving the application;
inform the Office of the reasons for rejecting the application within 30 days of receiving it;
ensure that the applicant has all the necessary documentation to prove that the application has been accepted;
approve the application and the validity of the registration.
Application for registration
If the Office accepts the application, the applicant must provide the following information:
the name and address of the person or company concerned;
the trade mark in question;
any other information requested by the applicant;
information concerning the nature, origin and nature of the trademark;
an estimate of the value of the registered trade mark.
Where the applicant fails to provide the information required, the Office may refuse to register the application or refuse to issue a certificate. If the applicant is unable to provide all of the information requested, it may decide that the registration has expired and notify the proprietor of the loss or damage caused by the infringements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The original regulation entered into force on 1 January 2005. It has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The EutM was set up under Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 696/2003.
For more information, see:
Trade marks (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trade mark: a sign or indication used to identify a product or service.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) No 529/2004 of 12 December 2004 on the Registration of the Union Trade Mark (Codified version) (OJ L 347, 14.12.2004, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 529/04 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 127:
Accessible formats for copyright and related rights for the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats of certain works and other protected subject matter (Marrakesh Treaty)
Assessment of Accessible formats in the field of cultural materials for the benefit of persons with disabilities (MARPAC)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 on the application of the Marrakesh Convention on Accessible Formats for the Making and Distribution of Copies of Copyright and Related Rights for the Benefit of Persons with Disabilities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the availability of accessible format copies of copyright, related rights and related cultural materials to persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled.
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright or related rights in order to make and disseminate accessible formats* of protected works and subject matter and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following:
copies of copyright-protected works or other subject matter made in an accessible format (e.g. in Braille, large print, adapted e-books, audio books and radio broadcasts).
Authorised entities (i.e. entities established in an EU country) may distribute, communicate or make available to beneficiary persons or to an authorised entity established in a non-EU country that is a party to the Convention on the protection of intellectual property rights (CPR) an accessible version of a work or other CPR made in accordance with the national legislation adopted by that country.
An authorised entity may also make available, to the public or to a third party, an accessible form of the same work or subject matter.
Export of accessible formats to third countries
EU countries may not restrict the export of these copies to third parties.
Exceptions to certain rights
An EU country may introduce a mandatory exception for certain rights that are harmonised by EU law. This exception must be provided for in the national law of the EU country in which the authorisation has been granted.
The authorisation is valid for a period of 5 years from the date of entry into force of the relevant national legislation. The authorisation period may be renewed or terminated at any time by the competent national authority.
Authorisation of authorised entities
An authorisation may be refused if:
the content of the work or the subject matter is protected by copyright;
the authorisation does not comply with the conditions laid down in this regulation; or
the use of the content is contrary to public policy or to the general public interest.
Accessibility of the accessible format may be subject to certain conditions, such as:
it must be accessible to persons with visual impairments or disabilities, insofar as, as a result of such impairments and disabilities, those persons are unable to read printed works to substantially the same degree as persons without such disabilities;
it may not be adapted to the extent that would normally be normally acceptable for reading; and
it is subject to the conditions set out in Annex I to this regulation.
In addition, an authorisation must be granted for the following purposes:
to:
make accessible formats available to beneficiaries;
to the public;
for the exchange of these accessible formats between EU countries;
in the event that the content of an adapted version of the adapted version is covered by national legislation;
exchange of these adapted formats between the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The Marrakhé Convention is the United Nations Convention for the Protection of Intellectual Property Rights (UNCRPD) and its Protocol on Accessibility of Cultural Materials for Persons With Disabilities.
For more information, see:
Accessible format for copyright (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Accessible format: any form of printed material that is intended to be read or understood by a person with visual or print-impaired eyes, but which is not intended for the naked eye.
* Copyright: the right to reproduce, reproduce, translate, translate or translate a work into a language other than that of the author.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratom of 11 December 2017 on the approximation of the laws and regulations of the Member States relating to access to cultural material for persons with disability and repealing Council Regulation (Euratom) No 596/2004 (OJ L 183, 12.12.2017, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
Council Decision (CFSP) 2015/856/CFSP of 25 November 2015 on the evaluation of the application by the Member State of the Convention of the Rights of the Blind, Visually Impaired and Otherwise Print-Disabled Persons to the Accessible Format for Copyright and related Rights in the Field of Cultural Material for Persons with Disability (



Summary 128:
Cooperation and coordination between EU countries’ consumer protection authorities and the EU’s consumer protection network (Regulation (EU) 2017/1128 — cooperation and coordination among the consumer protection networks and the networks of regulatory bodies and authorities established by sectoral EU legislation
Coordination and cooperation among EU countries' consumer protection organisations and the national authorities
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/2195 — cooperation between the European Commission and national authorities in the field of consumer protection
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cooperation between EU Member States’ competent authorities in order to prevent and combat cross-border infringements of EU consumer protection legislation.
It establishes a common framework for cooperation between national authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following types of infringements:
cross-border infringement:
the infringement by a trader of a product or service which has a European dimension (i.e. the infringement is carried out by more than one trader in the same Member State);
an infringement with a Union dimension (e.g. an infringement that affects all EU consumers).
It does not apply to intra-EU infringements.
EU-wide actions
Member States must cooperate with each other to prevent, detect, investigate and enforce the infringement of a trader in their own Member State.
The Member States must notify the Commission of any infringements that they consider to be widespread or widespread.
They must inform the Commission within 3 months of receiving a notification of an infringement.
If an infringement is widespread, the Member State must take all necessary measures to prevent or remedy it.
In the event of a Member State failing to do so, the Commission must take appropriate action to ensure the protection of the consumer.
Reporting
The Commission must report annually to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the enforcement priorities submitted by the Member States under this regulation and on the results of the investigations and enforcement actions carried out under the regulation.
Cooperation between the Commission and the competent authorities
The European Commission is responsible for:
coordinating the actions taken by the Commission, Member States and the Commission;
monitoring the implementation of the regulation;
assessing the effectiveness of the cooperation between Member States;
drawing up a list of priority actions to be prioritised;
preparing a report every 2 years on the implementation and results of enforcement actions;
sharing information on enforcement priorities.
Penalties
The following penalties are applicable:
a fine of up to EUR 100,000 for each infringement;
a maximum fine of EUR 500,000 if the infringement takes place in more than 1 Member State;
an additional fine of €500,000 in the case of a widespread infringement or widespread infringement with an EU dimension.
Exemptions
Directive 2014/17/EU and Directive 2014/92/EU (see summary) provide for the possibility for Member States to prohibit the sale of certain goods and services to a third party in the European Union (EU), subject to certain conditions. However, this prohibition does not extend to the sale, supply or use of these goods or services within the EU.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 provides for a review of the Regulation and its operational mechanisms and its implementation. This Regulation is part of this review.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
  (1) Member State: the country in which the infringement took place.
* Member States: the EU country where the infringements took place and where the infringement was committed.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member Statute
Official Journal
Regulation(EU) No 2195/2018
1.1.2019

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices (i.e. medical devices) — EU rules for their safety and quality
In vitro diagnostics (i-IVM)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 — in vitro diagnostic* medical devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework for the sale and placing on the market of in vitro diagnostic medical devices* (I-IVMs).
It aims to ensure a high level of safety and health whilst supporting innovation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the sale, manufacture, importation and distribution of I-VMs and their components.
It does not apply to:
in vitro diagnostic devices* and their accessories* intended for use in the human body;
medical devices intended only for the diagnosis and treatment of human beings;
diagnostic devices intended exclusively for the use of animals or plants; or
medical equipment intended only to be used in the animal or plant population.
Manufacturers and distributors
The manufacturer is responsible for ensuring that:
the products are manufactured in accordance with the requirements laid down in the regulation;
they comply with the EU’s national legislation;
the devices meet the requirements of the European Medicines Agency (EMSA);
they have been approved by the competent national authority in the EU country in which they are placed on sale; and
they fulfil the requirements set out in Annexes I and II and III of the regulation.
Distributors
they must ensure that the products they place on the EU-registered in another EU country conform with the regulation and comply with its requirements.
Importers and importers must comply with all the requirements listed in Annex IV and III, as well as with Annexes IV and V.
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts to amend the regulation in the light of scientific and technical progress.
Amendments to the regulation
Implementing acts
Amending Regulation (Euratom) 2019/856 amends Regulation (EC), which introduces a new type of electronic label for I-IV devices. It replaces the previous type of label, which was replaced by an electronic label. This new type is called an ‘AIDC label’. AIDC technologies include bar codes, smart cards, biometrics and RFID. The new label must be compatible with all EU countries’ national laws and regulations. It must be clearly visible and clearly distinguishable from other medical devices on sale in the same EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   Â  
In 2014, the European Commission adopted Directive 2014/40/EU on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member State in which in vitro medical devices are to be sold (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘In vitro Diagnostic Medical Devices’ on the Euratom website.
* KEY TERMS
* In vitro medical device: a medical device that can be used to diagnose, assess, monitor and evaluate the health and safety of an individual or a group of individuals. It is used to detect, diagnose, monitor, assess and monitor the effects of an illness or injury on the body or the environment, and to assess the effectiveness of medical treatment.

Summary 130:
Ensuring appliances and fittings comply with EU safety rules
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/856 — EU safety requirements for appliances and fitting
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that appliances and their fittings are safe and to ensure the safety of consumers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all electrical and electronic appliances, including:
electromagnetic compatibility (e.g. the compatibility of electrical appliances with electromagnetic compatibility devices);
appliances which are intended to be placed on the market and put into service in the EU;
equipment intended for the repair or maintenance of an appliance or a fitting.
It does not apply to products intended for sale in non-EU countries.
Importers and importers must ensure that:
the appliance or the fitting is accompanied by a CE marking and an EU declaration of conformity, as set out in Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on conformity assessment;
the manufacturer and importer provide the relevant information and documentation to the competent national authority of the EU country in which the appliance or fitting is to be sold;
instructions are written in a language which can be easily understood by the consumer;
importers are informed of any residual risks due to any shortcomings of the protection measures adopted and indicate whether any particular precautions are required.
Manufacturers must ensure the CE marking is affixed to the appliance and the fitting in accordance with the relevant national legislation.
The EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure conformity assessment procedures are carried out in conformity with this regulation.
EU countries must also ensure that the conformity assessment procedure is carried out by an independent body designated by the European Commission.
Conformity assessment procedure
The manufacturer must carry out a conformity assessment to check that the appliances or fittings meet all the essential requirements of the regulation. If the appliance is found to be in breach of these essential requirements, the manufacturer must inform the notified body of the reasons for its decision. The notified body must then take corrective measures and issue a certificate or approval decision.
If the manufacturer does not comply with the regulation, it must notify the EU countries of its decision and inform them of any corrective measures taken.
In addition, EU countries are required to take appropriate measures to prevent, detect and remedy any non-compliance.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
 This regulation is one of a series of measures designed to improve the safety and environmental protection of products in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
Electromagnetic Compatibility (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Electrical compatibility: the ability of a product to be used by electromagnetic means, such as a light bulb or a light switch, without the need for any additional equipment.
* Fitting: the part or part of a device which is incorporated into an appliance, including the appliance itself, the appliance’s accessories and the fittings.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 856/2015 of 12 July 2015 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the safety, conformity assessment and control of appliances and fitted products and repealing Council Directive 2009/142/EC (OJ L 96, 29.4.2015, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2016/852 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 131:
Cross-border transport of euro cash by road: cash-in-transit (CIT)
Cross-based transport of euros
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009 on the cross-border cross-Border transport of cash in the euro area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the transport by road of euro-denominated cash within the euro zone (Euro area).
It aims to improve the security of the euro and to ensure the smooth functioning of the single currency.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
cash in-vehicle (IBNS) transport between participating EU countries;
cross-border cash deliveries/pick-ups;
cash services of national central bank branches or CIT cash centres;
the servicing of off-premises ATMs or other types of cash dispensers.
It does not apply to the carriage of euro banknotes by road.
Participating EU countries may decide that only end-to-end IBNS may be used on their territory for the servicing of ATMs, provided that the same rules apply for domestic CIT transport.
CIT companies must:
ensure that:
the vehicle is of ordinary appearance;
there are at least two CIT security staff per vehicle;
none of the security staff wears a uniform.
The company operating the vehicle is authorised by the competent national authority;
inform the national authority of the country in which it is operating before the start of the transport of the cash;
provide a certificate of origin and destination of the vehicle and of the number of deliveries/picks-up that it may carry out.
EU countries must ensure that CIT vehicles comply with the following requirements:
a certificate of authorisation has been issued by a national authority confirming that the vehicle complies with all the requirements laid down in the regulation;
a CIT company is authorised to carry out the transport within the territory of an EU country;
at least one of the CIT staff must be a national of the EU country of destination;
an annual training module is to be organised for all CIT workers in accordance with the requirements set out in Annex II to the regulation.
Annex III sets out the conditions for the establishment of a national control centre for the operation of CIT companies operating the vehicles.
Each EU country must designate one or more national control centres to be responsible for the implementation of the regulation and for the coordination of its implementation with the other EU countries. The control centre must ensure:
that:
each CIT vehicle is equipped with an emergency number to contact the police authorities in the country where it is being used;
information is provided to the control centre on the conditions relating to the use of the emergency number;
each vehicle is accompanied by an emergency communication tool to allow contact to be made at any time with the control centres of the national authorities and with the competent authorities.
A national authority is responsible for:
monitoring the safety of the vehicles;
checking that the vehicles are being used correctly;
taking the necessary measures to prevent, detect and respond to any irregularities;
keeping a record of any infringements.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* CIT: a company that carries out cross-boundary cross-currency transport of money by road between participating countries.
* IBNS: a vehicle used to transport cash in cash in euro denominations of €1, €2, €5, €10, €20, €50, €100 and €500.

Summary 132:
Information to consumers on the safety and health of food and drink
Information on the safe and wholesome food and beverage information
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1234/2011 — information to consumers concerning the safety of foodstuffs and beverages
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It lays down rules on the information to be provided to consumers about the safety, health and nutrition of food, drinks and food ingredients.
It aims to ensure that food businesses are able to provide consumers with accurate and clear information on the health and safety of their food and drinks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
foods for which the labelling must include at least one or more of the following:
the name, type or category of the food or food ingredients;
the country or country of origin;
a description of the ingredients and the intended use of those ingredients; and
a nutrition declaration.
The information must be provided in a language easily understood by the consumer. It must not be hidden, obscured, detracted from or interrupted by any other written or pictorial matter or any other intervening material.
Labelling must be clearly visible, legible and clearly distinguishable from other information provided by the food business operators.
Information must be clear, understandable and not misleading.
Food businesses must ensure that the following information is clearly visible to the consumer:
a statement of the product’s intended use;
an indication of the quantity of alcohol and/or its alcohol content;
information on the intended for children and pregnant women (not recommended for children or pregnant women);
a warning on the potential risks to the health of infants, young children and the elderly;
instructions on the preparation and storage of food;
any other information that could mislead the consumer, such as a statement of health risks to infants or young children.
In addition, food businesses must indicate the origin of a food and the name of the place of production or place of sale where the food originates.
This must be accompanied by an indication in the format set out in Annex II to the regulation.
Annex III sets out the list of mandatory particulars that must be included in the nutrition declaration (Annex IV sets out a list of the mandatory particulars for each food type and category of food.
Directive 2005/29/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 May 2005 concerning unfair business-to-consumer commercial practices in the internal market (see summary) lays down certain aspects of the protection of consumers against misleading actions and omissions of information.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 12 December 2011. It had to become law in the EU countries by 12 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
Food business operators are responsible for ensuring that the information they provide to consumers is accurate, clear and understandable.
For more information, see:
‘Health and nutrition information’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Food business operator: any person or business that sells food or drinks to consumers, whether directly or indirectly, to a consumer or to a mass caterer.

Summary 133:
Issuance of euro coins and 2-euro commemorative coins: circulation and collector coins (Regulation (EC) No 1099/2011)
Issuation of euro coin and 2 euro commemorative coin (Regulating Council Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation 2011/1099/EC — Regulation on the issuance of euro notes and coins
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the issuing and circulation of euro banknotes and coins.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro. It does not apply to non-euro-area countries.
Issues
EU countries may issue 2- and 1-euro euro coins:
circulation coins intended for circulation, the denominations and technical specifications of which are laid down in Regulation (EC), and
collector coins, which are intended for collection that are not issued with a view to their entry into circulation.
The circulation coins are issued in denominations of 1.5 and 2.5 cents.
1.5-euro coins have the status of legal tender only in the issuing EU country.
Collector coins are not legal tender in the EU country in which they are issued. They are issued by the issuing country and are subject to limits per year and per issuing country for the number of issues of each type of coin.
2.2-coin designs
The 2-coin design is designed to commemorate a specific subject and may not be used in circulation coins. The design must not exceed 5 % of the cumulated total net value and volume of circulation coins issued by an EU country, taking into account only years with positive net issuance.
A limited number of 1-sized coins may be put on the market above face value if justified by the special quality of the coin, a special packaging or any additional services provided.
3.3-cent coins may also be issued, subject to certain conditions:
they must not have a shaped edge with fine scallops or a ‘Spanish flower’ shape.
4.4-centre coins may only be issued if they comply with the technical specifications laid down by the European Central Bank (ECB).
5.5.6-cent coin designs may be used only if they do not exceed the limits set by the ECB.
6.7.5 cent coins may not have the shape of a Spanish flower.
7.8.5% of the total number of circulating euro coins must be issued by a country in a calendar year.
8.9.10.2021 and
9.11.2022 and
10.12.2023 and
11.1.2024 and
12.2.2025 and
13.3.2026.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Council Regulation 2011/2010/EC has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The euro is the single currency of the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘The euro’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Circulation coins: coins that are intended to circulate in the euro area and have a value of €1 or €2.

Summary 134:
The European Social Fund (ESF) and its activities
The European social fund (ESSF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 laying down the mission and scope of the European social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s social fund and lays down specific rules for its funding.
It also sets out the specific types of activities that may be financed by the ESF.
KEY POINTS
The ESF supports the EU countries and the regions in order to support, for example, students, job creation, job mobility, the mobility of workers, social inclusion and social entrepreneurship.
The objectives of this regulation are to:
promote the better functioning of labour markets by enhancing the transnational geographical mobility of EU workers;
improve health and safety at work and increasing employability;
encourage the participation of those seeking asylum and refugees in the labour market;
increase participation of disadvantaged people such as the Roma in education and training;
contribute to combating poverty and combating social exclusion;
support the social inclusion of disadvantaged groups such as women, children and people with disabilities.
Funding
The regulation provides for the following types of funding:
investment priorities:
1.
Investment priority ‘community-led local development’;
2.
3.
Support under the investment priority "youth employment initiative (YEI);
4.
5.
Community-led development.
Eligibility of expenditure:
EU countries may decide to allocate a limited amount not exceeding 10% of the funds under the YEI to young persons residing in sub-regions which experience high youth unemployment levels and which are outside the eligible NUTS level 2 regions.
EU governments may also decide to grant grants to young people residing in regions which are not covered by a dedicated priority axis.
Participation:
participants in education/training (e.g. students, young people engaged in job searching, unemployed, inactive, people gaining a qualification upon leaving education or training, people seeking asylum or refugees, people who are vulnerable to social exclusion.
Data collection:
the European Commission must collect and store data for each priority axis;
the data must be collected in a computerised form based on a sample of EU countries’ national data systems;
participation of the partners referred to in Article 5 of Regulation (EC) No 1081/2006 in the implementation of the operational programmes may take the form of global grants as defined in Article 123(7).
The Commission must regularly evaluate the effectiveness, efficiency and impact of ESF support in combating poverty, in particular with regard to disadvantaged people.
Financing:
The EU countries may allocate up to 40 % of the specific allocation for the specific priority axis to the following:
education/training;
job creation;
social inclusion;
migration;
employment;
health and safety;
transnational mobility;
education and training.
In addition, the Commission may provide financial support to the European network of employment services (EURES) in relation to recruitment and related information, advice and guidance services at national and cross-border level.
Rights and obligations of the EU institutions:
Each EU country must designate one or more bodies responsible for the management of the financial resources allocated to it under this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2014 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The social fund was established under the Treaty on European Union (TFEU).
For more information, see:
‘European Social Fund’ on the European Commission's website.
