Summary 135:
European statistics on population and vital events (e.g. birth, death, migration and death)
Eastern Europe (European statistics)
Establishing a common framework for the development, production and dissemination of European statistics on demographic statistics (e-statistics)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 223/2009/EC — the European statistical framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common EU-wide framework for developing, producing and disseminating statistics on the European Union’s (EU) population and on vital events.
KEY POINTS
Each year, EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the total population at national level and on the number of deaths and births in each EU country, as well as information on:
live births by sex, month of occurrence, live birth-order, mother‘s age, country of birth, mother's country of citizenship and mother's region of residence;
deaths by age, sex, year of birth and country of residence.
The data are used to assess the long-term sustainability and quality of public finances of EU countries’ public finances on the basis of population projections produced by Eurostat.
Each EU country must transmit the data to Eurostat in accordance with the NUTS classification, which is based on the population of the country in which the data are to be transmitted. The data are broken down by territorial unit (i.e. the country of origin or country of nationality).
Eurostat then forwards the information to the Economic and Financial Affairs Committee (EACFiscal Affairs Committee, which assesses the sustainability of EU public finances and the quality of the public finances based on Eurostat statistics.
EU countries must ensure that the information they transmit to the Commission is accurate, comparable and up-to-date. The Commission then forwards this information to EAFRICOM (the European Agency for the Safety and Health of the European Communities) which then forwards it to the European Statistical System of Central Statistics (ESSC).
The Commission is responsible for examining the data transmitted by the EU countries and for ensuring that they comply with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE?
The regulation entered into force on the twentieth day following that of its publication in the Official Journal.
BACKGROUND
Annual demographic statistics are fundamental for the study and definition of a wide range of policies, with particular regard to social and economic issues, at national and regional level.
For more information, see:
‘Population’ on the Eurostat website.
* KEY TERMS
* Total population: the total number of people in an EU country.

Summary 136:
Labelling and marking of textile products
Labeling and marking: labelling and markings
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the labelling of textiles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules to ensure that textile products are clearly identifiable by their fibre names and the descriptions of their composition.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all textile products, including:
wool, carded cotton, cotton twine, unbleached cotton, rayon, woolen twine and woolen tows;
cotton and rayon;
fibre mixtures of wool, cotton and rayonet;
wax used in mountaineering and water sports.
Labelling
Textile products must bear the following information:
the fibre name (e.g. ‘cotton’, ‘wool’), ‘carded cotton’ or ‘unbleached’;
the composition of the fibre;
a description of the fibres used in the product.
This information must be accompanied by a picture of the product and a description of its characteristics.
The information must not be confused with the information provided by the EU countries’ national authorities.
EU countries must ensure that any information supplied by the economic operators is clearly distinguishable from the information contained in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATED REGULATIONS APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
It had to become law in the European Union (EU) Member States by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Textiles’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Textile product: any item of clothing or material intended to be used for the production, presentation or sale of a textile.

Summary 137:
European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF) — rules for the European Maritime and fisheries Fund and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) (revised by Regulation (EU) 2019/1380)
European Maritime & Fisheries Fund — rules governing the European maritime and fisheries fund (EMDF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019 / 1380/2013 — rules laying down the rules on the EU’s contribution to the common fisheries fund and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 1083/2006
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and procedures for the EU's contribution to support for the EAFF.
KEY POINTS
The EAFDF is the European Union's (EU's) main fisheries fund. It is funded by the European Commission and is managed by the EU countries.
The CFP is the main instrument of the EU fisheries policy. It aims to support the sustainable development of the marine environment and the sustainable management of fisheries in the EU.
It has a budget of €2.8 billion for the period 2014-2027.
Funding
The European Maritime Fund is the largest fisheries fund in the world. It supports projects in the field of fisheries and fisheries management. It has an annual budget of around €1.5 billion.
Its main objectives are to:
promote sustainable growth and jobs;
improve the quality of fishing and aquaculture;
encourage the sustainable use of marine resources;
contribute to the conservation of marine life;
support the sustainable fishing;
increase marine biodiversity and marine protected areas;
reduce the impact of fishing on the environment;
ensure the sustainability of fishing fleets;
provide financial support for projects that contribute to marine research and development;
facilitate cooperation between EU countries on the implementation of marine research.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EMFF is one of the three main instruments of the European Fisheries Fund. The other instruments are:
the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD);
the EU Fisheries Fund for Scientific Research and Innovation (FARE), which supports research and innovation (R&I) in the fields of fisheries;
the Fund for the Protection of the Marine Environment (FPM), which provides financial support to the European Marine Environment Agency (EMEA).
For more information, see:
‘Funding for sustainable growth’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work's website.
* KEY TERMS
* EAFRD: the European Fund for Strategic Investments (EMF).
* FARE: this is the fund's main instrument for providing financial support. It provides the EU with the financial resources it needs to implement its fisheries policy objectives.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 138:
Insurance-based investment products — key information and product information
Insurance - key information & product information and marketing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1095/2010 on the marketing of investment products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that investment products are clearly identifiable and clearly distinguishable from other investment products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all investment products, whether or not they are covered by insurance.
It does not apply to investment products that are not covered by the EU’s financial stability directive (see summary).
It applies to products which are not directly linked to the financial markets and which are designed to provide investors with investment solutions that do not involve buying or holding assets themselves.
The key information document (KIC) is a document that contains information on a product, such as:
the investment objectives and risks;
a description of how the product works;
the risks associated with the investment product;
investment objectives and investment risks.
Key information document
The KIC must be clearly identifiable, separate from any marketing communications and must contain:
a summary of the investment objectives, risks and investment objectives;
an indication of the expected investment outcome;
information on the risks and benefits associated with an investment product, including a description of the risk/recovery ratio and a risk-benefit ratio;
how the product is expected to perform in the event of a significant change in the value of the underlying investment assets;
instructions on how to invest in the investment assets.
A KICs must be accompanied by an English translation of the product information provided by the manufacturer.
An EU country must ensure that the KIC is translated into a language easily understood by the end-users.
EU countries must also ensure that:
inform the European Insurance Information Office (EIOPA) and the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) of all the products covered by this regulation;
ensure that the European Commission adopts the technical standards applicable to all products within the scope of this regulation.
Regulatory technical standards
The European Commission must adopt technical standards relating to the technical specifications applicable to the products listed in Annex I to the regulation. The Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on whether these technical standards are compatible with the European System of Central Banks’ (ESCB) classification of investment instruments and with those of the European Investment Bank (EIB).
The Commission must also report on whether those technical standards provide for reliable and accurate calculations for all products in its scope.
Implementation of the regulation
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 lays down the rules for the implementation of the Regulation.
Amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/856 introduces a new type of product information document to be used by the EIOPA. This document must contain the following information:
information about the investment objective and the risks involved in investing in investment products;
actual investment results;
explanations for investment decisions;
procedures to be followed when making investment decisions and investment strategies;
recommendations for the use of investment strategies and investment instruments;
adoption of investment policies and investment procedures;
assessment of risks and risks related to investment instruments.
In addition, the regulation sets out the responsibilities of the EU country responsible for supervising the supervision of the market for investment products (EU country).
Amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2017/1369
The amending regulation has been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2011 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Investment product: a product that provides investors with a means of investment with a view to providing them with investment results.
* Insurance investment product: an investment solution that is not a direct connection with the financial market and does not involve the purchase or holding of an asset.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Resolution of failing banks and the Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM) — rules for the early intervention and resolution of failed banks
Resolving failing banks
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 575/2014 — rules on the resolution and resolution powers of the European system of banking supervision
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on how the European System of Banking Supervision (ESM) works.
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU), the European Central Bank (ECB) and the national authorities involved in resolving failing banks are able to resolve failing banks quickly and effectively.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out rules on:
the structure of the ESCM;
the role of the ECB and national authorities in the resolution process; and
the rules on cooperation and information exchange between the ECB, national authorities and the ECB.
The ESCM is the EU’s single resolution mechanism. It aims to:
ensure that failing banks can be brought to resolution as quickly as possible;
provide a single point of entry for resolving a failing bank;
facilitate cooperation between national authorities, the ECB or the national competent authority, the Board and the relevant national resolution authorities;
promote the use of resolution tools;
improve the efficiency of the resolution procedure;
reduce the time it takes for banks to come to a resolution;
protect client funds and client assets;
increase the effectiveness of the system of resolution; and improve the transparency of the process.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLACE IN FORCE?
  
It has applied since 19 August 2014 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 19 August 2016.
BACKGROUND
The ECB is responsible for the resolution of failing banking institutions. It is assisted by the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA), which is the competent national authority in the Member States.
For more information, see:

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions at European Union airports (until 14 June 2023) (see Annex II)
Noise - noisier air transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — noise-related restrictions at airports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to reduce the impact of noise on human health and the environment at EU airports.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all airports in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to airports in non-EU countries.
It requires EU countries to assess the noise situation at their airports and to adopt measures to reduce it.
EU countries must ensure that:
noise performance information is reliable, accurate and up-to-date;
airport authorities are transparent and transparent;
measures are proportionate, proportionate and non-discriminatory;
there is no discrimination based on nationality or identity;
no one airport is excluded from the scope of the regulation.
Airport authorities must take the necessary measures to:
ensure that air traffic management network capacity at an airport is not affected by noise;
monitor the performance of the airport, including the number of aircraft operating at the airport;
implement noise abatement measures to achieve the objectives set out in Annex II to the regulation;
take all appropriate measures to ensure that the noise levels are reduced or eliminated;
establish and maintain an effective monitoring system.
The European Union Aviation Safety Agency (EASA) is responsible for monitoring and evaluating the implementation of the measures adopted by the EU countries and the EASA.
Exemption for aircraft operations of an exceptional nature
The competent authorities may, on a case-by-case basis, grant an exemption for an aircraft operating in an airport in an exceptional situation.
Where an EU country grants an exemption, it must notify the competent authorities of the other EU countries, the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for the decision.
If an airport does not comply with the regulation’s requirements, it may be subject to an operating restriction.
A regular assessment of the situation at airports is carried out every 2 years.
An airport is assessed every 5 years and the results of the assessment are published in the Official Journal of the European Commission.
In the case of an airport which is subject to a regular assessment, the competent authority must:
assess the impact on the health and safety of the population and of the environment of the area concerned;
identify the measures to be taken by the airport in order to achieve its objectives;
make a decision on the application of the decision within 6 months of receiving the information;
draw up an action plan to address the issues arising from the assessment;
decide whether to adopt any new measures.
Amendments to Regulation (EC) No 2096/2005
The Commission has adopted a number of delegated acts to implement the regulation, including:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/849 amending Regulation (Euratom) No 691/2010 laying down a performance scheme for air navigation services and network functions and amending Regulations (EC, EFTA) No 1296/2004 and (EC), No 1297/2005 laying down common requirements for the provision of air navigation and network services (OJ L 201, 3.8.2019, pp. 1-8)
Deregulation (EC)(1) 2019 (see summary) amends Regulation (EA) No 1099/2013 laying down the conditions for the exercise of the powers conferred on the Commission in relation to the exercise by the Commission of the power to adopt delegated acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 14 June 2018 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 14 June 2021.
BACKGROUND
 The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the sound-level management of air traffic at EU countries’ airports. It complements Directive 2002/49/EC on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States in the field of noise.
For more information, see:
Airports (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Aeronautical noise: the noise emitted by an aircraft as a result of the operation of the aircraft.
* Noise: the sound emitted by the aircraft in the air as a consequence of the operating activities of an aircraft, in accordance with the rules of the International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO).
* Noise: the level of noise emitted from an aircraft in an aircraft at which an aircraft is operating.

  Егранска ганданна банка, гългарка  глана



Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings in the EU
Cross-Border insolvencies in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 — cross-border proceedings in insolvencations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to improve the application of the law of the EU in the event of a company’s bankruptcy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
bankruptcy proceedings;
securitisation proceedings; and
secondary bankruptcy proceedings.
It does not apply to the following situations:
the liquidation of an insolvent company;
the winding-up of a business; or
the sequestration of a bank or other financial institution.
Application of the regulation
It applies to cases where:
a company is insolvent;
it has ceased or is likely to cease to exist;
its assets have been sold or are being liquidated;
a debtor has not been able to pay its creditors;
an insolvent business has been declared insolvent or has ceased to exist.
The debtor has the right to seek a court order to prevent or prevent the bankruptcy of the company or to prevent it from going into liquidation.
In addition, the regulation provides for a number of other possibilities, such as:
group coordination proceedings (i.e. the formation of a group of persons or bodies to coordinate the course of the proceedings in order to avoid bankruptcy proceedings);
the establishment of a court-supervised group coordination proceedings, in which all the parties are represented by the same person or body;
closer cooperation between the parties in the case of the same company or business;
confidentiality of the information exchanged by the parties during the group coordination process;
guaranteeing the protection of employees’ claims against the insolvent companies;
appointment of a bankruptcy trustee;
protection of the interests of the employees of the debtor;
recognition and enforceability of judgments.
Amending Regulation (EU-wide)
The amending regulation introduces a new type of group coordination procedure. It allows for the creation of a ‘group coordination procedure’ in cases where the debtor is unable or unwilling to participate in the proceedings themselves. This procedure allows the parties to agree on the terms and conditions of participation of the parties.
Participation in group coordination procedures is voluntary. The parties are not obliged to take any of the following actions:
participation by the debtor in the main or secondary proceedings, including the appointment of the bankruptcy trustee, the appointment by the court of the country in which the proceedings have been opened, and the choice of the liquidator;
adoption of a plan to avoid the proceedings, which must be approved by the competent court of that country.
Cooperation between parties
The parties are required to take all necessary steps to ensure that the proceedings are conducted in a way that ensures the proper functioning of the system and that the parties’ interests are respected.
A group coordination plan must be drawn up in accordance with the national law of each EU country. It must contain the following information:
details of the nature and content of the agreement between the debtor and the parties;
information on the nature, content and duration of the undertaking;
details on the steps to be taken by the party to avoid or avoid secondary proceedings. The undertaking must be made in writing.
Appointment of an administrator
The competent court appoints an administrator, who is responsible for:
appointing the administrator, appointing the administrator and appointing the person responsible for the administration of the case;
implementing the regulation, in particular by:
establishing a national insolvence register;
drawing up a list of the persons and bodies covered by the regulation;
designating the person or bodies whose interests are to be represented by an administrator or the person in charge of the administration;
amending the list of persons who may be appointed by the appointed person.
Implementing Regulation (EEC) 2019/856
The implementing regulation amends the implementing regulation and introduces the following new rules:
instructions to be given to the parties before the opening of proceedings (main or secondary);
rules to be adopted by the European Court of Justice;
rules on the right of access to the court and to the information to be made available to the public.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is one of the main instruments of the European Commission‘s effort to modernise and streamline the national laws of the Member State in which it is applicable.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 142:
Limitation of liability for maritime claims
Limitations of liability in the event of maritime accidents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 on the application of the International Convention on Limitation of Liability for Maritime Claims
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that insurance companies are not liable for damage caused to passengers in maritime accidents.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the liability of insurance companies for the carriage of passengers and their luggage by sea and to the loss of or damage to their luggage.
It does not apply to:
carriage of passengers by sea;
the carriage of goods by sea.
Limits of liability
Carriers and their performing carriers are liable for:
death and personal injury;
damage to passengers’ personal property;
loss of and damage to the luggage of passengers.
The liability of the carrier and the performing carrier is based on the following principles:
the carrier is liable for the total amount of damage caused by the passenger, including any loss or damage resulting from the negligence of the passenger or the negligence or recklessness of a passenger.
If the passenger fails to comply with this principle, he or she is presumed to have acted with the intent to cause such damage, or recklessly and with knowledge that such damage would probably result.
In relation to the carriage performed by a performing carrier, the aggregate of the amounts recoverable from the carriers and performing carriers and from their servants and agents acting within the scope of their employment shall not exceed the highest amount which could be awarded against either the carrier or the performing carriers under this Convention, but none of the persons mentioned shall be liable for a sum in excess of the limit applicable to him or her.
Liability of the insurer
The insurer is liable only for its part of the total liability of a carrier or performing carrier. The insurer is not entitled to benefit from the benefit of any other person’s insurance policy.
Insurers are also liable for any damage to passengers:
damage caused by an accident;
death or personal injury resulting from an act or omission by a passenger;
luggage loss resulting from a delay in the arrival of the luggage;
delays resulting from labour disputes;
delay resulting from any action taken to prevent the luggage from being re-delivered.
An insurance company is not liable if:
it has not complied with its obligations under the regulation;
it does not have the financial means to cover the risks involved;
in the absence of an insurance policy, it is not obliged to cover these risks.
Amendments to the convention
The convention has been amended several times, most recently by the Protocol of 2002 to the Convention. The most recent amendment was adopted in 2015.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The Convention is one of a series of international agreements that aim to protect passengers against maritime accidents and to ensure a high level of protection for them.
For more information, see:
‘Limitations on liability’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Carriage: a means of transport by sea in which a passenger is transferred from one place to another, usually by land, by means of the means of a vessel or by sea craft.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratom of 13 December 2008 on the exercise of the right of the Member States to express their consent to be bound by the Convention relating to the Carriage of Passengers and their Luggage by Sea, 1976 (OJ L 347, 14.12.2008, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2016/849 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 143:
Framework for the development of freight corridors in the European Union (EU)
Framework Framework for the Development of freight networks
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2010 on the framework for the creation of a European freight corridor
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for establishing and managing freight corridors* in the EU.
It sets out the general objectives and tasks of the European Commission and the EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following freight corridors:
the crossing by the freight corridor of the territory of at least three EU countries, or of two EU countries if the distance between the terminals is greater than 500 km;
the consistency of the freight corridors with the TEN (Trans-European Transport Network).
The objectives and scope of the framework are set out in the annex to the regulation.
The framework for each freight corridor is based on the following principles:
mutual benefit;
non-discriminatory access to international rail services;
quality of service;
consistency with the other rail networks in the area;
fair competition between the different rail networks.
Governance
Each EU country must establish an executive board responsible for defining the general objective and taking the measures necessary to achieve the objectives of the corridor. The executive board is assisted by a management board. The management board takes its decisions, including decisions regarding its legal status, the establishment of its organisational structure, resources and staffing, on the basis of mutual consent of the infrastructure managers.
A management board is responsible for the allocation of infrastructure capacity.
Investment in the freight freight corridors
The management board determines the investment plans for investment in freight corridors. The investment plans must be approved by the management board and the executive board. Investment planning must take into account the needs of the users and the economic situation of the area concerned.
Quality of service
The executive board must ensure that:
traffic is carried out in accordance with the EU’s legislation on the carriage of goods and passengers by rail;
freight traffic is handled by a single railway company or a single rail company;
services are provided by one or more rail companies operating on the same railway network.
Management of the network
The EU countries must ensure:
that:
fees are paid by the operators of the rail infrastructure concerned;
finance is provided by the European Investment Bank (EIB);
the cost of the investment is borne by the railway companies;
transparency is ensured;
information is shared between the national authorities and the bodies responsible for monitoring the performance of the railway infrastructure.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 7 July 2010.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Freight corridor: an international rail route which provides a means of transport for goods, passengers and freight between two or more EU countries or between them and a non-EU country.
* TEN: the top 10 busiest freight routes in the world, as defined by the International Classification System (ICS).
*MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratom of 27 June 2010 on the Framework for establishing a European Freight Corridor (OJ L 183, 7.7.2010, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2018/856 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  (1)
  The European Commission adopts Regulation (EU, EC) No 652/2010 of 7 June 2010 laying down the general principles and procedures for the establishment and management of a freight corridor and amending Regulations (EC, EFTA and Euratom) No 496/2009, (EC and EFTA) No 1052/2009 and (EU), (EC).
last update 14.01.2020



Summary 144:
Road transport: common rules for road transport operators (Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 — common rules on the authorisation of road transport undertakings
Road transport : common rules in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2016/852 — common EU rules on road transport
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out common rules to ensure that road transport companies are authorised to operate in the field of road transportation.
It aims to improve the quality of road traffic and to improve transparency in the internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all road transport enterprises in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to undertakings operating in the transport of goods by road.
Authorisation
An undertaking is authorised to engage in the occupation of the road transport operator if it meets the following conditions:
it satisfies the conditions laid down in the regulation:
its establishment is established in an EU country;
it is established by a competent authority designated by the competent authority of the EU country in which it is established;
its activities are carried out in accordance with the rules set out in this regulation;
the authorisation is valid for a period of 5 years;
there are no restrictions on the number of persons authorised to carry out the activities of the undertaking.
Certificates of good repute and equivalent documents
The competent authorities of EU countries must recognise the following certificates and documents as proof of professional competence:
a certificate issued by an authority of one or more EU countries;
a declaration made by an undertaking established in another EU country and valid for 5 years or more;
certificates issued by a body recognised by a national authority established in a non-EU country.
EU countries may refuse to recognise certificates or documents if they do not comply with the above conditions.
Procedure for the suspension and withdrawal of authorisations
An EU country may suspend or withdraw an authorisation if it considers that an undertaking no longer fulfils the requirements of the regulation. It must inform the Commission of the reasons for its decision and of the measures to be taken if it is found that the applicant no longer meets these requirements.
Penalties
The Commission must draw up a list of categories, types and degrees of seriousness of serious infringements of EU rules which, in addition to those listed in Annex IV, may lead to the loss of the company’s authorisation, or to the suspension or withdrawal of its authorisation.
The penalties provided for are effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
National electronic register
Each EU country must keep a national electronic register of all undertakings which have been authorised by its competent authority.
This register must be made publicly accessible to the public and must contain:
the names and addresses of all the undertakings with which it has been granted authorisation;
all the information relating to each transport operation, and in particular those relating to the vehicle, the driver, the goods and luggage;
any changes to the approved documents;
details of the number and type of vehicles and their components (chassis, engine, transmission system, braking system, etc.);
the number, type and number of journeys per vehicle, per kilometre, per journey or per tonne of goods transported;
information on the costs per vehicle and per journey of each operation, including the total cost of each journey per vehicle.
In addition, the Commission must establish an EU-wide list of undertakings that have been suspended or withdrawn from authorisation because they have not complied with this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 4 December 2008 and had to become law in all EU countries by 4 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to modernise the regulation, including:
Directive 96/26/EC of the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the harmonisation of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions of the Member States relating to road transport (see summary);
Decision 1999/468/EC on the comparability of vocational training qualifications between the EU countries (OJ L 199, 31.7.1999, pp. 56-57);
Regulation 2007/849/EC (see amending acts) (see below).
KEY TERMS
  * Authorisation: a decision by an EU authority to authorise an undertaking to pursue the occupation or to provide services in the activity of transport.
* Authorised: a certificate or other document issued by the authority of a Member State to which an undertaking has been authorised to provide transport services.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council delegated Regulation (EEC) 2015/856 of 4 December 2015 laying down common rules concerning the exercise of the right of establishment and the right to provide road transport services (



Summary 145:
Social security — mutual recognition of claims between EU countries’ social security institutions
Social security – mutual recognition in cases of unemployment and long-term care
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the mutual recognition and recognition of social security claims between Member States
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the way in which social security benefits are shared between the social security systems of the Member States.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU citizens who are entitled to benefit from social security in more than one Member State, irrespective of their place of residence or where they live or work.
It does not apply to people who are covered by the national social security legislation of their country of residence.
Beneficiaries must be able to access benefits only from their Member State of residence if they are:
employed or self-employed;
living in another Member State (i.e. it is permanent or of an ad hoc or temporary nature);
employed; or
employed in a non-EU country (e.g. not in the EU country where they are resident).
It also applies to:
people covered by national social protection legislation;
self-employed people;
persons who are not self employed;
people who are unemployed;
those who have left the European Union (EU country of employment).
The following are excluded from the scope of the regulation:
non-EU nationals;
individuals who are resident in a Member State other than the one in which is the subject of its national legislation.
Persons who do not covered by this regulation.
People covered by Regulation (EU countries may not benefit from the regulation if they do not live in the same Member State as the person covered by it. However, they may be entitled to benefits from the Member State in which they are insured, provided that these periods do not overlap.
In addition, the regulation provides for a number of additional rights, including:
reimbursement of claims;
recovery of claims in cash or in kind;
recovery of claims on the basis of actual expenditure;
the right to be informed of any changes to the legislation applicable to their case;
access to the information necessary to calculate unemployment benefits, in particular the salary or professional income received.

In 2014, the European Commission adopted a regulation (EU Implementing Regulation) laying down common rules for the application of the basic Regulation.
The new rules apply from 1 January 2016. The regulation repeals Regulation (EC), which had to become law in all Member States by 1 January 2017.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND

Summary 146:
Statistical data collection in the fields of public health and health and safety at work (2008-2013)
Statistical information on public and private health
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2008 — statistical data collection and dissemination in the areas of public and public health
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish a European statistical system for the collection of statistics on the causes of death and the quality of health care in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to the EU’s Member States (1), Iceland, Norway and Switzerland.
It applies to data collected by the European Commission (Eurostat) and the European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS).
EU countries are required to ensure that the data collected are comparable to those collected in other EU countries.
Data collection
Eurostat collects the data in the following areas:
public health
health care
health and social protection
social protection
preventive and curative measures (e.g. prevention, diagnosis, treatment, monitoring, etc.).
The data are collected by Eurostat on the following subjects:
deaths (deaths up to the age of 28 months and deaths up to and including neonatal deaths);
prevention, diagnosis and treatment of health problems;
treatment of physical and psychosocial health problems.
The aim of this domain is to provide comparable, comparable and comparable data and information on all the causes and consequences of death.
Health care
The Commission is responsible for collecting the data for all the subjects covered by this regulation. It is assisted by the EDPS, the European Monitoring Centre for Health and Social Policy (EMC), the European Health Statistics Office (EHSSO) and Eurostat.
Quality
The quality of the health care is considered in the data collection, as is the level of protection of the personal data of EU citizens.
Evaluation of the data
The evaluation of the results of the regulation is carried out by the Eurostat, the EPCS and the ESDS.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULARATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
  (European Commission)
The European Statistical System (ESS) is the main instrument for collecting and disseminating health information and knowledge in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Health statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
KEY TERMS
* Public health: statistics on health, social protection and preventive measures.
* Social protection: measures taken to protect the health and well-being of people, especially children and vulnerable groups, such as the elderly, vulnerable groups such as pregnant women, children with disabilities and the disabled, and their families, and the vulnerable groups (such as the disabled and the elderly).
* Health care: the provision of quality, safe and high-quality health care, including the assessment and assessment of the quality and safety of health services.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егланска гранда барка, гълганка  гарна какова,  халка (OJ L 127, 1.4.2009, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 752/08 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 147:
Data on job vacancies
Data on employment vacancies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — production of job vacancy statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It lays down the rules for the production of EU-wide job vacancy data.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that EU countries provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with reliable, comparable and up-to-date data on the employment situation in the European Union (EU).
The data cover all economic activities defined by the common classification system for economic activities in the EU (NACE) in force, except for the activities of households as employers and activities of extraterritorial organisations and bodies.
EU countries must compile the data with reference to specific reference dates, which are determined by Eurostat in accordance with the regulation’s technical specifications.
Eurostat must transmit the data to Eurostat within 3 months of the end of the quarter in which the data are to be collected. The data must be transmitted in the form of:
quarterly data;
year-by-year data; and
seasonally adjusted data*.
The statistical programme committee is consulted by the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of this regulation.
The Commission is responsible for ensuring the quality and reliability of the data transmitted by EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The Regulation is one of a series of measures designed to improve the quality of EU statistics. The other measures include:
the creation of a common statistical framework for EU countries’ statistical information;
the harmonisation of national statistical systems,
the establishment of a European statistical system for the collection and transmission of data on economic activities (the European Statistical System for Employment and Social Affairs (ESSA);
the introduction of the European Employment Statistics System (ESS), which is based on the NACE classification system.
For more information, see:
‘Job vacancy statistics’ on Eurostat's website.

Summary 148:
Motor vehicles using hydrogen: type-approval and test procedures for hydrogen components and systems (Regulation (EC) No 596/2008)
Motor vehicles with hydrogen: test procedures and certification
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2008/43/EC on the type–approval of hydrogen for motor vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
  
It lays down common technical requirements for the type‑approval, testing and certification of hydrogen components* and systems* for use in motor vehicles.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
motor vehicles designed to use compressed (gaseous) hydrogen;
compulsory hydrogen storage or usage;
new forms of hydrogen storage* and additional hydrogen components;
vehicles with a propulsion system.
It does not apply to hydrogen components, other than containers, which are intended to be used for the storage or use of hydrogen.
Requirements for the installation of hydrogen systems
Vehicles must have the following characteristics:
a propulsion system capable of operating at the maximum allowable working pressure (MAWP) and then back to atmospheric pressure within a short period of time.
The propulsion system must have electrical continuity with the vehicle’s earth.
A vehicle must be able to operate at a maximum working pressure of no more than 1.5 atmospheres above atmospheric pressure.
Annexes I and II of the regulation set out the requirements for type–applicable test procedures to be followed by the European Member States.
Member States must ensure that the requirements are met by the Member States and that the competent national authority carries out the necessary checks.
If a Member State considers that a hydrogen component or system does not comply with the regulation, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for its decision and inform the Commission, which may adopt measures to amend the regulation.
In addition, the Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts to ensure that:
the regulation does not impede the free movement of hydrogen between Member States;
the Member States do not impose disproportionate burdens on the development and testing of new types of hydrogen; and
the Commission is not obliged to adopt any measures that are contrary to the objectives of this regulation, in order to protect the safety of the public or the environment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
Fuel-powered motor vehicles are becoming increasingly common in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Alternative fuels’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Motor vehicle: a vehicle designed to operate on the internal market.
* Propulsion system: a system consisting of a fuel cell or a separate propulsion system that supplies electricity to the engine and/or hydrogen to the vehicle.
ACT
Council Directive 2008/42/EC of 9 July 2008 relating to the type‐approval procedure for hydrogen (OJ L 183, 12.8.2008, pp. 1–8)
Successive amendments to Directive 2008 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 149:
Common rules for the internal market in natural gas (common rules on network access and third party access services)
Common rules governing network access
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2009 — common rules for access to the natural gas transmission networks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down basic principles and rules regarding network access* and third-party access services, congestion management, transparency, balancing and the trading of gas capacity rights.
It aims to promote the completion and functioning of the EU’s internal market for natural gas and cross-border trade.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all gas transmission, storage and liquefied natural gas system operators (TSS) in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to the operators of natural gas pipelines or to natural gas storage and LNG systems.
Access to the gas market
Transmission system operators must:
provide the public with all the technical information necessary to gain effective network access;
ensure that network users have access to all the essential services they need;
promote cooperation between the ENTSO for Gas, the European Network of Transmission System Operators for Gas (ETSO for Gas), which is set up by Directive 2009/73/EC (see summary).
The European Commission is responsible for drawing up a Community-wide network development plan for gas. It must include a priority list of priority areas for the development of gas infrastructure.
The Commission must adopt a network code for each of the priority areas. This code must be adopted by 1 July 2014. The Commission must submit it to the European Agency for Safety and Health at the European Commission for its opinion within 6 months of its adoption.
Ensuring transparency
Each TSO for gas must publish at least annually, by a predetermined deadline, all planned maintenance periods that might affect network users’ access to gas and all the operational information that is necessary for them to make an informed decision.
Transparency
Each operator must ensure that:
the information they provide to the public is accurate, comparable and up-to-date;
transparency is based on the best available technical information;
information is provided in a transparent and non-discriminatory manner;
the public is informed of any changes to the network code.
Trading of capacity rights
Each transmission system operator must take reasonable steps to allow capacity rights to be freely tradable and to facilitate such trade in an open, transparent and transparent manner, in line with the principles set out in Regulation (EU) No 1775/2005 on the European network of transmission system operators for gas, and in accordance with Regulation (Euratom) No 652/2014 on the principles of market access and access services.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    данска гаранка  барганна каканда бъларка, годаланца народна and горалказна (бальна  харна) бодкакса ванта заки блакка (нарсабка) and  гультагнажа (калика).
BACKGROUND
The gas market is one of the most competitive in the world and the regulation aims to ensure that it remains so.
For more information, see:
Natural gas (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: the person or body responsible for the operation of the gas transmission system.
* Natural gas pipelines: the means by which natural gas is transported from one place to another, including the transmission of gas to, from or to the end of a gas storage or LNG storage facility.

Summary 150:
Agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries on matters of civil justice
Agreements with non-European Union (EU) countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 593/2009 — mutual legal assistance in civil matters between Member States and third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure to authorise an EU country to amend an existing agreement or to negotiate and/or conclude an agreement with a third country on matters relating to civil justice.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to agreements concerning particular matters falling, entirely or partly, within the scope of the regulation.
It does not apply to:
contractual obligations;
non-contractual agreements;
agreements relating to the exercise of the rights and obligations of EU citizens;
obligations of EU countries in respect of the protection of intellectual property rights.
Amending an agreement
An EU country may amend an agreement or negotiate an agreement relating to matters falling within its competence if:
the agreement does not contain a clause providing for either:
recognition of the EU’s competence in the field of civil law; or
the EU country is not a party to the agreement;
an EU country does not fulfil its duty of loyal cooperation with the EU country concerned.
The EU country must notify the European Commission of its intention to amend or negotiate the agreement within 30 days of receiving the notification. The Commission must assess the agreement and, if necessary, make a reasoned decision on its application.
Before authorising the opening of formal negotiations, the Commission may propose negotiating guidelines and may request the inclusion of particular clauses in the agreement.
Authorisation to conclude the agreement
If the Commission considers that the agreement is not ready for conclusion, it may decide to open formal negotiations with the country concerned, or to postpone the conclusion of the agreement until it is ready to conclude it.
Where the Commission concludes that the negotiations are not ready, it can authorise the agreement to be concluded without delay.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The Regulation entered into force on 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Mutual legal assistance*: a legal agreement between two or more EU countries on the basis of which one or more of the parties agrees to cooperate in a specific field.
* Non-binding agreement: an agreement between a party and a non-party to an international or bilateral agreement on the interpretation, interpretation and application of the laws of a third party.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratom of 7 July 2009 on the Mutual Legal Assistance in Civil Matters between the Member States of the European Union and the Republic of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland and repealing Council Regulation (Euratom) No 1049/2001

Summary 151:
Statistics on migration and asylum: collection and analysis of Community statistics (2007-2013)
Statistics on immigration and asylum (2007 - 2013)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 311/76 on the collection, analysis and dissemination of statistics in the field of migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure the production of comprehensive statistics necessary for the development of fair and effective EU policies on migration.
It lays down detailed rules for the application of Council Regulation (EU) No 343/2003 (see summary).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to EU countries’ national statistics on international migration, usually resident population and acquisition of citizenship, and to non-EU nationals who have in fact left the territory of the EU.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with the following statistics:
the total number of persons who have entered the EU and those who have left the EU, as a result of an administrative or judicial decision or act;
the number of transfers of persons between EU countries;
total number of asylum seekers who have been taken back or taken charge of an asylum seeker;
information on the number of requests for information made by EU countries to the European Migration Office (EMO);
the numbers of transfers to which the decisions referred to in point 1 and 2 are based;
data on the residence permits and residence of third-country nationals.
The data are to be collected in the form of:
records of administrative and judicial actions;
registers relating to administrative actions; and
statistics of the population of persons or of a particular sub-group of persons;
censuses;
sample surveys;
other appropriate sources.
Eurostatistics must be supplied within 3 months of the end of the reference year and must be provided to Eurostat within 2 months of their end.
In addition, EU countries are required to provide the EMO with:
a list of the categories of persons covered by the regulation;
a description of the types of information to be supplied;
an indication of the sources of the information;
statistical information on the types and methods used to compile the statistics.
Reporting
The EMO must report to the Commission on the quality, comparability and completeness of the statistics it receives, and on any changes in the methods and data sources used. It must also report on the information it receives from the Member States.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Migration statistics’ on Eurostat’s website.
KEY TERMS
* International migration: the movement of people, goods and services by land, sea or air between countries.
* Non-EU national: a person who is not a national of an EU country but who is legally resident in another EU country and has the right to live and work legally in that country.

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2011 — statutory audits of statutory auditors and audit firms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for the appointment of statutory audit* and audit firm* bodies* to carry out statutory audits* of EU countries’ statutory audited entities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
statutory auditors or audit firms appointed by EU countries for the purpose of carrying out audits of EU-wide public-public interest entities;

Appointment

the public interest entity is the subject of the statutory audit;
there is no conflict of interest between the statutory auditor and the audit firm.
Audit fees
The statutory auditor is paid by the audited entity for the work it carries out.
The audit committee is responsible for the selection procedure and the selection process.
Duration of the audit engagement
The duration of the engagement is determined according to the nature and scope of the work.
Public-interest entity
Audited entity
The auditor is appointed for a period of 5 years. The duration of this period is determined on the basis of the information provided by the public-national audit committee.

Summary 153:
Common Fisheries Policy (CFP)
Common Fisheries Programme
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2015/852 — common fisheries policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It sets out the common framework for the conservation and sustainable exploitation of fisheries resources under the Common Fisheries Policy.
KEY POINTS
The CFP is the EU’s fisheries policy. It aims to ensure the conservation of marine biological resources and the sustainable management of fisheries targeting them.
It is based on the principle of relative stability, which means that the sustainable use of the resources of the fisheries covered by the CFP does not change over time.
The aim is to maintain or improve the conservation, sustainable management and management of the marine ecosystems covered by this common framework.
To achieve this, the EU has adopted a number of measures, including:
a multiannual national strategic plan for the period 2014-2020;
fisheries partnership agreements between EU countries;
the establishment of an Advisory Council for Aquaculture and for the Advisory Council on Markets;
agreements with non-EU countries and non-governmental organisations (NGOs);
the creation of a European Fisheries Information System (EFIS).
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of these measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 29 March 2015.
BACKGROUND
The common fisheries programme is the main instrument of the EU fisheries policy, which aims to protect and conserve the marine environment and the marine biodiversity.
For more information, see:
Fisheries (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Common fisheries policy:
common fisheries policy (CPP): the policy of the European Union (EU).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2011/884/EU of 25 and 26 March 2011 on the adoption, on behalf of the Member States, of the common Fisheries Policy and of the Sustainable Fisheries Partnership Agreement (CFPA) (OJ L 183, 29.4.2011, pp. 1-2)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  (1)
  The European Commission adopts the common fisheries partnership agreement (FISA) with the European Economic and Social Committee (EASC) and the European Maritime and Fisheries Committee (EMAC) (EC) on the sustainable fisheries partnership agreements (SFPAs).
Agreement between the European Commission and the EASC:
Sustainable fisheries partnership (SFFAs): the agreement between the Commission, the European Environment Agency (EEA), the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA), the EEA and the World Trade Organization (WTO) on sustainable fisheries management.
Agreements between the EU and the EU countries:
conservation of marine ecosystems;
management of fisheries;
protection of marine biodiversity;
promotion of sustainable fishing opportunities;
effective control, inspection and enforcement of IUU fishing activities.
Safeguards
The EU and its Member States must:
ensure that:
fishing opportunities are allocated according to objective, transparent and objective criteria;
allocating the fishing opportunities available to each Member State are based on fair and transparent criteria, including those of an environmental, social and economic nature;
protecting marine life;
avoidance and minimising unwanted catches;
implementing measures to prevent, reduce and eliminate illegal fishing;
ensuring the protection of marine protected areas;
cooperating with the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and other non-member countries in the field of fishery conservation;
monitoring and reporting on the use of fishing opportunities.
In addition, Member States are required to:
take the necessary measures to ensure compliance with the rules of CFP;
identify and prevent the illegal fishing of certain species;
take appropriate measures to reduce the number of illegal fish caught;
prevent and minimise the impact of illegal fishing on marine ecosystems.
Member States are also required to take the necessary steps to ensure that the catch of unwanted fish is minimised and to prevent the introduction of new species into the fishery.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The agreement entered into force on 1 January 2016 and will expire on 31 December 2020.

Summary 154:
Fishery and aquaculture products — common organisation of markets in the European Union (CMO)
Fisheries and aquaulture products: common organisation and common market organisation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the common organisation, organisation and supervision of the markets in fishery and aquatic products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that fishery products marketed in the EU are marketed in a way that is fair and transparent to consumers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out common rules for the organisation and management of the EU’s markets in fisheries and aquatic products (FMO).
It also sets out the conditions for the recognition of producers’ organisations (i.e. organisations representing fishery producers) and inter-branch organisations.
It establishes a common market information system (CIS) for the marketing of FMO products.
The CIS is based on a set of principles:
fishery products should be marketed according to the best available scientific and technical knowledge;
the products should not be marketed as if they are products of non-European origin;
good manufacturing practice (GMP) should be used in the production and marketing of the products;
marketing standards should be set out in a language easily understood by consumers;
consumer information should be available to the public;
information should be made available to consumers about the products they buy;
competition rules should be in line with EU competition rules.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
From 13 December 2014.
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘The Common Market Information System’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* FMO: the market organisation of products (e.g. the market for fishery, aqua-based products).
* Producer organisation: an organisation or body representing a fishery producer or aquacultural producer.
ACT

Summary 155:
Securities settlement systems (SSS) — rules for credit institutions and credit institutions (Regulation (EU) No 1095/2014)
Securities settlements systems (RSS)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1024/2014 on securities settlement systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the functioning of the securities settlement* systems (CSDs)* in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules for the operation of securities settlement (SSDs)* systems.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all credit institutions, including banks, insurance companies, investment firms, investment banks and other financial institutions.
It does not apply to credit institutions that are not covered by Directive 98/26/EC on the prudential supervision of financial institutions (see summary).
Requirements for CSDs
SSDs must comply with the following requirements:
be based in an EU Member State and must have a minimum number of employees;
be established in a Member State with a minimum age of at least 18 years;
not be based in a non-EU Member State;
operate in a country other than the one in which they have their registered office;
have at least one registered office in the country where they are based;
provide a minimum level of risk management and risk management services to the authorities of the host Member State.
In addition, a CSD must:
ensure that the competent national authorities and the competent authorities in the Member State in which it is based have the necessary to identify and monitor the CSDs’ compliance with the regulation;
take all necessary steps to ensure that the CSD complies with the requirements laid down in the regulation, in particular with regard to:
compliance with the relevant national legislation;
identify, monitor and investigate the risks that CSDs may face;
establish procedures that facilitate the settlement of transactions in financial instruments;
cooperate with the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Central Bank (ECB) in order to ensure the safe operation of their SSDs;
comply with the rules laid down by the European Banking Authority (EBA).
The CSD’s obligations
A CSD is responsible for:
implementing the rules and procedures set out in the Regulation;
ensuring the timely recovery of operations and the fulfilment of the obligations in the event of events that pose a significant risk of disrupting operations.
The competent authorities must ensure that:
the CSD has the necessary resources to meet its obligations;
the relevant national authorities have access to the information and expertise necessary to assess and assess CSDs; and
the competent authority is subject to on-site inspections.
Regulatory technical standards
The European Commission, in close cooperation with the members of the ESCB, is to develop draft regulatory technical standards to specify the reconciliation measures that the SSDs are to take under paragraphs 1, 2 and 3 of the regulation. The Commission will submit those draft standards to the European Parliament and the Council by 18 June 2015.
Amendments
The Regulation has been amended several times. The most recent amendments to the regulation have been incorporated into the basic text. These amendments are as follows:
Amending Regulation (Euratom) 2019/852 introduces a new definition of ‘securities settlement system’ in relation to ‘financial instruments’, and amends Regulation (EC) No 648/2012 on the requirements for the establishment and operation of the SSD. This definition replaces the previous definition of securitisation system, which was replaced by ‘banking-type ancillary services’ as of 1 January 2020. The new definition applies from 1 January 2021.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING ACT APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2014/1024/EU has applied since 16 December 2014 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 16 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
The securities settlement system is one of the most important components of the financial system and is essential for the smooth functioning of financial markets.
For more information, see:
Settlement systems (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* CSD: a credit institution or investment firm.
* Credit institution: an investment firm or other financial institution that provides securities settlement services.
ACT

Summary 156:
Cohesion Fund — funding for transport infrastructure and environment projects in the area of transport infrastructure (Cohesion Regulation)
The Cohesion Fund is the European Union’s (EU) investment fund for the fields of environment and trans-European transport infrastructure.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006 of 11 July 2006 establishing a Cohesion fund and repealing Regulation (Euratom) No 1164/94 (OJ L 210, 31.7.2006)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The regulation establishes the tasks of the European Commission and the scope of its support for the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The ERDF is the main instrument of the EU's investment for growth and jobs goal. It provides financial support to the European Communities (now the EU countries) in the areas of transport, energy, transport and sustainable urban development.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The objectives of the ERDF are to:
promote the sustainable development of the environment and transport infrastructure in the EU;
contribute to the creation of an integrated and sustainable transport network in Europe;
support the development of a high-quality, safe and environmentally sound transport system in Europe.
In order to achieve these objectives, the EU is committed to investing at least €2.5 billion in the following areas:
environmental and sustainable development;
trans-European infrastructure;
energy;
sustainable urban development; and
transport.
The cohesion fund supports projects in these fields in order to contribute to achieving the objectives set out in the investment objectives.
Cohesion fund:
provides financial support for projects of common interest, i.e. projects that contribute to the achievement of the common objectives, such as:
the creation of a sustainable urban environment;
the improvement of the quality of life and health of the urban population;
improvement of the competitiveness of the labour market;
improving the environmental performance of the transport sector;
encouraging the use of renewable energy;
enhancing the sustainable use of energy resources;
promoting the sustainable urbanisation of the area;
fostering sustainable urban renewal;
providing for the sustainable management of land, water, energy and transport resources.
Scope of support
The EDF is responsible for providing financial support, in particular for:
investment priorities;
programme-specific results indicators and, where relevant, programme-specific output indicators;
cohesion fund investment priorities.
Specific provisions concerning the type of activities which can be supported by the EDF are laid down in Annex I to this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation (EC, Euratom) 2018/856 has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission is the implementing body for the Cohesion Regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Cohesion Funds’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Environmental impact assessment: an assessment of the impact of a project on the environment, on human health, on the economy and on the social and economic well-being of its beneficiaries, and on other aspects of the social, economic and cultural life of the beneficiaries.

Summary 157:
The European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the European Cohesion Fund (Cohesion Fund) (2014-2020)
The European regional development fund (Euratom)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013/EC — the structure and operation of the European Regional development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the structure, rules and procedures for the EU’s financial instrument for the development of regions and the co-financing of projects in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The ERDF is the main instrument of the EU's common investment policy (CEP).
It aims to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
It is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Social Fund (ESF) and by the Cohesion fund (CSF).
The main objective of the ERDF and the ESI is to:
reduce disparities between regions;
contribute to the sustainable development of all the EU countries;
improve the quality of life of the most disadvantaged regions; and
promote the sustainable urbanisation of all EU countries.
The ESI and CSF contribute to the creation of jobs, growth and employment opportunities in the EU.
the ERDF contributes to the achievement of sustainable urban development and to the growth and diversification of the labour market.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The regulation is based on Article 174 TFEU (the Treaty on European Union's Economic and Social Rights and Freedoms).
For more information, see:
‘The structure and the operation’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Regional development fund: the European Development Fund, the European Cooperation Fund, or the European Fund for Rural Development (EURDP).
* Cohesion funds: the Fund for Regional Development is a joint EU-European Union investment instrument. It provides financial support to EU countries in the form of grants, loans, grants-in-kind and grants-as-a-service. It is not a grant-for-grants instrument.

Summary 159:
Trans-European energy networks (TEN-E) — guidelines for trans-EU energy infrastructures (2014-2020)
Trans-Europa energy networks: guidelines on how to build trans-European infrastructure (2014 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 on guidelines for the construction of trans-Europaea energy networks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down guidelines for how to establish a European Union (EU) list of energy infrastructure projects of common interest.
It aims to improve the transparency and comparability of the EU’s energy infrastructure and to ensure that projects are prioritised.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out guidelines on the construction and evaluation of projects of ‘common interest’ (see Annex I to the regulation).
The list is updated every 2 years.
Projects are divided into 3 categories:
energy infrastructure: projects which have a significant impact on the energy system, such as energy transmission and distribution networks, energy storage and distribution grids, and energy distribution networks;
trans-border energy network: projects that have a major impact on energy security and security of supply and demand (e.g. cross-border electricity transmission, distribution and interconnecting energy networks);
cross-border carbon dioxide network: development of carbon dioxide transport infrastructure between EU countries and with neighbouring countries in view of the deployment of carbon capture and storage (CCSPs);
energy grid infrastructure: infrastructure;
energy system wide cost-benefit analysis: assessing the benefits of a project compared to a lower-risk alternative.
The European Commission is responsible for the selection of projects on the EU list.
EU list
Each year, the Commission draws up a list of projects which are of interest to the Commission and the Member States. The list is published in the Official Journal of the European Union. The Commission publishes the list in Annexes I and II of the regulation.
Member States are responsible for selecting projects from the list and for ensuring that they are approved by the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EASC). The Commission is also responsible for monitoring the implementation of the projects and ensuring that the public benefits are taken into account in the decision-making process.
Each project is assessed by the EASC on the basis of the following criteria:
cost-benefit;
benefit of the project compared with a lower risk alternative;
environmental impact;
impact on the environment;
social and economic impact.
If the Commission considers that a project does not meet all of these criteria, it may refuse to grant a permit or authorise a project, unless it is in the overriding public interest of the Member State concerned. If the project is not in the public interest, the project must be approved within the time limit set out in Article 10(1) of this regulation. If a project is considered to be a public interest project, the decision must be taken within the period set by Article 11(2) of the Regulation.
In addition, Member States must ensure that:
the project is implemented in line with the principles of subsidiarity;
public consultation is carried out;
the public consultation process is open to all interested parties;
a public participation process is in place;
information is made available to the public on the European Commission's website and on the project's website;
all information is kept confidential, except for any commercially sensitive information;
EU funds are allocated and disbursed by the Union for each project on the Union list;
The Commission reserves the right to make proposals in this direction based on the experience gained from the monitoring of implementation of projects.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
 
Energy infrastructure is one of the priorities of the Europe 2020 strategy, which aims to create a more resource-efficient, more sustainable and more competitive economy.
For more information, see:
Transition to a more energy-efficient Europe (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Energy infrastructure: an infrastructure project is defined as a project which has a significant environmental impact and which has the potential to reduce or eliminate energy consumption.
* Transmission and distribution network: a network which provides electricity, gas or other energy-related services in the form of a transmission or distribution system.
ACT
 			
  The Regulation of the General Assembly and of the Council of 11 December 2014 on guidelines on guidelines relating to the building of trans‑European energy infostructures and repealing Decision No 1364/2006/EC (OJ L 347, 12.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 529/2014 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
